PART 1 — PostgreSQL for a 5-year-old 🧒

Track A · PostgreSQL foundations · 🟢 Beginner→🟡 Intermediate · ▶ next: Part 2

1. What is PostgreSQL?

Imagine a giant magic library where you keep ALL your drawings, toys, and secrets in very tidy little boxes. When you want one, you ask the librarian and they bring it to you instantly.

  • The drawings and toys = your data.
  • The library = the database.
  • The librarian = PostgreSQL.

It is a super-smart librarian that stores, finds, and looks after your things without losing any. It has been working for over 35 years, it is free, and nobody owns it (lots of people from all over the world look after it).

👦 Technical explanation. PostgreSQL is an open-source relational database management system (RDBMS): software that stores data as tables (rows and columns), lets many people read and write it at the same time without corrupting it, and guarantees your data survives crashes. "Relational" means data lives in separate tables you can join on shared keys. You talk to it in SQL, it's ACID-compliant (§8), ~35 years old, community-governed (no single vendor owns it), and highly extensible (§9). It's the dependable engine behind web apps, analytics, and — increasingly — AI systems.

2. How do you talk to it? → SQL

  • SELECT = "Bring me my dinosaur drawing." 🦕
  • INSERT = "Save this new drawing."
  • UPDATE = "Change the dinosaur's color to green."
  • DELETE = "Throw this drawing in the trash."
👦 Technical explanation. SQL (Structured Query Language) is a declarative language: you describe what result you want, not how to compute it — the planner (§7) decides the "how". The four everyday verbs are the CRUD operations: SELECT (read), INSERT (create), UPDATE, DELETE. Beyond those, DDL (CREATE/ALTER/DROP) defines the shape of your tables, while DML manipulates the rows inside them. Because it's declarative, the same query can run many different ways under the hood — which is exactly why the optimizer matters.

3. The architecture (the team of helpers)

  • The head doorkeeper (postmaster): assigns each new child their own personal helper. One connection = one helper (backend).
  • The shared central table (shared buffers): where everyone puts the drawings that are being used right now, so they don't have to go down to the basement every time.
  • The dark basement (the disk): keeps ALL the little boxes forever, even with the lights off.
👦 Technical explanation. PostgreSQL uses a process-per-connection model. A supervisor process, the postmaster, listens on a TCP port (default 5432) and fork()s a dedicated backend process for each client connection. All those backends coordinate through a region of shared memory — most importantly shared_buffers, an in-RAM cache of 8 KB disk pages, so hot data is served without touching disk. Durable data ultimately lives in files on disk. (Contrast with thread-per-connection engines like MySQL or SQL Server; the process model is more isolated but makes each connection relatively heavy — hence connection poolers.)

4. The magic diary (WAL)

Before changing any drawing, the librarian first writes it down in a diary that never gets erased: "I'm going to paint the dinosaur green." Only AFTERWARD does it make the change. If the power goes out 💥, when it comes back it reads its diary and finishes. That way nothing is ever lost. That diary is the WAL.

👦 Technical explanation. Write-Ahead Logging is the rule "log the intention before changing the data." Before a modified page is written to its data file, PostgreSQL first appends a compact record to a sequential log (the WAL) and, at COMMIT, fsyncs that log to disk; data pages are flushed lazily later. On crash, restart replays (REDOes) the WAL from the last checkpoint to reconstruct every committed change — so a confirmed commit is never lost. This is the D (Durability) of ACID and the basis of replication and point-in-time recovery. Appending sequentially is also far cheaper than random data-page writes.

5. Everyone playing at once (MVCC) + cleanup (VACUUM)

When your friend goes to paint, they don't erase the old drawing: they make a new copy. You keep seeing yours. Nobody waits. This is MVCC: reading doesn't block writing and writing doesn't block reading.

Since old drawings get left lying around, a man with a broom (VACUUM / autovacuum) picks them up and frees space.

👦 Technical explanation. MVCC (Multi-Version Concurrency Control): instead of locking a row so readers wait, an UPDATE keeps the old version and writes a new one (a DELETE just marks the old version dead — it writes no new tuple); each transaction sees a consistent snapshot as of when it started, so readers never block writers and vice-versa — this is the I (Isolation) of ACID via snapshots, not locks. The trade-off is obsolete row versions ("dead tuples"), which VACUUM reclaims while also refreshing planner statistics and freezing old transaction IDs to prevent 32-bit XID wraparound (the transaction counter is 32-bit and must never "lap" itself, or old data could look like it's from the future); autovacuum automates this, and un-reclaimed dead tuples are bloat (tuning it is a real ops skill — Part 2 §3).

6. Indexes

It has an ordered card catalog: "Dinosaur → shelf 5, drawer 3". These are the indexes (B-tree, GIN, GiST, BRIN…). They make searching fly. 🚀

👦 Technical explanation. An index is an auxiliary data structure that finds matching rows without scanning the whole table — you trade extra write cost and storage for faster reads. The default is a B-tree (equality, ranges, ORDER BY); others fit other jobs: GIN (full-text, JSONB, arrays), GiST (geometric/fuzzy), BRIN (huge naturally-ordered tables), Hash (equality). An index is only a possibility — the planner (§7) uses statistics to decide whether an Index Scan beats a Sequential Scan for your query. Over-indexing slows writes; under-indexing slows reads.

7. The planner

You ask the librarian "bring me every book about green dinosaurs". Before taking a step, they think about the best route: go straight to the sorted card catalog (the index) and jump to the exact shelf? Or — since almost every book is about dinosaurs — walk aisle by aisle? And if you ask them to cross two catalogs ("dinosaurs that also appear in movies"), do they pair up one by one, build a lookup table, or sort both lists and cross them in one pass?

They don't guess: they keep a little notebook of statistics ("aisle 5 ≈ 3 dinosaur books; aisle 2 ≈ 40,000 history books") and use it to estimate the effort of each route and pick the cheapest. That notebook is kept current by the ANALYZE helper. This is the query planner / optimizer; the chosen route is what you spy on with EXPLAIN.

👦 Technical explanation. The query planner/optimizer is cost-based: for one SQL query there are many valid plans — which index, which join algorithm (nested loop / hash / merge), what join order — and it estimates each one's cost from table statistics (row counts, value distributions) gathered by ANALYZE, then runs the cheapest. Inspect its choice with EXPLAIN (estimated) or EXPLAIN ANALYZE (estimate vs. reality). The #1 cause of sudden slowness is stale statistics leading to a bad estimate.

7.1 🔬 The bridge: what the planner does inside (with the real code)

The librarian's notebook is the analogy; now let's open the real machinery. The planner isn't magic: it's a procedure with well-defined stages in the PostgreSQL source. Your SQL first passes through the parser and the analyzer/rewriter (the full four-stage flow is in Part 2 §6.6); this is a zoom inside the "PLANNER" box:

A zoom inside the PLANNER box from Part 2 §6.6. The orange step is the heart: costing each path with ANALYZE's statistics. The winning plan tree is exactly what EXPLAIN prints.

  1. Entry — standard_planner() (src/backend/optimizer/plan/planner.c). Takes the validated query tree and returns a plan tree.
  2. Preprocessing — subquery_planner(). Flattens subqueries, inlines functions, and simplifies constant expressions (2+2 → 4 once, not per row).
  3. Access paths per table. Generates the possible paths (Seq Scan, Index Scan, Bitmap Heap Scan…). This is where an index stops being a possibility (§6) and competes.
  4. Join ordering. Dynamic programming (standard_join_search) for few tables; a genetic algorithm (GEQO) once there are more than geqo_threshold (12 by default).
  5. Costing — the heart (src/backend/optimizer/path/costsize.c). Cost = parameters (seq_page_cost, random_page_cost, cpu_tuple_cost) × estimated cardinality, taken from pg_class.reltuples and pg_statistic — what ANALYZE keeps fresh.
  6. Cheapest wins → create_plan(). Becomes a plan tree handed to the executor (ExecutorRun). That tree is what EXPLAIN shows.

That closes the loop with §6 (indexes) and §5 (MVCC/VACUUM): ANALYZE (which autovacuum fires alongside VACUUM) fills the notebook, the planner reads it to cost paths, and that's why stale statistics → bad estimate → bad plan → slow query. Part 2 §2 teaches you to read the plan with EXPLAIN.

Accuracy note: the symbols (standard_planner, subquery_planner, query_planner/make_one_rel, standard_join_search, create_plan, cost_* in costsize.c) are standard PostgreSQL internals, included so you can locate them in the source (src/backend/optimizer). Exact names and fine details can vary by version; cross-check against the code and the docs.

7.2 🧪 A hands-on example: watching the planner change its mind

The best way to get the planner is to watch it choose differently for the same table depending on how many rows it thinks it will touch. We build a table with 1 million rows and 1000 users (≈ 1000 rows per user) and let ANALYZE fill the statistics notebook (pg_statistic):

CREATE TABLE events AS
SELECT g AS id,
       (g % 1000)      AS user_id,     -- 1000 users, ~1000 rows each
       repeat('x', 50) AS payload
FROM generate_series(1, 1000000) g;    -- 1 million rows

CREATE INDEX idx_events_user ON events(user_id);
ANALYZE events;   -- fills pg_statistic: n_distinct≈1000, histogram, etc.

Case A — selective query (few rows) → Index Scan wins. You ask for a single user: the planner estimates ~1000 out of 1,000,000 (0.1%), so a few random jumps into the index are cheaper than reading the whole table:

EXPLAIN ANALYZE SELECT * FROM events WHERE user_id = 42;
--  Index Scan using idx_events_user on events
--    (cost=0.42..3100 rows=1000 width=58) (actual ... rows=1000 loops=1)
--    Index Cond: (user_id = 42)             ← used the index

Case B — non-selective query (many rows) → Seq Scan wins. Now you ask for 90% of the table. The index would force ~900,000 random jumps into the heap; reading everything sequentially (cheap seq_page_cost) costs less, so the planner deliberately drops the index:

EXPLAIN ANALYZE SELECT * FROM events WHERE user_id < 900;
--  Seq Scan on events
--    (cost=0.00..21925 rows=900000 width=58) (actual ... rows=900000 loops=1)
--    Filter: (user_id < 900)                ← ignored the index on purpose

Same index, same table — the decision is dictated by the estimated cardinality, not a fixed rule. That's the costing of §7.1 live: cost_index vs cost_seqscan, cheapest wins.

The stale-statistics trap (the #1 performance bug). We insert 500,000 new rows for user 42 without running ANALYZE. The notebook still says "≈1000 rows", so the planner picks Index Scan… but there are now 501,000 rows: estimate and reality diverge — the tell is estimated rows vs actual rows:

INSERT INTO events
SELECT 1000000 + g, 42, repeat('x', 50) FROM generate_series(1, 500000) g;

EXPLAIN ANALYZE SELECT * FROM events WHERE user_id = 42;
--  Index Scan ...  (cost=... rows=1000 ...) (actual ... rows=501000 loops=1)
--                              ▲ estimated 1000       ▲ actual 501000   ← 500× off!

ANALYZE events;   -- refresh pg_statistic

EXPLAIN ANALYZE SELECT * FROM events WHERE user_id = 42;
--  Seq Scan ...    (cost=... rows=501000 ...) (actual ... rows=501000 loops=1)
--  Now the estimate is right → the planner switches to Seq Scan (the correct plan)

That estimated rows ≠ actual rows is exactly the "problem signal" from Part 2 §2: it closes the §7.1 loop — ANALYZE keeps the notebook fresh, the planner reads it to cost paths, and stale statistics → bad estimate → bad plan → slow query.

8. ACID

A group of actions happens all or nothing (a transaction): Atomic, Consistent, Isolated (MVCC), Durable (WAL).

👦 Technical explanation. ACID names the four guarantees a transaction (BEGIN … COMMIT/ROLLBACK) gives — what makes a DB trustworthy for money/inventory/bookings. Atomicity: all-or-nothing (WAL + rollback). Consistency: every commit obeys all constraints. Isolation: concurrent transactions don't corrupt each other's view — tunable via isolation levels (Read Committed → Serializable), implemented with MVCC snapshots. Durability: once COMMIT returns, data survives a crash (the fsynced WAL). §4 (WAL) and §5 (MVCC) are literally the machinery behind the "D" and the "I".

9. Extensions (like LEGO)

You add pieces: PostGIS for maps 🗺️, your own types and functions… without breaking it.

👦 Technical explanation. PostgreSQL exposes extension and hook APIs so you can add new data types, functions, operators, index methods, and background workers without forking the core — packaged as extensions installed with CREATE EXTENSION. This is why it keeps conquering new domains: PostGIS (geospatial), pgvector (vector similarity search for AI/embeddings), pg_stat_statements (query telemetry), pg_cron (scheduling), and hundreds more. Extensibility is a big reason a 35-year-old database is first-class for modern AI workloads instead of being replaced by them.

10. 🔬 One level deeper: the real machinery behind postmaster, shared_buffers, WAL, MVCC and VACUUM

The nine sections above are the intuition. This section is the bridge to the engineering: it opens up the five load-bearing mechanisms down to their data structures, on-disk layout and algorithms — the "what is actually in memory / on disk" level. It is deliberately dense; Parts 2 and 3 then reuse these pieces in context. Everything here is standard PostgreSQL core (verify against the source and the docs).

10.1 🎩 The Postmaster — a signal-driven state machine

The postmaster is a single process that never runs SQL and, after boot, deliberately avoids touching shared memory so it can stay alive as the "reaper" even if a backend corrupts that memory. Boot order matters — it creates shared state before forking so children inherit it:

  1. Read postgresql.conf / pg_hba.conf / pg_ident.conf.
  2. Write the data-dir lock file / initial postmaster.pid (early), and start the syslogger if enabled.
  3. Open listen sockets (TCP + Unix), then create one contiguous shared-memory segment + semaphores (CreateSharedMemoryAndSemaphores) — sockets are actually opened before shared memory in the real code.
  4. Fork the startup process (crash REDO — see §10.3).
  5. Launch the auxiliary processes (checkpointer, bgwriter, walwriter, autovacuum launcher, archiver…) — not all at once: each comes up as the state machine reaches its phase (bgwriter/checkpointer during/after recovery, autovacuum launcher and archiver at PM_RUN).
  6. Enter ServerLoop.

In steady state it is a state machine (PM_STARTUP → PM_RECOVERY → PM_HOT_STANDBY → PM_RUN → PM_STOP…) that blocks in select()/poll() on the listen sockets plus a self-pipe/latch, and reacts to events:

  • New connection: accept() → BackendStartup → fork(). ⚠️ The child, not the postmaster, reads the startup packet and authenticates. One connection = one OS process (fork_process + InitPostmasterChild).
  • A child dies (SIGCHLD): the reaper() runs waitpid() in a loop and calls CleanupBackend.
  • A backend crashes abnormally: it assumes shared memory may be corrupt → HandleChildCrash sends SIGQUIT to everyone, waits, and reinitializes the whole instance (governed by restart_after_crash). That is why one segfault briefly tears down the instance — integrity over uptime.
  • Postmaster death detection: children watch a pipe (postmaster_alive_fds); PostmasterIsAlive() returns false the instant the pipe's write end closes, so orphans exit fast instead of writing into dead shared memory.
  [boot] ──create shm+sems, open sockets──▶ PM_STARTUP
     │ launch startup process (REDO)
     ▼
  PM_RECOVERY ──recovery consistent──▶ PM_RUN ◄─────────────┐
                                         │  accept() → fork() backend
                                         │  SIGCHLD → reaper() → CleanupBackend
                                         ├──SIGTERM/SIGINT──▶ PM_WAIT_BACKENDS ──▶ [exit]
                                         └──a child CRASHES──▶ SIGQUIT all
                                                              → reinit (restart_after_crash) → PM_STARTUP
Signal to postmasterEffect
SIGHUPReload config, propagate to children.
SIGTERMSmart shutdown (let sessions finish).
SIGINTFast shutdown (abort sessions, checkpoint, close).
SIGQUITImmediate shutdown (no checkpoint; used internally after a crash).

10.2 🧠 shared_buffers — the buffer manager, descriptor by descriptor

shared_buffers is an array of 8 KB frames plus a parallel array of BufferDesc descriptors and a partitioned hash table that maps "which page" → "which frame". It is fixed at startup (resizing needs a restart). PostgreSQL still sits on top of the OS page cache, so a cold read can be double-buffered.

  • Buffer tag = { relfilenode, forknum, blocknum } — the unique identity of a page. The SharedBufHash maps a tag to a buffer id, protected by 128 BufMappingLock partitions so lookups on different pages don't contend.
  • BufferDesc holds an atomic state word packing refcount, usage_count (0–5) and flags (BM_DIRTY, BM_VALID, BM_IO_IN_PROGRESS, BM_JUST_DIRTIED…), plus a content LWLock and an io_in_progress mechanism (only one process reads a given page in).
  • Pin (refcount) = "in use, do not evict". usage_count = recency for the replacement policy.
  • Clock-sweep eviction (StrategyGetBuffer): a hand (nextVictimBuffer) sweeps the descriptors; on each unpinned buffer it decrements usage_count; the first buffer that reaches 0 is the victim. If it's dirty it is written (FlushBuffer → smgrwrite) before reuse.
  • Access strategies (ring buffers): big sequential jobs use a small ring so they don't evict the whole cache — BAS_BULKREAD/BAS_BULKWRITE and BAS_VACUUM (256 KB). This is why a VACUUM or a huge SELECT doesn't blow away everyone's hot pages.
  ReadBuffer(rel, blocknum)
        │  build BufferTag {relfilenode, forknum, blocknum}
        ▼
  SharedBufHash lookup  (under a BufMappingLock partition)
        ├── HIT  → pin (refcount++), bump usage_count → return frame
        └── MISS → StrategyGetBuffer: clock-sweep for a victim (or strategy ring)
                        │  victim BM_DIRTY? ──yes──▶ FlushBuffer → smgrwrite (WAL already durable)
                        ▼
                   read 8KB disk → frame, insert tag, BM_VALID, pin → return frame

How the pieces connect. The diagram above is the flow of a read; this one is the anatomy — the three structures that live in shared memory and how they point at one another. The key idea is that they are three parallel arrays indexed by the same buf_id: the hash table translates a tag into a buf_id, and that same index gives you both the descriptor (BufferDesc[buf_id]) and the data frame (BufferBlocks[buf_id]).

  ┌──────────────────────── shared memory (fixed at startup) ────────────────────────┐
  │                                                                                   │
  │   SharedBufHash                 BufferDescriptors[]              BufferBlocks[]    │
  │   (partitioned hash)            (1 per frame)                    (the 8 KB frames) │
  │  ┌───────────────────┐         ┌────────────────────────┐      ┌────────────────┐ │
  │  │ tag {relfilenode, │         │ state (atomic):        │      │ frame 0: 8 KB  │ │
  │  │   forknum,blocknum}│─buf_id─▶│  refcount·usage(0–5)   │      │ frame 1: 8 KB  │ │
  │  │        → buf_id    │         │  BM_DIRTY·BM_VALID·IO  │─────▶│ frame 2: 8 KB  │ │
  │  │ 128 partitions,    │         │ content LWLock         │ same │   …            │ │
  │  │ 1 BufMappingLock ea│         │ buffer tag · freeNext  │buf_id│ N = SB / 8 KB  │ │
  │  └───────────────────┘         └────────────────────────┘      └───────┬────────┘ │
  │                                        ▲                                │          │
  │            replacement strategy ───────┘  picks victim                  │          │
  │            (nextVictimBuffer clock-sweep · freelist · BAS_* rings)      │          │
  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┼──────────┘
                                                     smgrread / smgrwrite (8 KB)
                                                                             ▼
                                              Disk / OS page cache (relfilenode.forknum)

Read it like this: one frame is the union of three things indexed by the same buf_id — its hash entry (how you find it), its BufferDesc (its state: pin, recency, dirty/clean, locks) and its 8 KB block in BufferBlocks[] (the actual data). The replacement strategy only touches the descriptors to pick a victim; I/O against disk (or the OS page cache) only moves the data block.

Practical example — watching it happen (miss → hit and which frames a table occupies). With the pg_buffercache extension you can look inside shared_buffers, and with EXPLAIN (ANALYZE, BUFFERS) you see the jump from a disk read to a cache hit:

CREATE EXTENSION IF NOT EXISTS pg_buffercache;

CREATE TABLE demo AS SELECT g AS id, repeat('x', 100) AS payload
FROM generate_series(1, 50000) g;   -- ~7 MB → dozens of 8 KB pages

-- Start cold (nothing touched yet), or restart the instance to clear the cache.

-- 1) COLD READ: the page is not in shared_buffers → 'read' (miss)
EXPLAIN (ANALYZE, BUFFERS, COSTS OFF)
SELECT count(*) FROM demo;
--  Buffers: shared read=834        ← 834 blocks pulled from disk/OS into the pool

-- 2) SAME QUERY again: the pages are now cached → 'hit'
EXPLAIN (ANALYZE, BUFFERS, COSTS OFF)
SELECT count(*) FROM demo;
--  Buffers: shared hit=834         ← 0 disk reads: all served from RAM

-- 3) Which frames does 'demo' occupy, and how "hot" are they (usage_count)?
SELECT c.relname,
       count(*)                        AS frames,
       pg_size_pretty(count(*) * 8192) AS cached,
       round(avg(b.usagecount), 1)     AS avg_usage
FROM   pg_buffercache b
JOIN   pg_class c ON c.relfilenode = b.relfilenode
WHERE  c.relname = 'demo'
GROUP  BY c.relname;
--  relname | frames | cached  | avg_usage
--  demo    |    834 | 6672 kB |       1.0

What you just observed maps 1:1 onto the anatomy: the shared read=834 in step 1 is 834 misses that walked the MISS → StrategyGetBuffer → read 8KB → insert tag branch of the flow diagram; the shared hit=834 in step 2 is 834 hits that left via the HIT → pin → return frame branch; and step 3 lists the 834 buf_ids whose BufferDesc points at demo's relfilenode, with their usage_count — the recency the clock-sweep hand will decrement when it next needs victims.

10.3 🪵 WAL — record anatomy, parallel insertion and group commit

The physical rule: no data page reaches disk before its WAL record is durable. Under the hood:

  • XLogRecord header per record: xl_tot_len, xl_xid, xl_prev (LSN of the previous record — the backward chain), xl_info, xl_rmid (which resource manager: heap, btree, xact, gin…), xl_crc. Then come block references (XLogRecordBlockHeader) naming the buffers touched, and the payload.
  • Building a record: XLogBeginInsert → XLogRegisterData / XLogRegisterBuffer → XLogInsert. The redo function of the record's rmgr replays it (rmgr->rm_redo).
  • Parallel insertion: to let many backends append at once there are NUM_XLOGINSERT_LOCKS (8) WAL-insert locks. A backend reserves its byte range with an atomic bump of Insert->CurrBytePos (ReserveXLogInsertLocation), copies its bytes into the WAL buffers (circular buffer), then releases the lock. LSN order is fixed cheaply and copying happens concurrently.
  • Durability & group commit: at COMMIT the backend calls XLogFlush(commitLSN). Under WALWriteLock, XLogWrite writes+fsyncs the WAL up to (at least) that LSN. Because many committers ask to flush around the same time, one fsync satisfies all of them whose LSN ≤ the flushed point — that's group commit.
  • pg_control stores the last checkpoint's location and the REDO point; on restart the startup process replays WAL forward from there (each record idempotent w.r.t. the page's pd_lsn).
  • Full Page Writes: the first change to a page after a checkpoint logs the whole 8 KB image to survive torn pages; wal_compression (pglz/lz4/zstd) shrinks those images.
  One WAL record:
    ┌ XLogRecord header: xl_tot_len · xl_xid · xl_prev(LSN) · xl_info · xl_rmid · xl_crc
    ├ block refs (XLogRecordBlockHeader)
    └ payload (change data + optional full-page image)
         │
         ▼
    XLogInsert: reserve byte range (atomic CurrBytePos) under 1 of 8 WAL-insert locks
         │
         ▼
    WAL buffers (circular) ──COMMIT──▶ XLogFlush(commitLSN): WALWriteLock · XLogWrite + fsync
         │
         ▼
    ✔ durable — one fsync covers every committer with LSN ≤ flushed point  = GROUP COMMIT

How the components connect. The diagram above follows one record; this one is the whole WAL subsystem — who produces WAL, where it lives in memory, how it lands on disk, and who consumes it afterward. The piece that ties it all together is the LSN (Log Sequence Number): an ever-increasing byte offset that acts as the database's "clock", and every page carries the LSN of its last change (pd_lsn).

  PRODUCERS                MEMORY (shared)               DISK — pg_wal/          CONSUMERS
  ─────────                ───────────────               ──────────────          ─────────
  backend 1 ┐              ┌─────────────────┐           ┌──────────────────┐    ┌───────────────┐
  backend 2 ┼─XLogInsert──▶│ 8 WAL insert    │           │ 16 MB segments   │──▶ │ checkpointer  │
  backend N ┘              │ locks: reserve  │           │ 0000…01, 0000…02 │    │ (recycle segs,│
                           │ byte range      │           │ written sequen-  │    │  REDO point)  │
                           │ (atomic         │           │ tially, recycled │    ├───────────────┤
                           │  CurrBytePos    │           └────────┬─────────┘──▶ │ archiver → PITR│
                           │  → fixes LSN)   │                    │              ├───────────────┤
                           └───────┬─────────┘           ┌────────▼─────────┐──▶ │ walsender     │
                                   │                     │ pg_control       │    │ → replica     │
                           ┌───────▼─────────┐           │ last checkpoint  │    ├───────────────┤
                           │ WAL buffers     │           │ + REDO point     │──▶ │ startup proc  │
                           │ (circular,      │           └──────────────────┘    │ REDO on crash │
                           │  wal_buffers)   │                                    └───────────────┘
                           └───────┬─────────┘
                                   │  COMMIT: XLogFlush(LSN) · WALWriteLock · fsync
                                   └──────────────────────▶ (bytes land in the 16 MB segments above)

Read it left to right: many backends produce records at once → the insert locks hand each one a byte range (that fixes LSN order cheaply) → they copy into the WAL buffers in RAM → at COMMIT, XLogFlush + fsync push them down into the 16 MB segments in pg_wal/ (the point at which the commit is durable) → and from there the WAL feeds its consumers: the checkpointer (recycles old segments), the archiver (PITR), the walsender (replicas), and, after a crash, the startup process (REDO).

Practical example — watching the WAL advance live. The LSN is observable through built-in functions; you can measure how much WAL an operation generates and watch the clock move:

-- 1) Where is the WAL "head" right now? Which segment does it fall in?
SELECT pg_current_wal_lsn()                   AS current_lsn,
       pg_walfile_name(pg_current_wal_lsn())  AS segment_file;
--  current_lsn |          segment_file
--  0/1A2B3C40  | 000000010000000000000001

-- 2) Measure how much WAL an INSERT generates: capture the LSN before and after.
SELECT pg_current_wal_lsn() AS before \gset
CREATE TABLE accounts (id int, balance numeric);
INSERT INTO accounts SELECT g, g*10 FROM generate_series(1, 100000) g;

SELECT pg_size_pretty(
         pg_wal_lsn_diff(pg_current_wal_lsn(), :'before')) AS wal_generated;
--  wal_generated
--  5544 kB          ← the INSERT left ~5.5 MB of WAL records in pg_wal/

-- 3) The "D" in ACID in one line: the flag that decides whether COMMIT fsyncs.
SHOW synchronous_commit;   --  on  → every COMMIT waits for the WAL fsync (durable)

And to open a real WAL record (seeing the xl_rmid, the size, the full-page image from §10.3 as data, not theory), the pg_walinspect extension (PG15+) decodes them:

CREATE EXTENSION IF NOT EXISTS pg_walinspect;

-- WAL records between two LSNs, one per row:
SELECT start_lsn, resource_manager, record_type,
       record_length, block_ref
FROM   pg_get_wal_records_info(:'before', pg_current_wal_lsn())
LIMIT 5;
--  start_lsn  | resource_manager | record_type | record_length | block_ref
--  0/1A2B3C40 | Heap             | INSERT       |            59 | blkref #0: rel 1663/5/16401 blk 0
--  0/1A2B3C7C | Heap             | INSERT       |            59 | blkref #0: rel 1663/5/16401 blk 0
--  …          | Btree/Transaction/… (the xl_rmid from the XLogRecord header, already decoded)

Those rows are exactly the XLogRecord header from the diagram, already decoded: resource_manager is the xl_rmid, record_length is xl_tot_len, and block_ref is the block references (XLogRecordBlockHeader). The wal_generated from step 2 is the sum of all those record_lengths — the WAL that the COMMIT's fsync made durable before the dirty page ever went to disk.

10.4 🔀 MVCC — the tuple header, hint bits, snapshots and HOT

MVCC is implemented inside every row. A heap tuple carries HeapTupleHeaderData:

  • t_xmin (xid that created it), t_xmax (xid that deleted/updated it, or a multixact id when several sessions lock it — flag HEAP_XMAX_IS_MULTI, backed by pg_multixact).
  • t_cid (command id — intra-transaction visibility, cmin/cmax), t_ctid (pointer to the next version, or to itself), and the bitmasks t_infomask / t_infomask2.
  • Hint bits in t_infomask (HEAP_XMIN_COMMITTED, HEAP_XMIN_INVALID, HEAP_XMAX_COMMITTED…) cache the commit fate of xmin/xmax so most visibility checks skip the CLOG (pg_xact SLRU) lookup. The first reader that consults CLOG sets the hint bit (which is why a plain SELECT can dirty pages).

Visibility (HeapTupleSatisfiesMVCC) compares the tuple's xmin/xmax against a snapshot (SnapshotData: xmin, xmax, and xip[] the array of in-progress xids). A tuple is visible if its creator committed before the snapshot and its deleter has not committed as of the snapshot. Isolation level decides when you take the snapshot: READ COMMITTED = one per statement; REPEATABLE READ / SERIALIZABLE = one per transaction.

The decision tree behind every row read: commit fate of xmin, then snapshot visibility of xmin, then the same for xmax.

Isolation levels (PostgreSQL has no dirty reads at any level — the snapshot forbids them):

LevelSnapshotPreventsStill allowsMechanism
Read Committed (default)per statementdirty readsnon-repeatable reads, phantomsfresh snapshot each command
Repeatable Readper transaction+ non-repeatable reads, phantomswrite skew (serialization anomalies)one stable snapshot; 40001 on write conflict
Serializableper transaction + SSI+ all serialization anomalies (write skew)—SSI tracks read/write deps via SIReadLock predicate locks; aborts a dangerous rw-cycle with 40001
🎓 This is why "predicate locks" exist (Part 3 §3): Serializable uses Serializable Snapshot Isolation (SSI) — same MVCC snapshots as Repeatable Read, but it also records read-sets as SIReadLocks and, at commit, detects the dangerous read-write dependency cycles that would break serializability, aborting one transaction (serialization_failure, SQLSTATE 40001). Retrying is the application's job.

HOT (Heap-Only Tuple) is the key optimization: if an UPDATE changes no indexed column and the new version fits in the same page, PostgreSQL writes the new tuple in that page and chains it via t_ctid without adding an index entry — indexes keep pointing at the head of the chain. Later, page pruning collapses the dead links (LP_REDIRECT) and frees space cheaply — no full VACUUM needed for that page.

  Page (8KB)                              Index entry ──▶ points only at the chain HEAD
   line pointers →  [LP1 REDIRECT]──▶[LP2]──▶[LP3]
   tuples:
     v1  xmin=100 xmax=150  t_ctid→v2   (DEAD after prune)
     v2  xmin=150 xmax=170  t_ctid→v3   (DEAD)   ← HOT chain
     v3  xmin=170 xmax=0    t_ctid→self (LIVE)
   A tx with a snapshot older than 150 still sees v1  → readers never block writers.

The 8 KB page and tuple layout (ties together the buffer manager §10.2, WAL pd_lsn §10.3, MVCC and VACUUM). A page fills from both ends — the line-pointer array grows down, tuples grow up, free space is in the middle:

 8 KB heap page                               HeapTupleHeaderData (23 B + null bitmap)
 ┌────────────────────────────────────┐      ┌──────────────────────────────────────┐
 │ PageHeaderData (pd_lsn, pd_checksum,│      │ t_xmin       creator xid             │
 │   pd_lower ↓, pd_upper ↑, pd_special)│     │ t_xmax       deleter xid / multixact │
 │ ItemId[] line pointers → grow DOWN ↓│      │ t_cid/t_xvac command id              │
 │   [LP1][LP2][LP3]…                  │      │ t_ctid       → next version (HOT)    │
 │        ▒▒ free space ▒▒  (pd_lower) │      │ t_infomask2  natts + HOT/key flags   │
 │   …tuples grow UP ↑     (pd_upper)  │      │ t_infomask   hint bits, HEAP_HASNULL │
 │   [tuple3][tuple2][tuple1]          │      │ t_hoff       offset to user data     │
 │ pd_special (index AMs only)         │      └──────────────────────────────────────┘
 └────────────────────────────────────┘        pd_lower == pd_upper  ⇒  page is full

TOAST — where big values go. A tuple must fit within a page (8 KB), so when a row exceeds ~2 KB (TOAST_TUPLE_THRESHOLD) PostgreSQL applies TOAST (The Oversized-Attribute Storage Technique): per-column storage strategy — PLAIN (never toasted), EXTENDED (compress, then move out-of-line if still big; default for text/bytea/jsonb), EXTERNAL (out-of-line, no compression — faster substring reads), MAIN (compress in-line, avoid out-of-line if possible). Out-of-line values are chunked into a hidden per-table TOAST table (pg_toast.pg_toast_<oid>) with its own index; the main tuple keeps a small TOAST pointer. That's why a wide jsonb/text column doesn't bloat every sequential scan — the heap tuple stays small and the big bytes are fetched only when that column is read.

10.5 🧹 VACUUM — phases, the visibility map, pruning and freezing

VACUUM reclaims dead tuples and, critically, freezes old XIDs to prevent 32-bit wraparound. A modern (non-FULL) vacuum runs in phases and, unlike VACUUM FULL, does not rewrite the table and takes only a weak lock (writes continue):

  1. Heap scan / prune (phase 1): heap_page_prune_and_freeze (PG17; heap_page_prune in PG16 and earlier — PG17 merged pruning and freezing into one pass) removes dead tuples within a page, turns their line pointers into LP_DEAD/LP_UNUSED/LP_REDIRECT, and collects the dead TIDs (PG17+ stores them in a memory-efficient TidStore radix tree; older versions used a maintenance_work_mem array).
  2. Index vacuum: each index removes entries pointing at those dead TIDs (parallel workers possible).
  3. Heap phase 2: now that no index references them, the dead line pointers become LP_UNUSED (reusable), and the Free Space Map (FSM) is updated.
If the dead-TID collection exhausts its memory budget, VACUUM loops phases 1–3 multiple times (each round re-scans indexes) — expensive on big tables. PG17's TidStore stores far more TIDs in the same memory, so these extra index passes become rare.

The Visibility Map (VM) — 2 bits per heap page (all-visible, all-frozen) — is what makes this scale: VACUUM (and index-only scans) can skip all-visible pages entirely, and an Index Only Scan can answer without visiting the heap when the page is all-visible.

Freezing & wraparound (the critical part): XIDs are 32-bit and wrap; PostgreSQL keeps a moving 2³¹ visibility horizon. Tuples older than vacuum_freeze_min_age are marked frozen (permanently visible); relfrozenxid/relminmxid track the oldest unfrozen value per table. When age(relfrozenxid) crosses vacuum_freeze_table_age VACUUM becomes aggressive; crossing autovacuum_freeze_max_age triggers a forced anti-wraparound autovacuum even if autovacuum is off. If nothing freezes in time, the server stops accepting writes to protect the data — hence monitoring age(relfrozenxid) matters.

  autovacuum picks the table  (dead tuples OR age(relfrozenxid))
        │                                     ╎ age > autovacuum_freeze_max_age
        ▼                                     ▼
  Phase 1: scan heap                    FORCED anti-wraparound vacuum
    heap_page_prune → mark LP_DEAD      (runs even if autovacuum = off)
    collect dead TIDs (TidStore)
    freeze old xids → relfrozenxid↑
        │
        ▼
  Phase 2: index vacuum — remove index entries for dead TIDs (parallel workers)
        │
        ▼
  Phase 3: heap cleanup — LP_DEAD → LP_UNUSED (reusable); update FSM + Visibility Map
        │
        ▼
  Visibility Map bits set → future VACUUMs & index-only scans SKIP the page

  Line-pointer life:
    LP_NORMAL ──(tuple dies)──▶ LP_DEAD ──(index vacuum done)──▶ LP_UNUSED ──(reused by next INSERT)
                   └─ HOT chain head kept as LP_REDIRECT so the index still finds the live version
PART 2 — PostgreSQL for teachers of adults 🎓

Track A · PostgreSQL foundations · 🟢 Beginner→🟡 Intermediate · ▶ next: Part 3

1. 🪵 How the WAL works on the inside

Physical rule: no data change reaches the disk before its WAL record is already on disk.

Flow of a write

  1. You modify a row → the page (8 KB) in shared_buffers changes; it becomes dirty.
  2. Before touching anything, a WAL record is generated in the WAL buffers.
  3. On COMMIT, an fsync() of the WAL is done up to the commit LSN. Only then is it durable.
  4. The dirty page goes down to disk later (checkpointer/bgwriter). The WAL already protects it.

Key concepts

  • LSN: byte address in the WAL stream (e.g. 16/B374D848). It is the "clock" of the database. Each page stores the LSN of its last change (pd_lsn): it does not go down to disk until that WAL LSN is persisted.
  • WAL segments: 16 MB files in pg_wal/, sequential and recycled.
  • WAL record: header (length, XID, previous LSN, CRC, resource manager) + data.
  • Full Page Writes: the first modification of a page after a checkpoint writes the full image, to guard against torn pages (half-finished writes from a power cut). wal_compression compresses them.

wal_level

minimal (crash only) · replica (default: archiving and physical replicas) · logical (logical replication / CDC).

Crash recovery (REDO)

The startup process finds the last checkpoint and re-applies the WAL from the "REDO point". Each record is idempotent with respect to the page's LSN. A confirmed commit is never lost.

ParameterWhat it's for
synchronous_commiton = full durability; off = faster but you may lose recent transactions (no corruption).
wal_buffersSize of the WAL buffer in memory.
max_wal_size / min_wal_sizeWAL accumulated before a checkpoint / kept for recycling.
checkpoint_timeoutHow often a checkpoint is forced (def. 5 min).
checkpoint_completion_targetSpreads out the checkpoint write across the interval (default 0.9 since PG14 — smooths the I/O; was 0.5 before).
wal_compressionCompresses the full-page images.
archive_mode / archive_command / archive_libraryArchiving of segments (PITR).

2. 🔍 How to read an EXPLAIN

EXPLAIN (ANALYZE, BUFFERS, VERBOSE, FORMAT TEXT)
SELECT * FROM pedidos WHERE cliente_id = 42;

Anatomy of a node

Index Scan using idx_cliente on pedidos  (cost=0.42..8.45 rows=3 width=64) (actual time=0.018..0.021 rows=2 loops=1)
  • cost=0.42..8.45 → startup_cost..total_cost in abstract units (not ms). First vs last row.
  • rows=3 → estimated rows; width=64 → average width in bytes.
  • actual time=... → real time (ANALYZE only), per iteration.
  • rows=2 loops=1 → actual rows and number of executions. ⚠️ they are per loop: multiply by loops.
It is read from the inside out and from the bottom up: the most indented nodes execute first.

Most common nodes

  • Seq Scan (reads the whole table) · Index Scan · Index Only Scan (index only, depends on the visibility map).
  • Bitmap Index + Bitmap Heap Scan (medium selectivity).
  • Nested Loop / Hash Join / Merge Join (the three ways to join).
  • Gather / Parallel … (parallel) · Sort / HashAggregate / Aggregate.

Trouble signs 🚩

  • Estimated vs actual very different → stale statistics → ANALYZE / raise default_statistics_target.
  • Huge Rows Removed by Filter → missing index.
  • Sort Method: external merge Disk → raise work_mem.
  • High Heap Fetches in an Index Only Scan → missing vacuum (visibility map).
  • With BUFFERS: shared hit (cache) vs read (disk). Lots of read = you're working outside memory.

3. 🧹 How to configure autovacuum

It fights bloat (dead MVCC tuples) and transaction-ID wraparound.

When it fires

tuplas_muertas > autovacuum_vacuum_threshold + autovacuum_vacuum_scale_factor × n_filas_vivas
  • autovacuum_vacuum_threshold (def. 50)
  • autovacuum_vacuum_scale_factor (def. 0.2 = 20%) → on large tables it's better to lower it (0.05 / 0.01).
  • ANALYZE: autovacuum_analyze_threshold + autovacuum_analyze_scale_factor (def. 0.1).
  • Since PG13, vacuum on inserts: autovacuum_vacuum_insert_threshold / _insert_scale_factor.

The engine and its pace

  • autovacuum = on (never turn it off globally).
  • autovacuum_max_workers (def. 3, tables in parallel) · autovacuum_naptime (def. 1 min, how often the launcher checks).
  • I/O brake: autovacuum_vacuum_cost_limit and autovacuum_vacuum_cost_delay. If the vacuum doesn't finish, raise the cost_limit or lower the cost_delay.

Anti-wraparound (critical) ⚠️

XIDs are 32-bit and get recycled. If a table is not frozen (freeze) in time, PostgreSQL stops writes. Defaults: vacuum_freeze_min_age (50M — tuples older than this get frozen), vacuum_freeze_table_age (150M — VACUUM turns aggressive, whole-table scan), autovacuum_freeze_max_age (200M — forced anti-wraparound vacuum runs even if autovacuum is off). As the oldest XID nears the limit the server warns (~40M remaining) then refuses new XIDs at ~1M remaining — watch age(relfrozenxid) well before that.

SELECT relname, age(relfrozenxid)
FROM pg_class WHERE relkind='r' ORDER BY age(relfrozenxid) DESC LIMIT 20;

Per-table tuning (best practice)

ALTER TABLE eventos SET (
  autovacuum_vacuum_scale_factor = 0.02,
  autovacuum_vacuum_threshold    = 1000,
  autovacuum_vacuum_cost_delay   = 2
);

4. 🔌 Connections and all the processes at work

Multiprocess model (not multithreaded): each client has its own OS process; all of them share shared memory.

The connection "handshake"

  1. The client opens a socket (TCP on port 5432 or a unix socket).
  2. The postmaster accepts and does a fork(), creating a dedicated backend.
  3. The client sends the startup packet (database, user, parameters).
  4. Authentication according to pg_hba.conf: scram-sha-256 (recommended), md5, peer, ident, cert, ldap, trust…
  5. AuthenticationOk + parameters + BackendKeyData → backend in the idle state.
💡 Each backend costs memory and fork() is not free. With many short connections you use a pooler (PgBouncer, Pgpool-II).

The full cast of processes 🎭

Supervisor and client

  • Postmaster: listens on the port, launches and watches over everyone, restarts on failures. It does not run queries.
  • Backend (postgres): one per connection. Parses, plans, and executes SQL; uses shared_buffers; generates WAL. Dies on disconnect.

Memory and disk

  • Background Writer (bgwriter): writes dirty pages to disk ahead of time and gradually, so the backends always find clean buffers. bgwriter_delay, bgwriter_lru_maxpages.
  • Checkpointer: runs checkpoints: flushes all dirty pages up to a point, writes the checkpoint record, marks the "REDO point", and allows recycling old WAL. checkpoint_completion_target spreads out the work.
  • WAL Writer: flushes the WAL buffers periodically (wal_writer_delay); useful with synchronous_commit=off.

Maintenance

  • Autovacuum Launcher (decides what to clean, every autovacuum_naptime) + Autovacuum Workers (run VACUUM/ANALYZE, up to autovacuum_max_workers).
  • Logger (Logging Collector): collects the log from all the processes.
  • Cumulative statistics (pg_stat_*). 📌 Since PG15 the old stats collector (UDP) is gone: they now live in shared memory.

Replication / high availability

  • WAL Sender (walsender): on the primary; one per replica; sends the WAL.
  • WAL Receiver (walreceiver): on the replica; receives the WAL, writes it, and passes it to the startup process to apply it in a loop.
  • Archiver: with archive_mode=on, copies completed WAL segments to an external store (archive_command/archive_library). Basis of PITR.
  • Startup Process: does the redo on startup; on a replica it never ends (applies WAL in a loop).
  • Logical Replication Launcher / Workers: orchestrate publications/subscriptions; the apply workers apply the changes.

Generic and parallelism

  • Background Workers: a general mechanism used by core and extensions (max_worker_processes).
  • Parallel Workers: collaborate on parallel queries; the Gather node merges results.

Who writes what to disk

WhatWho
Data (dirty pages)Checkpointer (in bulk) + bgwriter (continuous)
WALBackends (on commit, fsync) + WAL writer (periodic)
Archived WALArchiver
WAL to another machinewalsender → walreceiver

5. 🌐 Physical vs logical replication

PostgreSQL can copy data to other servers in two very different ways. Both travel over the WAL, but at different levels.

Physical replication (streaming / WAL shipping)

It copies the cluster byte by byte: the replica is an exact binary clone of the primary. The walsender sends the WAL stream and the replica's walreceiver applies it with the startup process in a continuous loop.

  • Level: blocks/pages (raw WAL). It replicates the whole cluster, you can't choose tables.
  • The replica is read-only (hot standby): useful for queries and failover.
  • Requires the same major version and the same architecture/platform.
  • It can be asynchronous (fast, minimal possible loss) or synchronous (synchronous_standby_names, zero loss).
  • Replication slots prevent the primary from deleting WAL that the replica hasn't consumed yet.
  • Uses: high availability, disaster recovery, scaling reads.

Logical replication (publish / subscribe)

It decodes the WAL into row-by-row changes (requires wal_level = logical) and sends them to a subscriber. It is based on a PUBLICATION (on the source) and a SUBSCRIPTION (on the target); some apply workers apply the changes.

  • Level: logical rows (decoded INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE). You choose specific tables.
  • The target is writable and can have other tables, indexes, or even a different major version.
  • It works between different versions and different platforms → ideal for near-zero-downtime upgrades.
  • It needs a replica identity (primary key or equivalent) for UPDATE/DELETE.
  • Limitations: it doesn't replicate DDL, nor sequences automatically, nor large objects; conflicts must be handled.
  • Uses: CDC, consolidating/distributing data, migrations and upgrades.
AspectPhysical (streaming)Logical (pub/sub)
Copy levelBlocks / raw WALRows (logical decoding)
GranularityThe whole clusterChosen tables
TargetRead-only (hot standby)Writable
VersionsSame major versionMay differ
wal_levelreplicalogical
Processeswalsender → walreceiver + startupwalsender (logical) → apply workers
Typical caseHA / DR / failoverCDC / upgrades / distribution

6. 🗺️ Diagrams

6.1 Process and memory architecture

The postmaster accepts connections and creates a backend per client; all of them share shared_buffers and WAL buffers; the auxiliary processes move the data to disk and to the replicas.

6.2 The path of a write (WAL → durability)

Golden write-ahead rule: the WAL record reaches disk before the data page. The commit is durable at step 3, long before the actual page touches the disk.

6.3 Checkpoint cycle

The checkpoint marks a safe point: it limits how much WAL must be re-applied after a crash and allows freeing/recycling old segments.

6.4 Connection handshake and authentication

From step 6 onward, the client talks directly with its backend (the postmaster no longer intervenes in the queries).

6.5 Physical vs logical replication

The two ways to replicate come from the same WAL: what changes is the level (bytes vs rows) and the flexibility (whole cluster vs chosen tables).

6.6 Flow of a query (parser → planner → executor)

The parse tree is pure syntax; the query tree already understands tables and views; the plan tree is the cheapest strategy; the executor runs it by pulling tuples. EXPLAIN shows you exactly that plan tree.

6.7 MVCC and VACUUM (live and dead row versions)

MVCC never overwrites a row: it creates a new version and marks the old one with xmax. The dead versions that nobody can see anymore are the bloat that VACUUM collects to reuse the space.

PART 3 — Internal anatomy of the processes 🔬

Track A · PostgreSQL foundations · 🟢 Beginner→🟡 Intermediate · ▶ next: Part 4

0. The multiprocess model (why all this exists)

PostgreSQL is multiprocess, not multithreaded: each connection and each background task is an independent operating-system process. This gives robustness (if one crashes, it doesn't corrupt the others' memory space), but it creates a problem: how do processes that don't share memory by default communicate? The answer is a region of shared memory created at startup, plus synchronization mechanisms: spinlocks, LWLocks, latches, semaphores, and Unix signals. Everything that follows revolves around that.

1. 🎩 The Postmaster — the supervisor

It's the first process (the one launched by pg_ctl start) and the parent of all the others. Its philosophy: be so simple and robust that it never crashes, so it can watch over and resurrect the others.

At startup (in this order)

  1. Reads the configuration: postgresql.conf, pg_hba.conf, pg_ident.conf.
  2. Creates the shared memory and the semaphores. Sizes a single contiguous segment (via shared anonymous mmap or System V shmem) before having children, so that when it does fork() they inherit it.
  3. Opens the listening sockets: TCP on listen_addresses:port and the unix domain socket.
  4. Launches the startup process (WAL REDO if the shutdown was not clean).
  5. Launches the auxiliary and background processes: checkpointer, bgwriter, WAL writer, autovacuum launcher, archiver, logger, logical replication launcher.
  6. Writes postmaster.pid and stays listening.

During operation

  • Accepts connections: does accept() and fork() of a backend. ⚠️ The postmaster does NOT authenticate or read the startup packet — that's done by the already-forked backend. That way it stays lightweight.
  • Supervises with a signal-driven state machine; it wakes up on SIGCHLD when a child dies.
Crash-and-restart: if a backend dies abnormally (e.g. a segfault), the postmaster assumes the shared memory may have been corrupted, sends SIGQUIT to all the children, waits for them to die, and reinitializes the whole instance (controlled by restart_after_crash). That's why a single connection that causes a crash briefly takes down the whole instance: it's a decision in favor of integrity.

Deliberate detail: even though it creates the shared memory, it avoids reading/writing its structures in normal operation — if it were corrupted and it touched it, it could crash too, and nobody would do the restart. It's the "last man standing".

SignalEffect
SIGHUPReloads config and propagates it to the children.
SIGTERMSmart shutdown: lets active sessions finish.
SIGINTFast shutdown: aborts transactions, checkpoint, closes.
SIGQUITImmediate shutdown: kills everything without a checkpoint (it will recover on startup).

2. 🧑‍💻 The Backends — one process per connection

The backend (postgres) serves one connection from start to finish. After the fork():

  1. Reads the startup packet (protocol, database, user, options).
  2. Authenticates according to pg_hba.conf.
  3. Attaches to the shared memory (inherited) and initializes its local state.
  4. Registers in the ProcArray: creates its PGPROC entry. This is essential for MVCC: that's where the visibility snapshots are computed.
  5. Enters the query loop: parser → analyzer/rewriter → planner → executor → rows.

Local vs shared memory

  • Private (per process): its memory contexts, work_mem, temp_buffers, and the catalog caches (relcache, catcache/syscache, plan cache).
  • Shared (common): shared_buffers, locks, ProcArray, CLOG…

Cache invalidation between backends

If a backend does ALTER TABLE, the others have an old copy in their relcache. The shared invalidation queue (SI) resolves this: the backend that changed the catalog emits invalidation messages through shared memory; the others consume them and discard their stale caches. On disconnect, the backend releases locks, deletes its PGPROC, and dies.

3. 🧠 The Shared Memory — the common bulletin board

A single contiguous segment, sized at startup (it doesn't grow hot; that's why changing shared_buffers requires a restart). Its tenants:

Shared memory is the only way PostgreSQL processes coordinate state: page cache, pending WAL, transaction visibility, locks, and coordination queues.

  • Buffer Pool: array of 8 KB buffers + descriptors (dirty flag, usage_count, pin count, content lock) + a mapping hash table (page)→(buffer). Replacement by clock-sweep.
  • WAL buffers: circular buffer of the WAL not yet flushed to pg_wal.
  • CLOG/pg_xact and other SLRUs (pg_subtrans, pg_multixact, pg_commit_ts): transaction state, vital for visibility.
  • ProcArray: source of truth for the snapshots; its global xmin limits how far VACUUM can clean.
  • Lock manager (heavyweight), predicate locks, LWLocks (shared/exclusive), and spinlocks (very brief exclusion).
  • Queues: fsync, invalidation (SI); stats in shared memory (PG15+, formerly a separate process); latches to wake processes.

4. 🧹 Autovacuum Launcher and Workers

The Launcher (scheduler, not laborer)

A permanent process (if autovacuum=on). It cleans nothing. Its loop: sleeps autovacuum_naptime / number_of_databases → chooses the next database (prioritizing wraparound risk) → since only the postmaster can fork(), it asks the postmaster (via signal + AutoVacuumShmem) to start a worker → goes back to sleep.

The Workers (laborers)

  1. They connect to the assigned database (unlike the auxiliary processes).
  2. They read the cumulative stats and pg_class and decide per table: dead tuples > threshold → VACUUM; inserts (PG13+) → VACUUM; age(relfrozenxid) > autovacuum_freeze_max_age → forced freeze VACUUM; changes > threshold → ANALYZE.
  3. They vacuum/analyze and die.

Coordination (the fine print)

  • autovacuum_max_workers limits concurrency; they coordinate through shared memory to not pick the same table.
  • Sharing of the I/O brake: the autovacuum_vacuum_cost_limit budget is split among the active workers; when exhausted, they sleep autovacuum_vacuum_cost_delay.
  • Safety net: even if you set autovacuum=off, emergency vacuums are launched against wraparound.

5. ⚙️ The Auxiliary Processes — core infrastructure

A distinct category from backends and background workers: a fixed set of core processes that don't serve clients nor (in general) run SQL. The postmaster forks them and they attach to the shared memory.

  • Background Writer: every bgwriter_delay it uses the clock-sweep to get ahead and write dirty buffers about to be replaced, so that backends find clean buffers. It doesn't do fsync (only writes); it passes the fsync requests to the checkpointer.
  • Checkpointer: runs checkpoints (flushes dirty pages → absorbs the fsync queue and does fsync of the data files → writes the checkpoint record → updates pg_control with the REDO point → allows recycling WAL). checkpoint_completion_target spreads out the I/O. A dedicated process since 9.2.
  • WAL Writer: flushes the WAL buffers periodically. With synchronous_commit=on the backend itself does the fsync up to its LSN; with off, the WAL writer guarantees the flush within a bounded window (there you can lose the last transactions, never corruption).
  • Startup Process: on startup it does REDO from the last checkpoint; on a replica it never ends (applies WAL indefinitely).
  • WAL Receiver: on the standby, started by the startup process; it connects to the primary's walsender, receives the WAL, writes it to its pg_wal, and notifies the startup process; it sends LSN feedback.
  • Archiver: with archive_mode=on, when a .ready appears it runs archive_command to copy the segment and marks it .done. The basis of PITR.
  • Logger (syslogger): captures the stderr of all the processes and writes it with rotation.
Version note: the old stats collector was another auxiliary process, but it disappeared in PG15 (the stats moved to shared memory).

6. 🧩 Background Workers — the extensible framework

A different category from the auxiliary processes: a generic framework (static registration since 9.3; dynamic registration since 9.4) for the core and the extensions to launch child processes managed by the postmaster.

  • Registration: static (shared_preload_libraries + RegisterBackgroundWorker()) or dynamic (RegisterDynamicBackgroundWorker() hot). max_worker_processes limits them. They can connect to a database, attach to shared memory, and participate in transactions.
  • Parallel query: the leader backend launches N dynamic workers; they communicate through DSM and shared-memory queues (shm_mq); the Gather node merges results (max_parallel_workers, max_parallel_workers_per_gather).
  • Logical replication: the launcher and the apply workers are background workers.
  • Extensions: pg_cron, Citus, maintenance agents, etc.

7. 📡 WAL Senders (walsender)

A special backend (not an auxiliary process) that is born when a client connects in replication mode. One per streaming replica/client; max_wal_senders limits them. It reads the WAL and sends it to:

  • physical standbys (streaming),
  • pg_basebackup / pg_receivewal,
  • logical subscribers (running the logical decoding with an output plugin like pgoutput).

Replication slots

They record the consumer's progress: restart_lsn (how far to keep WAL), confirmed_flush_lsn and, in logical, the catalog xmin (prevents VACUUM from deleting catalog still needed). ⚠️ An abandoned slot can fill the disk with retained WAL.

Synchronous replication (the finest interaction)

  1. The backend that does COMMIT writes its WAL and goes to sleep waiting for confirmation (SyncRepWaitForLSN).
  2. The walsender sends the WAL; the walreceiver responds that it has already written/applied it.
  3. The walsender updates shared memory and wakes the backend (touches its latch).
  4. Only then does the COMMIT return "ok" → zero loss.

Also: wal_sender_timeout (keepalives) and hot_standby_feedback (the standby communicates its xmin so that the primary's VACUUM doesn't delete rows that its long queries are still reading).

8. 🔗 Choreography: how they interact

Startup

postmaster → creates shared memory + sockets → startup process (REDO) → checkpointer, bgwriter, walwriter, autovacuum launcher, archiver, logger → accepts connections (forks backends).

A write transaction (who touches what)

  1. The backend requests the buffer (pins it, takes the content lock), modifies the row → buffer dirty.
  2. Inserts the WAL record into the WAL buffers (WAL insert lock).
  3. On COMMIT: fsync of the WAL up to its LSN (or delegates it to the walwriter if synchronous_commit=off), marks the XID committed in CLOG, clears its XID from the ProcArray.
  4. Later, bgwriter (trickle) / checkpointer (bulk) flush the page; the checkpointer does the real fsync.
  5. The walsender sends that WAL to the standby's walreceiver → its startup process applies it.
  6. When the segment completes, the archiver copies it to the external store.

Reads, MVCC, and maintenance

  • A backend reads the ProcArray for its snapshot and consults CLOG for visibility (xmin/xmax). The global xmin tells autovacuum how far to clean.
  • A DDL emits SI messages; the other backends refresh their caches.
  • The autovacuum launcher requests workers from the postmaster; they clean while respecting the global xmin and freeze XIDs.

Mechanisms that thread it all together

MechanismWhat for
Shared memoryCommon state (buffers, ProcArray, locks, queues, stats).
LWLocks / spinlocksProtect those structures.
LatchesWake a sleeping process (e.g. walsender → backend on synchronous commit).
Unix signalsThe postmaster orders (reload/shutdown) and reacts to dead children.
SemaphoresSleep/wake on heavyweight locks.
DSM + shm_mqTemporary communication between leader and parallel workers.
PART 4 — What changes in Lakebase 🧱

Track B · Lakebase essentials · 🟢 Beginner→🟡 Intermediate · ▶ next: Part 13

0. The underlying shift

Lakebase is the managed Postgres from Databricks, built on Neon. At the compute layer it is real PostgreSQL (16/17), but its big idea is to separate compute and storage, with the WAL as the single write/durability interface (compute → safekeepers). Pages come back over a separate request/response page-service protocol (GetPage@LSN, pageserver → compute) — the write path one direction, the read path the other.

  • Compute (compute endpoint): a standard Postgres that owns no durable state — "it exists to run work, not to preserve data".
  • Safekeepers: receive the WAL and make it durable with Paxos consensus (quorum). The commit is durable when a quorum confirms → the latency depends on the network, not the local disk.
  • Pageservers: reconstruct each page by combining a base image + WAL deltas; they serve pages to the compute and offload cold data to object storage (S3).

Consequence: everything that in standard Postgres revolved around "flushing pages to local files and doing fsync of the local WAL" is replaced. The SQL engine is kept almost intact.

The WAL stops being a "local-disk diary" and becomes the network interface between compute (Postgres) and storage (safekeepers + pageservers + S3).

1. What of the above applies? (table)

Topic explainedIn LakebaseDetail
SQL, ACID, MVCC (xmin/xmax)✅ SameIt's real Postgres; semantics intact.
Indexes (B-tree, GIN, GiST, BRIN, pgvector)✅ SameThey work the same.
Query flow and EXPLAIN✅ SameParser/planner/executor unchanged.
Postmaster + backends✅ SameThe compute is real Postgres.
Background / parallel workers✅ SameParallel query and bgworkers operate.
shared_buffers / ProcArray / CLOG / locks✅ Same (+ extra)They exist the same; the NVMe cache (LFC) is added.
autovacuum (anti-wraparound)✅ Still ⚠️Still necessary; the I/O economics change and the config is managed.
WAL (write-ahead, LSN)🔄 ChangesDurability moved from the local pg_wal to the safekeepers (Paxos). The WAL is the compute↔storage interface.
Full Page Writes❌ DisabledThere's no local page to tear → −94% WAL traffic (~58 KB → <4 KB per transaction).
WAL fsync on COMMIT🔄 ReplacedDurability = safekeeper quorum, not local fsync.
Checkpointer🔄 Heavily changedThere are no local data files; image generation is pushed to the pageserver, by the real number of changes per page.
Background Writer🔄 ChangedIts function is distributed across the pageservers.
WAL Writer🔄 ChangedThe compute transmits WAL over the network, not to the local disk.
Startup process / REDO🔄 ChangedStorage reconstructs pages from the WAL history.
Archiver + archive_command (PITR)❌→⭐ ReplacedClassic PITR → instant branching / restore (copy-on-write).
Physical replication (standby with its own copy)🔄 ReplacedThe read replicas share the same storage (pageservers); there's no second physical copy.
Logical replication (pub/sub)✅ SupportedClassic bridge for moving data in/out.

2. What is ⭐ exclusive to Lakebase (doesn't exist in standard Postgres)

  1. Separation of storage and compute — the basis of everything (standard Postgres is monolithic).
  2. Safekeepers (durability via Paxos) and Pageservers (page reconstruction from WAL).
  3. Local File Cache (LFC) — a second-tier read cache of pages on local NVMe, sitting between shared_buffers (RAM) and the pageserver (network). It is managed in chunks (128 KB default, up to 1 MB), resized live with the compute VM, and its working-set estimate drives compute autoscaling (§7.1). Read path: RAM → NVMe → pageserver; losing it only costs a cold cache, never data (deep-dive in Part 5 §2).
  4. Copy-on-write branching — clone the entire database instantly (dev/test/agents), paying only for what's modified.
  5. Instant restore / PITR — over references to the history, without copying data.
  6. Scale-to-zero and autoscaling of the compute (the state lives in the storage).
  7. Full Page Writes disabled by design — an optimization impossible in standard Postgres.
  8. Integration with Databricks / Lakehouse: synced tables (managed Unity Catalog/Delta ↔ Postgres synchronization), governance with Unity Catalog, Databricks identity/OAuth, and integration with Apps / feature serving.

3. Constraints of the managed model

  • No full superuser nor access to the host filesystem.
  • No tablespaces nor custom file paths (there's no durable local disk).
  • Limited extensions to a supported set (~52: pgvector, PostGIS, pg_stat_statements…).
  • Many GUCs are managed (you don't freely reconfigure full_page_writes, the checkpointer, or shared_buffers).
  • archive_mode/archive_command are not your backup lever → you use the platform's branching/PITR.
The architectural details are confirmed against the official documentation (see the sources at the bottom); some specific limits of the managed service (exact extension list, exposed GUCs) evolve — it's best to check them against the current Lakebase documentation.

4. 🧠 Mental rule

The Postgres "engine" → the same: SQL, MVCC, planner, executor, indexes, backends, autovacuum, locks, snapshots.

The "storage and durability layer" → replaced: local fsync, full page writes, checkpointer/bgwriter writing to local files, classic archiver/PITR, and physical standby with its own copy → safekeepers + pageservers + branching.

The WAL stops being a local-disk diary and becomes the network interface between compute and storage.

5. 🔄 Synced tables (snapshot / triggered / continuous)

The synced tables serve lakehouse data inside Lakebase Postgres with low latency (this is reverse ETL): a Unity Catalog / Delta table is synced to a Postgres table that your apps query directly. The sync is run by a managed Lakeflow Spark Declarative Pipeline (serverless).

Snapshot re-copies the whole source table and swaps it in place atomically; triggered and continuous apply only the changes since the last run (that's why they both require Change Data Feed on the source). The synced tables are read-only so as not to break integrity with the source.

  • Primary key required: it allows reading/updating/deleting rows efficiently; the PK columns cannot be NULL (rows with null in the PK are excluded).
  • Only additive schema changes (e.g. adding a column) are reflected in triggered/continuous modes.
  • When to choose each mode: snapshot when >10% of rows change per cycle (~10× more efficient) or the source doesn't support CDF (views, Iceberg tables); triggered when rows change incrementally on a predictable schedule — good cost/lag balance, but running more often than ~every 5 min can cost more than continuous; continuous for the lowest possible lag (refresh cycle min. ~15 s), accepting the highest cost.
  • How to choose: weigh change frequency (how much of the source turns over per cycle), required freshness, and cost tolerance. Snapshot works universally (no CDF) but re-copies the full table each run; triggered and continuous are incremental but both require Change Data Feed enabled on the source.

👉 The synced tables are the UC → Lakebase direction (reverse ETL). The opposite direction, Lakebase → UC (forward ETL / Change Data Feed), is covered in §5.17 with its code.

6. 🌿 How branching works on the inside

A branch is a copy-on-write clone of your database: it's created instantly because it doesn't copy data, it only creates a reference to a point in the history. Recalling Part 4.0: the storage (pageservers) keeps the data as base images + WAL deltas addressed by LSN. Branching takes advantage of exactly that.

Below the branch LSN, both branches share the same pages (zero copy). Above it, each branch accumulates its own WAL deltas. Restoring or a PITR is simply attaching a compute to an earlier LSN of the history.

  • What a branch is: a new line of history (timeline) that starts from a parent LSN. The pageserver serves the branch's pages by reusing the parent's history up to that LSN and applying, on top, only the branch's deltas.
  • When you write to the branch: "the changes are saved as a delta" — new WAL in the branch's timeline. It doesn't affect or add load to the parent.
  • Storage cost: only what's modified consumes extra space; the common history isn't duplicated.
  • Restore / PITR: "you can roll the branch back to any point within the history window". Since everything is addressed by LSN, restoring = pointing the compute at a past LSN (without copying data).
  • Compute: each branch can have its own compute endpoint (scales/stops independently), all backed by the same shared storage.
  • Typical uses in Lakebase: instant dev/test environments, agent/AI testing on real data without touching production, and validating migrations before applying them.

What happens when you write to the branch

The branch's compute endpoint generates WAL → it goes to the safekeepers (consensus) → the pageserver ingests it and creates new DeltaLayers in the branch's timeline. The parent neither finds out nor receives additional load. Each branch can have its own compute (scales and shuts down independently) on the same shared storage.

Lifecycle (how it's created and connected)

  1. You request a branch (Databricks API/SDK/UI) → the control plane calls CreateBranch.
  2. The child Timeline is created pointing at parent@LSN (metadata, instant — see §5.14).
  3. A compute endpoint is provisioned/assigned for that branch (see §5.16).
  4. You connect via normal Postgres, with its own identity/role and its databricks_branch_id (via databricks_auth).

Restore / Point-in-Time is branching

Since everything is addressed by LSN, "going back in time" is creating/positioning a timeline at an earlier LSN. A PITR to "yesterday at 2:00 PM" = a branch whose branch point is the LSN of that moment. No data is copied; the history is referenced. Condition: that LSN must be within the history window (what the pageserver's GC hasn't yet removed).

Cost, isolation, and limits

  • Storage cost: only the branch's own deltas; the common history isn't duplicated.
  • Total isolation: the branch's reads/writes don't affect the parent's performance.
  • Time limit: you can't branch from a point older than your retention window.
  • It's not bidirectional replication: a branch doesn't "re-merge" into the parent like a Git merge; it's a fork. To bring changes back you'd use migrations/ETL.

What it's used for

  • Instant dev/test on real production data, without copying it or risking it.
  • Agent/AI testing: each agent works on its disposable branch.
  • Validating schema migrations before applying them in production.
  • PITR / recovery from a human error ("I dropped a table at 10:05" → branch at 10:04).
  • CI: an ephemeral branch per pull request.
In one sentence: a branch is a new timeline that points at the parent at an LSN; below that point it shares the immutable layers of the parent (copy-on-write, zero copy) and above it accumulates its own deltas — orchestrated by the control plane (CreateBranch) and served by the pageserver, with its own isolated compute.
Accuracy note: the mechanics of copy-on-write, per-branch delta, history window, and PITR are documented; the finer detail (timelines, image/delta layers addressed by LSN in the pageserver) is derived from the storage architecture described in Part 4.0 and is confirmed in the code in §5.14–5.15.

7. ⚖️ Autoscaling of compute and storage

This is the elasticity that the separation of compute and storage (Part 4.0) makes possible. Because the compute holds no durable state and the storage lives in pageservers + S3 addressed by LSN, the two layers scale independently and along different axes: the compute scales vertically (more/less CPU·RAM for the same endpoint) and down to zero when idle; the storage scales elastically and automatically — there is no disk to pre-provision and nothing to "run out of".

Two layers, two axes. The compute moves between 0 / min CU / max CU in place; the storage spills from RAM/NVMe to pageservers to S3 and grows on demand. They are decoupled because the only thing crossing between them is the WAL stream.

7.1 Compute autoscaling (vertical + scale-to-zero)

The compute runs inside a micro-VM (NeonVM/QEMU on Kubernetes) whose CPU and RAM are resized live, without restarting Postgres and without dropping TCP connections (live migration). Size is expressed in Compute Units (CU) and you configure a min CU / max CU range; the platform keeps the VM as small as the workload allows and grows it only when needed.

⚠️ What "1 CU" means depends on the platform (don't mix these up): the ratio differs between Lakebase versions (Part 13/15) — Lakebase v2 (Autoscaling): 1 CU = 0.25 vCPU + 2 GB RAM (range 0.5…64 CU); Lakebase v1 (Provisioned): 1 CU = 2 vCPU + 16 GB RAM (CU_1…CU_8); and upstream Neon.com uses yet another ratio (~1 vCPU + 4 GB). The mechanism below is the same; only the unit size changes. This section describes v2 Autoscaling (the model all new instances use).

The key idea is a two-sided approval — the autoscaler-agent decides a target size, but scaling up needs the scheduler's OK (is there room on the node?) and scaling down needs the vm-monitor's OK (is the VM still using that RAM?).

The agent decides; the scheduler approves upscales; the vm-monitor approves downscales and can force an immediate upscale on a memory spike. The neonvm-controller does the in-place resize.

  1. The autoscaler-agent (a DaemonSet, one per node) fetches each VM's signals on two cadences: load average + memory usage every ~5 s, and the LFC working-set estimate every ~20 s (EstimateTrueWorkingSetSizeV2). The working set drives the RAM target; CPU load drives the vCPU target (DesiredCPUV3).
  2. Its scaling policy computes a desired CU, clamped to your min/max.
  3. To scale up it asks the autoscale-scheduler (a K8s scheduler plugin, AutoscaleEnforcer) — which keeps an in-memory model of each node's reservations and approves, caps, or triggers a live migration. To scale down it asks the vm-monitor (inside the VM) whether the RAM is still needed.
  4. Once approved, the agent patches the VirtualMachine object; the neonvm-controller applies the change — either QMP CPU hotplug or sysfs onlining via neonvm-runner→neonvm-daemon — and the VM grows/shrinks in place (no reconnect).
  5. The vm-monitor is notified of the new size and resizes the LFC. It also watches memory pressure continuously; on a rapid spike it can request immediate upscaling over its websocket to the agent, without waiting for the next poll.
  6. Usage (CPU-seconds/VM) is metered continuously and reported to the billing pipeline (Databricks pays in DBUs; cf. the CU-hour meter, Part 15 §15.9).
🧩 The NeonVM component cast (all part of running Postgres in a VM on K8s): vm-builder turns the compute Docker image into a QCOW2 VM image; the autoscale-scheduler places VMs on nodes; neonvm-controller reconciles the VirtualMachine/VirtualMachineMigration CRDs and drives resizing + live migration; neonvm-runner manages the QEMU process in each pod; neonvm-daemon does sysfs CPU onlining inside the guest; neonvm-sandbox wraps the workload with seccomp + AppArmor; the autoscaler-agent + vm-monitor are the two halves of the sizing decision (host / guest). (Code map: Part 5 §13.)

Scale-to-zero (suspend) and wake

When an endpoint is idle for long enough, the compute is suspended — it drops to zero and you stop paying for compute (storage is unaffected; it persists in the pageservers/S3). The next connection wakes it on demand:

The client never connects directly to Postgres when the compute is off: the proxy's wake_compute step ensures a live compute first (§5.19), starting the NeonVM via compute_manager/autoscaling; pre-warmed pools (§5.16) shorten the cold start.

  • Cold-start caveat: right after a wake (or any scale event) the caches are cold (RAM/shared_buffers and the NVMe LFC are empty), so the first queries are slower until the working set is re-cached (Part 4.9). Open, long-lived connections and a connection pooler smooth this out.
LeverEffect
min CUFloor of CPU·RAM always available (set it >0 to avoid scale-to-zero / cold starts on latency-critical apps).
max CUCeiling for bursts; protects from runaway cost and is bounded by the node.
Scale-to-zeroIdle → 0 compute cost; great for dev/test, branches, spiky or intermittent apps.
Live resizeCPU·RAM change without reconnecting → no failover, no connection storm.

7.2 Storage autoscaling (elastic by design)

There is no fixed disk to size and no "disk full" to manage. Durable data lives in the pageservers (as base images + WAL deltas) and is offloaded to object storage (S3), which is effectively unbounded. Storage grows automatically as you write, and you pay only for what you store — there is no separate "grow the volume" operation as in classic Postgres.

Storage grows along the WAL path and spills to S3, which auto-grows. GC/compaction trims history beyond the retention window. You pay roughly for live data + retained history + per-branch deltas.

What drives storage size (and therefore cost):

  • Logical data: the live size of your tables/indexes.
  • History / retention window: the WAL history kept for PITR and branching (Part 4.6). A longer window = more history retained = more storage; GC trims everything older than the window (applied_gc_cutoff_lsn, §5.14).
  • Branches: each branch adds only its own deltas (copy-on-write) — the shared history is not duplicated.
  • Bloat: dead tuples not yet vacuumed still occupy storage — autovacuum still matters (Part 4.1, Part 4.9).

Two layers scale on different axes here, and you should not confuse them:

  • Capacity (durable bytes) → elastic via pageservers + S3, automatic, no action required.
  • Throughput / fan-out → the platform scales the safekeeper/pageserver tier and can shard a large tenant across pageservers (managed; the storage controller assigns shards, §5.14). This is operated by the platform, not a knob you turn.
