The NVMe LFC (the local read cache, Part 4.2) is not durable storage: it is sized with the compute VM, so when compute scales up the cache can hold more of the working set, and when it scales to zero the cache is discarded (hence the cold start in §7.1).

Mental model: Compute autoscaling answers "how much CPU·RAM right now?" (vertical, can hit zero, live, you set min/max). Storage autoscaling answers "where do the durable bytes go as they grow?" (automatic, elastic, pay-per-use, no provisioning). They are decoupled precisely because the WAL is the only durable-write interface between them (Part 4.0; reads come back over the separate GetPage@LSN page-service protocol): you can scale or suspend the compute without touching a single durable byte.
Accuracy note: the compute autoscaling mechanism (vertical resize via NeonVM, scale-to-zero/wake through the proxy, the autoscaler-agent → scheduler → neonvm-controller loop, LFC working-set + CPU as the scaling signals) is verified in code in §5.13/§5.16/§5.19. The CU↔vCPU/RAM mapping comes from the Neon heritage and Databricks meters it in DBUs — verify the exact CU granularity, min/max ranges, suspend timeout, and retention-window defaults against the current Lakebase docs for your instance.

8. 🔐 How to connect (OAuth / Databricks identity / ephemeral tokens)

Lakebase offers two ways to authenticate: with OAuth tokens from your Databricks identity (recommended), or with native Postgres roles with a password. The connection is standard Postgres (port 5432, default database databricks_postgres), but the password is an ephemeral token.

The Databricks identity generates an OAuth token (≈1 h) used as the password for Postgres over SSL; the user is a Postgres role that you must create and map yourself.

Generate the token and connect

# Databricks CLI (v0.256.0+)
databricks database generate-database-credential \
  --request-id $(uuidgen) \
  --json '{"instance_names": ["mi-instancia"]}'
# → returns { "token": "...", "expiration_time": "..." }

# Connect with psql (the password is the token; SSL required)
export PGPASSWORD='<OAuth token>'
psql "host=<HOST> port=5432 dbname=databricks_postgres user=<rol_pg> sslmode=require"
# Python SDK (v0.56.0+) — useful for refreshing the token in a pool
from databricks.sdk import WorkspaceClient
import uuid

w = WorkspaceClient()  # M2M: WorkspaceClient(host=..., client_id=..., client_secret=...)
cred = w.database.generate_database_credential(
    request_id=str(uuid.uuid4()),
    instance_names=["mi-instancia"],
)
# use cred.token as PGPASSWORD; check expiration_time and refresh before opening new connections
  • Ephemeral tokens (~1 h), workspace scope: they don't work across workspaces. Expiration applies only at login; an already-open connection keeps working, but any re-authentication with an expired token fails.
  • M2M for apps: use a service principal with OAuth credentials and mint tokens with the same flow.
  • Identity mapping: Databricks and Postgres are separate systems without automatic synchronization; create the corresponding Postgres role. For groups, any member enters as the group's role.
  • Refresh in pools: check expiration_time on each use or have a thread that periodically refreshes the token before opening new connections.

9. ✅ Best practices and tuning in the managed environment

The engine is Postgres, but the separated compute/storage architecture and the managed mode change where your lever is. Recommendations (📗 = documented/derived from sources; 🧭 = general best practice for this architecture):

Connections and authentication

  • 🧭 Use a connection pooler (PgBouncer or your app/driver pool). Each backend is a process; opening/closing connections per request is expensive and, with 1 h tokens, it multiplies the logins. Keep long connections and refresh the token before opening new ones.
  • 📗 SSL always (it's required with a token). Prefer Databricks identity over native roles; reserve native roles for clients that can't rotate credentials.
  • 📗 M2M with service principals for apps/services; don't embed native-role passwords if you can avoid it.

Modeling and OLTP performance

  • 🧭 It's an OLTP database: design it for short transactions and many accesses by key. Index for your access patterns (the PK and the most frequent filters); use EXPLAIN (ANALYZE, BUFFERS) to validate plans (Part 2.2).
  • 🧭 Don't use it for heavy analytics: massive scans and large aggregations belong to the lakehouse. Bring to Lakebase only what you serve operationally (via synced tables).
  • 🧭 Take advantage of the read caches (RAM → NVMe/LFC → pageserver): patterns with good locality avoid going to the pageserver over the network. A just-awakened compute (scale-to-zero) has a cold cache: expect higher latencies on the first queries.
  • 📗 The COMMIT depends on the network (safekeeper quorum), not the local disk: group writes into reasonable transactions rather than thousands of micro-commits.

Maintenance (MVCC / autovacuum)

  • 🧭 Autovacuum remains critical: MVCC bloat and the risk of XID wraparound exist the same. Watch hot tables (pg_stat_user_tables: n_dead_tup, last_autovacuum) and age(relfrozenxid) (Parts 2.3 and 3.4).
  • 🧭 On very active tables, tune autovacuum per table if the platform allows it (ALTER TABLE … SET (autovacuum_vacuum_scale_factor=…)); remember that many global GUCs are managed and not yours.
  • 🧭 Avoid long idle-in-transaction transactions and slots/queries that hold the global xmin: they block vacuum cleanup.

Dev/test, deployments, and data

  • 📗 Use branching for instant dev/test environments, to validate schema migrations, and for agent/AI testing on real data without touching production or adding load to it.
  • 📗 Choose the synced-table mode by freshness/cost: snapshot (high churn), triggered (near-real, on demand), continuous (minimal lag). The synced tables are read-only: don't write to them.
  • 📗 Backups/PITR are platform-level (branching/restore by LSN), not archive_command. Know your history window.

What you should NOT expect (vs your own Postgres)

  • 📗 No full superuser, no filesystem access, no tablespaces; extensions limited to the supported set (~52).
  • 📗 You don't reconfigure full_page_writes (disabled by design), the checkpointer, or shared_buffers like on your own server.
Accuracy note: the connection/authentication (📗) is confirmed against the cited documentation; the tuning recommendations marked 🧭 are best practices for this architecture — validate the available limits and GUCs against the current documentation for your instance.

PART 5 — Lakebase in the Databricks source code 🦀

Track D · Vision & deep internals · 🔴 Advanced / reference · 🏁 last in the learning path

0. The real repos (verified against the Databricks code)

This part is verified against the private Databricks repositories (github.com/databricks-eng/…), not against public Neon. Lakebase doesn't live in a single place: it is spread across four repos. The proprietary code is private; the class/file names here are copied from the real sources.

RepoWhat it isLanguage
databricks-eng/hadronThe engine: Neon fork (neon-main branch) — pageserver, safekeeper, proxy + Postgres extensions and proprietary compute services.Rust + C
databricks-eng/neon-cloudThe control plane (LBM): the "Lakebase Manager" and the "Megabase" platform + Helm.Go + Helm
databricks-eng/neon-autoscalingAutoscaling: scales the Postgres micro-VMs with live migration.Go (+ Rust)
databricks-eng/universeDeployment: brickstore/lakebase-manager/ runs and configures the LBM binary (does not compile it).Jsonnet + Proto

The engine (hadron) registers its pageservers/safekeepers with the LBM (neon-cloud), which is deployed from universe; neon-autoscaling scales the compute VMs.

Convention: the names within code are copied from the real sources. The naming evolves; treat them as a reference, not as a stable API. Where the purpose is inferred from file names (not from comments), it is indicated.

1. The engine: hadron (Neon fork, neon-main branch)

The hadron README is literally Neon's: "separates storage and compute… Pageserver + Safekeepers". It mixes Rust (storage) and C (the extension loaded into the compute's Postgres). Modules (real directories in hadron/):

  • pgxn/neon/ — C extension in the compute's Postgres (smgr, LFC, communicator, walproposer).
  • pageserver/ — Rust: materializes and serves pages (layers, WAL ingest, compaction, GC).
  • safekeeper/ — Rust: WAL durability by consensus.
  • proxy/, proxy-cplane-api/ — Rust: connection proxy and its control plane API.
  • storage_broker/, storage_controller/, storage_scrubber/ — pub/sub, attach/sharding, S3 cleanup.
  • Databricks additions: compute_manager/ (provisioning/billing/routing of the compute), endpoint_controller/ + endpoint_storage/ (per-endpoint orchestration), compute_tools/ (compute_ctl).
  • libs/ — shared crates: pageserver_api, safekeeper_api, postgres_ffi, remote_storage, vm_monitor, lakebase-replicator-lib.

The compute (C) emits WAL to the safekeeper (consensus) and requests pages from the pageserver; the pageserver ingests that WAL, materializes it into layers, and uploads them to S3. storage_broker connects safekeepers and pageservers.

2. The neon extension in the compute (C · pgxn/neon/)

The compute is real Postgres, but it loads the neon extension that replaces the storage layer without touching the SQL engine.

The Storage Manager: pagestore_smgr.c

Postgres accesses the disk through an API called smgr. Neon implements that API against the pageserver instead of the filesystem. Real functions:

  • neon_read() / neon_readv() / neon_read_at_lsn() — read blocks (at a specific LSN).
  • neon_write() / neon_writev() and neon_wallog_page() — when evicting a dirty page, it ensures its WAL is recorded (it doesn't "flush the page" to a local file).
  • neon_nblocks(), neon_exists(), neon_extend(), neon_truncate(), neon_create(), neon_unlink(), neon_prefetch().
  • Dispatch by persistence: according to reln->smgr_relpersistence, temporary/unlogged tables go to md.c (local disk) and permanent ones to the pageserver via the communicator.
  • Index build without WAL: neon_start_unlogged_build() → neon_finish_unlogged_build_phase_1() → neon_end_unlogged_build() (avoids the cost of WAL-logging during the build and uploads the result at the end).
  • REDO optimization: neon_redo_read_buffer_filter() decides whether a block needs redo (by checking whether it's in shared buffers or in the local cache).

LSN tracking and the read path

neon_get_request_lsns() computes the request LSNs in the neon_request_lsns struct (request_lsn, not_modified_since, effective_request_lsn): in recovery it requests the page at the replay LSN; in normal operation, the latest version. The path of a read is:

  1. communicator_prefetch_lookupv() — was it prefetched? → use it.
  2. lfc_read() / lfc_readv_select() — Local File Cache on NVMe → avoids the network.
  3. communicator_read_at_lsnv() — if everything above fails, it requests the page from the pageserver at the computed LSN.

The Local File Cache: file_cache.c

The LFC is the compute's second cache tier: a file (or raw block device) on local NVMe that holds 8 KB pages below shared_buffers (RAM) and above the pageserver (network). Because the compute is stateless, losing the LFC only costs a cold cache — never data; every page is still reconstructable from the WAL history in storage.

  • Granularity — chunks, not single pages. A FileCacheEntry covers lfc_blocks_per_chunk blocks × 8 KB, keyed by BufferTag in a shared-memory hash table, with 2 bits of state per block (GET_STATE/SET_STATE over the state[] flex array; the FileCacheBlockState enum is UNAVAILABLE → AVAILABLE → PENDING → REQUESTED). The chunk size is set by neon.file_cache_chunk_size — default 16 blocks = 128 KB, up to MAX_BLOCKS_PER_CHUNK = 128 (1 MB). Coarse chunks keep the index small (the header notes a page-granular map of an 8 TB DB would need ~40 GB; 1 MB chunks cut that to ~320 MB) and improve seqscan locality.
  • Partitioned by hash. The control area is an array of FileCacheControlPartition parts[] (neon.lfc_partitions), each with its own HTAB + LWLock, chosen by LFC_PART_NO(hash) = hash & (lfc_partitions-1) — so concurrent backends contend per-partition, not on one global lock.
  • Eviction — LRU per partition. Each partition keeps a doubly-linked LRU list (lfc_ctl->lru); lfc_init_new_entry() reuses a hole if one exists, else allocates within the limit, else evicts the least-recently-used unpinned chunk (pinned chunks are exempt).
  • Read path — lfc_readv_select() locks the partition, waits on condition variables for any PENDING/REQUESTED blocks, issues one pg_preadv(), and returns the hit count; a miss falls through to communicator_read_at_lsnv() (pageserver). Writes go through lfc_writev() (pg_pwritev()); prefetch results land via lfc_prefetch() (single pg_pwrite()).
  • Live resize (SIGHUP). neon.file_cache_size_limit is the soft working limit and can change at runtime — the vm-monitor resizes the LFC as the VM grows/shrinks (Part 4 §7.1). Shrinking uses fallocate(FALLOC_FL_PUNCH_HOLE) to hand disk back without changing the file's nominal size; freed regions are tracked as a holes list (lfc_ctl->holes) and reused before the file is extended. neon.max_file_cache_size (postmaster-level) is the hard ceiling — the soft limit may not exceed it; neon.file_cache_path selects the backing file/device.
  • Working-set estimation (feeds autoscaling). Every read/write calls wss_track_page(&working_set->wss, &tag), feeding a HyperLogLog sketch (hll.h); lfc_approximate_working_set_size_seconds(duration, reset) returns the approximate count of distinct pages touched in the last duration seconds (with 60 rolling per-minute windows). This is the working-set signal the autoscaler-agent polls (~every 20 s) to size RAM (Part 4 §7.1). On replica promotion the primary's sketch is transferred via get_hll_state/replace_hll (bytea).
  • Prewarm / tiered cache (Databricks additions). lfc_get_state(max_pages) snapshots the hottest chunks into a bounded structure so a fresh compute can prewarm the LFC after a wake/scale event, turning the Part 4 §7.1 cold start into a warm one faster (neon.prewarm_update_ws_estimation controls whether prewarmed pages count toward the working set). Hadron also adds a tiered-cache mode (enable_tiered_cache, neon.tiered_cache_max_pending_lfc_writes) that treats the LFC as an eviction target for clean buffers, tracked separately as read_evicted_pages.
  • Observability. lfc_get_stats() exposes hits/misses/writes/used/used_pages/evicted_pages/limit/pinned (plus hadron's redo_evicted_pages/read_evicted_pages); lfc_local_cache_pages() lists per-page records (relation, db, block, access count) — the backing data for the neon.neon_local_cache / LFC hit-ratio views.

3. The page service protocol

The communicator talks to the pageserver via a message protocol defined in pagestore_client.h. The message tag is the NeonMessageTag enum (requests 0–99, responses 100–199). Real types:

RequestResponseWhat for
NeonExistsRequestNeonExistsResponsedoes the relation exist?
NeonNblocksRequestNeonNblocksResponsenumber of blocks of the relation
NeonGetPageRequestNeonGetPageResponseget a page
NeonDbSizeRequestNeonDbSizeResponsesize of the database
NeonGetSlruSegmentRequestNeonGetSlruSegmentResponseSLRU segment (CLOG, etc.)

The requests inherit from NeonRequest and the responses from NeonResponse (over the base type NeonMessage with tag, request id, and LSN). The NeonGetPageRequest carries { NeonRequest hdr; NRelFileInfo rinfo; ForkNumber forknum; BlockNumber blkno; } — that is, it identifies a page by (relation, fork, block) and an LSN. On the Rust side, in the pageserver_api crate, the equivalent messages of the pagestream flow are PagestreamGetPageRequest, PagestreamExistsRequest, PagestreamNblocksRequest, etc., served by the pageserver's page service.

4. Safekeeper + walproposer (the consensus & WAL high availability)

The durability tier is a Paxos-style proposer/acceptor protocol split between the proposer (the leader, running as the walproposer background worker inside the compute, C) and the acceptors (the safekeepers, Rust). A timeline is replicated across a set of safekeepers (typically 3); the WAL is durable the instant a majority has it — this replaces the local fsync of stock Postgres.

The proposer: walproposer.c / walproposer.h (compute)

  • Central struct WalProposer (the protocol state machine) and Safekeeper (one connection descriptor per acceptor); WalProposerConfig.
  • Functions: WalProposerCreate(), WalProposerStart(), WalProposerBroadcast(), WalProposerPoll().
  • proposer→acceptor messages: ProposerGreeting, VoteRequest, ProposerElected, AppendRequestHeader.
  • acceptor→proposer messages: AcceptorGreeting, VoteResponse (with flush_lsn and term history), AppendResponse (persistence feedback + pageserver state).
  • Terms/epochs: term_t (uint64), propTerm, propTermHistory, propTermStartLsn, donorLastLogTerm.
  • Quorum = (n_safekeepers / 2) + 1 (2 of 3). The proposer computes commit_lsn as the WAL position the majority has flushed. Connection states in the SafekeeperState enum (SS_OFFLINE … SS_ACTIVE).

The acceptor: safekeeper/src/ (Rust)

  • SafeKeeper (safekeeper.rs) is the acceptor; AcceptorState { term, wal_proposer_id, term_history } (state.rs) is its persistent consensus state — term = the highest term it has voted for (the Paxos "promise"). Message enums ProposerAcceptorMessage / AcceptorProposerMessage, dispatched by process_msg → handle_greeting / handle_vote_request / handle_elected / handle_append_request.
  • Election: handle_vote_request grants a vote only if acceptor_state.term < msg.term, and persists the new term (fsync) before replying — so a rebooted safekeeper never double-votes for a term (enforced by the test_voting unit test).
  • Single WAL line (no split-brain): TermHistory (a Vec<TermLsn>) records where each term begins. find_highest_common_point refuses to adopt a proposer's history if the same index has a different LSN, and handle_elected refuses to truncate below commit_lsn. An acceptor accepts AppendRequests only in its current term; a stale proposer gets a term_only AppendResponse that forces it to re-elect. Since each new proposer must win a majority (bumping the term and truncating to the common point), only one WAL line ever commits. TLA+ specs live under safekeeper/spec/.

High availability — how the WAL tier survives failures

The proposer commits with any majority; a dead/partitioned safekeeper is tolerated and later backfilled from a peer. Safekeepers exchange only status via the storage broker, never consensus WAL directly.

  • A safekeeper dies or is partitioned: the proposer keeps committing as long as it still has a majority (2 of 3 tolerates losing one). It stops advancing the dead node's ack position. Safekeepers never talk directly for consensus — they only exchange status through the storage_broker (broker.rs, SafekeeperTimelineInfo pushed once/second).
  • Reconnect races self-heal: a late packet from a previous compute connection produces a typed AppendRewriteError (info-level), turned into a clean connection reset (receive_wal.rs; network read and the disk-write WalAcceptor task run split via tokio::select!).
  • Catch-up / backfill when a safekeeper rejoins: recovery.rs (recovery_main → recovery_needed) picks a donor — a peer strictly ahead on (last_log_term, flush_lsn) — but only when there is no live streaming compute (get_num_streaming() == 0). It then handshakes (HTTP TimelineStatus, a local VoteRequest, find_highest_common_point) and opens a Postgres physical replication stream (START_REPLICATION PHYSICAL <lsn> (term=…)) to backfill.
  • Dynamic membership (add/remove a safekeeper): RFC 035. A Configuration { generation, members, new_members } (safekeeper_api/membership.rs) uses generation numbers and Raft-style joint configurations — while new_members is set, a commit needs a majority in both sets. Every consensus message carries the generation and is rejected on mismatch; the storage controller coordinates the two-phase switch (pull_timeline copies the timeline → bump term → wait for the new majority → commit the final config).
  • WAL removal / S3 backup: a safekeeper only deletes WAL below calc_horizon_lsn (remove_wal.rs) = min(remote_consistent_lsn, backup_lsn, commit_lsn, flush_lsn, …) — after the pageserver consumed it and it's backed up. One node is elected offloader (wal_backup.rs::determine_offloader) to upload WAL to S3.
🧱 Databricks (hadron) specifics. safekeeper/src/hadron.rs adds registration with the HCC (Hadron Cluster Coordinator) for the v1/HCM control path, a global-disk-usage limiter (GLOBAL_DISK_LIMIT_EXCEEDED) that stops accepting WAL when the volume is full, hadron_determine_offloader (re-elects the offloader if a node lags too far), and CMK/fscrypt encryption fields (dbx_workspace_id, dbx_encryption_enabled). The core protocol, TermHistory, quorum semantics, broker gossip and RFC-035 membership are inherited unchanged from Neon.

5. Pageserver: the layer engine (Rust)

It turns the WAL into pages. Central concept: the 2D space of (Key × LSN), where Key addresses a page (relation + block) and Lsn the point in the WAL.

Layer types (pageserver/src/tenant/storage_layer/)

  • ImageLayer — "a snapshot of all the keys in a range, at a single LSN" (end_lsn = lsn+1). Immutable.
  • DeltaLayer — "WAL records/page images over a key range × an LSN range". Immutable. An L0 delta spans the whole key space; L1 deltas span sub-ranges (the level is implicit from key coverage, not stored).
  • InMemoryLayer — the open layer where recent WAL accumulates (backed by an EphemeralFile). Open → Frozen → written out as a DeltaLayer.
  • LayerMap (layer_map.rs) — the per-Timeline index: open_layer + frozen_layers: VecDeque + historic (a BufferedHistoricLayerCoverage interval structure over the on-disk layers, not a plain Vec — optimized for "which layer covers this Key at this LSN?").

Reconstruction of a page

On Timeline::get(key, lsn) / get_vectored: the LayerMap locates the most recent ImageLayer ≤ L and applies the DeltaLayer/InMemoryLayer WAL up to L via reconstruct_value → walredo_mgr.request_redo(...). WAL redo runs in a sandboxed postgres --wal-redo process, one per tenant (PostgresRedoManager, seccomp, stdin/stdout). If the Timeline is a branch, the read recurses into the parent timeline below the branch LSN (get_ready_ancestor_timeline) — this is how branches, read replicas and PITR read shared history from one primitive. A missing base image raises MissingKeyError.

The LayerMap locates the base ImageLayer and applies the deltas to it up to the requested LSN. The cold layers are uploaded to S3; the hot ones live on the pageserver's NVMe.

WAL ingest, compaction, and GC

  • Ingest: WalReceiver keeps exactly one active safekeeper streaming connection (chosen via the broker); WalIngest / DatadirModification decode records into the open InMemoryLayer. DatadirModification is a staging buffer whose commit() flushes a batch and advances the visibility LSN.
  • Freeze → flush: the open layer rolls when it reaches checkpoint_distance (default 256 MB; scaled by shard count when sharded) → becomes a frozen layer → flush_loop writes an L0 DeltaLayer and RemoteTimelineClient uploads it to S3 (remote_storage).
  • Compaction (compaction_loop, ~every 20 s): L0→L1 merge-sorts ~10–20 L0 deltas into sorted L1 deltas (compaction_target_size 128 MB); L1 image compaction materializes a new ImageLayer where ≥ 3 deltas overlap. This image-generation pushdown replaces Full Page Writes. A per-tenant circuit breaker disables compaction for 24 h after 5 failures.
  • Garbage collection (gc_timeline): removes superseded layers below the retention horizon. GcCutoffs { space, time } — space from gc_horizon (~64 MB), time from pitr_interval; applied_gc_cutoff_lsn is the enforced floor. GC also honors retain_lsns (child branch points), LSN leases, and [hadron] standby-horizon leases that hold GC back for lagging read replicas.
  • Remote state: IndexPart (index_part.json) is the authoritative manifest of a timeline's S3 files. Uploads are strictly ordered (layers before the index that names them) and every layer PUT asserts its generation matches the attachment (§5.1).

5.1. Pageserver high availability & failover

A pageserver is not just storage — it's a cache tier that reconstructs pages, so "HA" means: keep a warm copy of the working set elsewhere, and make failover safe against split-brain on S3. Three mechanisms do it:

On failure the controller promotes a warm secondary to attached with an incremented generation; the stale generation on the dead node can no longer corrupt S3.

  • Attach + generations (the split-brain guard). Each attachment holds a Generation (Valid(u32)), a monotonic fencing token. Every layer written to S3 carries a generation suffix; on startup/reattach the pageserver calls the controller's re-attach and gets a freshly incremented generation, and before deleting/trusting remote objects it calls validate — a superseded generation returns false. So a zombie pageserver writes under a stale suffix and can never corrupt current state. Deletions run through a generation-validated deletion queue. AttachmentMode::{Single, Multi, Stale} controls whether a location may delete layers (only Single may).
  • Secondary locations (warm standby). A SecondaryTenant keeps a warm on-disk cache of layers without serving reads or ingesting WAL. The attached pageserver publishes a heatmap (its hottest layers) to S3; the secondary pre-fetches those layers, so a promotion doesn't start cold. [hadron] Hot secondary (HotSecondaryTenant, WriteMode::HotSecondary) goes further: it ingests WAL and serves reads but never writes S3, enabling near-instant in-place promotion.
  • Failover. The Heartbeater polls every node (~3 s); a node flips Offline only after ~10 s of silence (and a partition-suspicion guard suppresses mass failover if > 50 % of nodes look offline). On ToOffline the controller reschedules the dead node's attached shards, promotes their secondaries (generation++), the Reconciler writes the new location_config, and the compute is notified (notify-attach) so Postgres reconnects.

5.2. Sharding: scaling one tenant across pageservers

A large tenant is split into shards, each an independent Timeline on a (potentially) different pageserver, so ingest and storage scale horizontally (libs/pageserver_api/src/shard.rs, RFCs 031/032/041):

  • Identity: ShardIdentity { number, count, stripe_size, layout }; TenantShardId = { tenant_id, shard_number, shard_count } (an unsharded tenant renders as the bare tenant id). Default stripe = 16 MiB (2048 × 8 KB pages).
  • Key → shard: key_to_shard_number() — shard 0 holds SLRU/aux/dir + relation-size keys; ordinary relation block pages are distributed by murmurhash32(relNode) combined with murmurhash32(blockNum / stripe_size) % count. Dividing the block number by the stripe size preserves locality.
  • Compute routing: the compute learns the shard→node map and stripe size from the controller's notify-attach hook (neon.pageserver_connstring, neon.shard_stripe_size) and sends each GetPage@LSN to the shard whose is_key_local(key) is true.
  • Splitting: shard_split doubles the shard count, hard-linking local layers for reuse.

5.3. The storage controller (storage_controller/)

The brain of the storage tier — it maps tenant shards → pageserver placements and drives all of the above through an intent → observed → reconcile loop, persisting to its own Postgres (via diesel):

  • Scheduler scores candidate nodes and is AZ-aware: it prefers the same AZ for the attached location but a different AZ for the secondary (so a zone failure can't take both). AffinityScore spreads a tenant's shards across nodes.
  • Reconciler converges one shard from its IntentState { attached, secondary[] } to reality: increment generation when needed → attach the primary → push secondaries → detach extras → compute_notify().
  • Node availability (Active/Offline/WarmingUp) is deliberately not persisted — every node loads as Offline until a heartbeat proves otherwise. What is persisted is the tenant-shard table including the generation numbers — that DB must be durable because those generations keep S3 data safe.
  • [hadron] additions: a whole hadron_* suite (hadron_pageserver_manager, hadron_dns, hadron_drain_and_fill, pageserver_autoscaler, pageserver_auto_shard_migrator, node-pool v1/v2, the Vplus migration orchestrator, empty_local_disk local-disk-loss recovery, and an operator override to pause offline transitions).

6. End-to-end flow with classes

WRITE (commit)
  Postgres backend → generates WAL
  → walproposer.c (WalProposer) proposes via AppendRequest
  → safekeepers (SafeKeeper/AcceptorState): quorum (n/2)+1 → ack
  → COMMIT durable                                  (NO local fsync)
  → storage_broker announces; pageserver (WalReceiver) consumes the WAL
  → WalIngest/DatadirModification → InMemoryLayer (Open)
  → when it fills up → DeltaLayer → RemoteTimelineClient → S3

READ (get page)
  backend → smgr neon_read() (pagestore_smgr.c)
  → communicator_prefetch_lookupv()  (prefetch?)
  → lfc_read()                        (LFC on NVMe)
  → communicator_read_at_lsnv() → NeonGetPageRequest → page service
  → Timeline (pageserver): LayerMap locates ImageLayer + DeltaLayers
  → wal-redo applies deltas up to the LSN → NeonGetPageResponse → page

7. Branching in the code (timelines)

A branch is a new Timeline that points at a parent timeline at a branch LSN. The branch's LayerMap contains only its own layers (the deltas above the branch LSN); to read below that point, the code recurses to the parent timeline (as the docs say: "recurse to look into the parent branch"). That's why:

  • Creating a branch is instant (metadata: ancestor + branch LSN), without copying layers.
  • Writing to the branch creates new layers in its timeline; the parent isn't touched.
  • PITR = creating/positioning a timeline at a past Lsn within the retention window (what the GC hasn't yet removed).
Accuracy note: the names of pgxn/neon/*.c (smgr, LFC, communicator), of the walproposer and of the Neon*Request/Response protocol, and of the ImageLayer/DeltaLayer/InMemoryLayer/LayerMap/Timeline layers are verified in the code (public Neon and the Databricks hadron fork). Some details of the reconstruction (the value reconstruct structure, the wal-redo process) are described by behavior and may vary between versions.

8. Databricks authentication inside Postgres — hadron/pgxn/databricks_auth/

This C extension implements, inside the compute's Postgres, the authentication with Databricks/OAuth identity that we saw in Part 4.8. It's the real connection between the "ephemeral token as a password" and the engine.

  • Struct DatabricksIdentity (databricks_auth.h): the validated identity (user + groups).
  • GUCs (databricks_auth.h): session_token, workspace_url, lakebase_manager_url, databricks_endpoint_id, databricks_instance_id, databricks_compute_id, databricks_branch_id, dbx_enable_pg_native_login, disable_pat_login, enable_sql_restrictions, scim_cache_ttl_s.
  • Token validation — token_auth.c: load_token_verification_keys(), isOAuthToken() / isInternalToken(), get_key_provider(), validate_internal_token(), log_internal_token_failure(). (The ~1 h OAuth token is validated here, at login.)
  • pat_auth.c — login by Personal Access Token. pctx_auth.c — signed principal context (pctx_signing_public_key_file).
  • role_creation.c / user_management.c — create Postgres roles for Databricks identities (this is the "no automatic sync" of Part 4.8). online_table_permissions.c — permissions for online/synced tables. sql_restrictions.c — SQL restrictions. workspace_api_handler.c — calls to the workspace API.
  • Auditing: hadron/pgxn/pgaudit_lakebase/pgaudit_lakebase.c — emit_log_hook hook that relays the audit log over TCP/AF_VSOCK to a pgaudit_receiver in the pod.

9. compute_manager and registration with the LBM — hadron/compute_manager/ (Rust)

A gRPC (tonic) + Diesel + Kubernetes service that provisions, reconciles, bills, and routes the Postgres compute nodes.

  • Service, ServiceContext — compute_manager/src/service.rs.
  • Compute, SchedulingState, MigrationState — compute_manager/src/compute.rs; Instance — instance.rs.
  • LakebaseTokenGenerator — compute_manager/src/lakebase_token.rs: generates EdDSA JWTs to authenticate against the Storage Controller (generate_admin_token(), generate_pageserver_api_token()).
  • Modules (lib.rs): billing, compute_ctl_client, compute_pool, reconcilers, storage_controller_adapter, persistence, pg_compute_routing_helper.
  • Registration with the LBM: storage_controller/src/api_clients/lakebase_manager_client.rs — LakebaseManagerClient, LbmPageserverRegisterRequest, LbmSafekeeperRegisterRequest: the storage controller registers the pageservers/safekeepers with the Lakebase Manager.

10. Forward ETL / CDF — the wal2delta extension (code & modules)

The engine that materializes Postgres changes toward Delta (the Postgres→lakehouse direction — this is forward ETL / Lakebase CDF, the same engine whose end-to-end flow is in §17, not reverse ETL) is the wal2delta extension (Rust/pgrx):

  • DataplaneBridgeClient — wal2delta/src/grpc_client.rs: gRPC client (tonic) toward the Databricks "dataplane bridge" (proto o11y/exporter/proto/lakebase_dataplane_bridge.proto).
  • RowAppender, DeltaTableTransaction, the Committer trait with BridgeCommitter and DirectStorageCommitter, and append_cdc_metadata() — wal2delta/src/delta_writer.rs: builds Delta rows with CDC metadata (change_type, LSN, xid).
  • Modules (src/lib.rs): bgworker.rs (background worker), executor.rs, initial_snapshot.rs and resnapshot.rs (the snapshot and re-snapshot logic), schema_extractor.rs, tuple_extractor.rs. SQL function wal2delta_write_changes() (functions.rs, #[pg_extern]).
  • hadron/pgxn/reverse_etl_worker/ (C) — belongs to the opposite direction (reverse ETL / synced tables, UC→Lakebase, §18): the Direct-to-Storage bulk worker (_PG_init, retl_checkpoint_flush_all, retl_heapam.c). Listed here only because it shares the pgxn/ tree — it is not part of wal2delta.
Verified by code symbols (DataplaneBridgeClient, append_cdc_metadata, DeltaTableTransaction); the exact detail of how it maps to the snapshot/triggered/continuous modes of Part 4.5 comes from the logic of initial_snapshot.rs/resnapshot.rs and from the control plane layer.

11. Lakebase-specific extensions — hadron/pgxn/

  • lakebase_vector/ (Rust/pgrx, src/lib.rs) — vector/ANN index (requires = 'vector'): supports embedding searches.
  • lakebase_text/ (Rust/pgrx) — full-text search with a BM25 index (src/index/lakebase_bm25/).
  • lakebase_tokenizer/ (Rust/pgrx) — tokenizer for FTS.
  • neon_walredo/ — the wal-redo process (applies WAL when reconstructing pages, section 5.5); neon_rmgr/, neon_monitor/, neon_procstat/, neon_utils/ — engine utilities.

12. The control plane (LBM): neon-cloud (Go) + deployment in universe

The Lakebase Manager (LBM) — pointed to by the lakebase_manager_url GUC and the LakebaseManagerClient — is built in neon-cloud and deployed from universe:

  • Code (Go) — neon-cloud/goapp/controlplane/: a single binary with a dual -api / -worker role (cmd_main.go, cmd_run_api.go, cmd_run_worker.go). API in internal/app/: publicapiv2, managementapiv2, computeapiv1, storageapiv1, generationsapiv1, eventsourcing. Worker jobs: hafailoverjob, operationsjob, computemonitorjob, poolfilljob, snapshotcreatejob, billingmetricsexporter.
  • "Megabase" platform (project/branch metadata) — neon-cloud/platform/cmd/{controlplane,storage,computeplatform,aigateway}; platformapi.Client client (CreateBranch/GetBranch/ListBranches/DeleteBranch). ← this is where branching lives as a service.
  • Helm — neon-cloud/helm/charts/neon-control-plane/ deploys the LBM (api.statefulset.yaml, worker.deployment.yaml, migrate.job.yaml).
  • Deployment (universe) — universe/brickstore/lakebase-manager/ runs the controlplane binary (does not compile it). gRPC contract: api/proto/service.proto → LakebaseManagerService (package databricks.lakebasemanager). Jsonnet defs: lakebase-manager-api-def.jsonnet, lakebase-manager-worker-def.jsonnet, lakebase-manager-migrations-def.jsonnet. Test fakes (Scala): LakebaseManagerFakePlugin, ProxyApiServiceFakePlugin.

13. Autoscaling — neon-autoscaling (Go)

It scales the CPU/RAM of the Postgres micro-VMs (NeonVM / QEMU on K8s) vertically with live migration, without dropping TCP connections. It underpins the scale-to-zero/autoscaling of Part 4.

  • autoscaler-agent: MainRunner (pkg/agent/entrypoint.go), Runner (runner.go), Dispatcher (dispatcher.go); the pure state machine State / NextActions() in pkg/agent/core/state.go; ExecutorCore in pkg/agent/executor/core.go.
  • autoscale-scheduler: AutoscaleEnforcer (K8s scheduler plugin) in pkg/plugin/framework_methods.go (PreFilter/Filter/Score/Reserve); Node/Pod/XactMap state.
  • neonvm-controller: CRDs VirtualMachine, VirtualMachineSpec/Status, Guest (neonvm/apis/neonvm/v1/virtualmachine_types.go), VirtualMachineMigration, IPPool; reconcilers VMReconciler, VirtualMachineMigrationReconciler.
  • The scaling math (pkg/scalingpolicy): EstimateTrueWorkingSetSizeV2 (lfc/v2.go) estimates the working set from LFC metrics, and DesiredCPUV3 (cpu/v3.go) computes the target CPU. pkg/reporting emits the billing events.
Cross-repo flow (verified): storage_controller (hadron) registers PS/SK with the LBM via LakebaseManagerClient → compute_manager (hadron) authenticates to the storage controller with LakebaseTokenGenerator → the LBM is built in neon-cloud and deployed from universe → neon-autoscaling scales the VMs and reports billing to the same pipeline.

14. The CreateBranch flow step by step (code from hadron)

Here we join the conceptual branching (Part 4.6 / §5.7) with the real verified code. A branch is a new Timeline that only stores a pointer to the ancestor + the branch LSN; the two figures show (a) the timeline model over the LSN axis and (b) the call chain that creates that timeline.

14.1 Timeline model and the PITR window

The branch starts from the parent at the branch LSN; below it shares layers (copy-on-write), above it accumulates deltas. You can't branch before the applied_gc_cutoff_lsn (what the GC has already deleted).

14.2 The call chain (from the API to the live Timeline)

From CreateBranch (control plane) to the live Timeline in the pageserver. Step 6 is where the branch is really born.

14.3 What branch_timeline_impl does (verified, tenant.rs)

  1. Takes the GC lock (self.gc_cs.lock()): so the garbage collector can't delete the ancestor's LSN while the branch is being created.
  2. If no start_lsn was passed, it branches from src_timeline.get_last_record_lsn() (the parent's last point).
  3. Idempotency: start_creating_timeline(dst_id, CreateTimelineIdempotency::Branch { ancestor_timeline_id, ancestor_start_lsn }) — a retry returns the same timeline instead of duplicating it.
  4. Validates the PITR window: checks that start_lsn is within the retention horizon (applied_gc_cutoff_lsn and the planned cutoff in gc_info). Branching further back → error.
  5. Creates the metadata with the ancestor: TimelineMetadata::new(...) ("noting the ancestor of the new timeline"). Here is the copy-on-write: no data is copied, only the ancestor + LSN link is recorded.
  6. prepare_new_timeline(new_metadata, start_lsn, ancestor = Some(src), …) → create_timeline_struct(…, ancestor, …) → UninitializedTimeline → finish_creation() leaves the Timeline live.

Sharding detail: the storage controller only sends start_lsn=None to shard 0; the rest receive the explicit LSN, so that all the shards branch at the same ancestor LSN (otherwise, corruption). And control_plane/src/branch_mappings.rs::get_branch_timeline_id translates branch names ↔ TimelineId.

Verified by reading hadron: timeline_create_handler (pageserver/src/http/routes.rs), create_timeline and branch_timeline_impl (pageserver/src/tenant.rs), with TimelineCreateRequestMode::Branch, CreateTimelineParamsBranch, CreateTimelineIdempotency::Branch and TimelineMetadata::new. The exact name of the client RPC (CreateBranch) and its route through the control plane come from neon-cloud/storage_controller.

15. Reading a page from a branch: Timeline::get + recursion to the ancestor

This is the "below the branch LSN you recurse to the parent" of §5.7, now in code. The entry point is Timeline::get(key, lsn) (single key) or, on the real path, get_vectored → get_vectored_impl → get_vectored_reconstruct_data (all in pageserver/src/tenant/timeline.rs).

A loop walks the ancestor chain: each timeline contributes what it has in its layers; what's missing is sought in the parent, lowering the LSN to the branch point — until no keys remain.

What get_vectored_reconstruct_data does (verified, timeline.rs)

  1. Seeds the chain with a link to itself (AncestorLink::seed_for_read) so the loop is uniform.
  2. On each iteration, over timeline: it first reads ancestor_snapshot() (crucial order to avoid breaking on a concurrent detach-ancestor), then searches in its own layers (the read path traverses the LayerMap) accumulating base + deltas in ValuesReconstructState and marking completed_keyspace.
  3. query.remove_overlapping_with(&completed) — removes the keys already satisfied.
  4. If query.is_empty() → break None (everything resolved). If there's no ancestor and keys remain → break Some(missing) (missing key error).
  5. If keys remain and there's an ancestor → it descends (get_ready_ancestor_timeline), capping the LSN range at the branch point (the ancestor only contributes below its ancestor_lsn), and repeats.
  6. GC protection: the AncestorLink/GcRetentionGuard proves that the ancestor's layers at the branch LSN are protected from the GC during the read (correlate of the GC lock of §5.14).
  7. Finally, ValuesReconstructState (base image + ordered deltas) is handed to the wal-redo process to materialize the 8 KB page (§5.5).

16. compute_manager: how the endpoint is assigned to the branch

When a branch appears, a compute is needed to serve it. It's orchestrated by hadron/compute_manager/ through reconcilers (declarative loop: brings the real state to the desired one).

The InstanceReconciler ensures tenant + timeline (with ancestor = parent → branch); the ComputeReconciler brings up Postgres on K8s, equips it with an endpoint token, and leaves it ready.

  • Domain objects: Instance (instance.rs) = a logical compute (tenant_id + timeline_id); Compute, SchedulingState, MigrationState (compute.rs).
  • InstanceReconciler (reconcilers/instance_reconciler.rs): reconcile_created_instance → create_tenant (if new) → create_timeline, which for a branch sets ancestor_timeline_id = parent_instance.timeline_id and new_timeline_id = instance.timeline_id, with assign_safekeepers: true. This is what triggers the creation of the branch's timeline (§5.14).
  • ComputeReconciler (reconcilers/compute_reconciler.rs): provisions the compute in Kubernetes (Pod/ReplicaSet), generates an endpoint scope token (generate_tenant_endpoint_scope_token(compute.compute_id, …) — via the LakebaseTokenGenerator of §5.9), runs the readiness probe (all_containers_ready), and marks the compute healthy.
  • Other reconcilers: compute_pool_reconciler maintains pre-warmed pools (fast assignment and scale-to-zero wakeup), rollout_reconciler (deployments), health_reconciler (health). Routing is helped by pg_compute_routing_helper.
Verified by reading hadron/compute_manager/src/reconcilers/instance_reconciler.rs and compute_reconciler.rs (InstanceReconciler, create_timeline with ancestor_timeline_id, ComputeReconciler, generate_tenant_endpoint_scope_token, all_containers_ready).

17. Forward ETL / Lakebase CDF (Lakebase → UC) — wal2delta

It's the direction opposite to synced tables (§4.5): if synced tables bring data from UC into Lakebase, the Forward ETL (internally ZeroETL) publishes the changes of your Postgres tables back to the lakehouse as Delta tables managed in Unity Catalog. The user-facing product is the Lakebase Change Data Feed (CDF): it captures every INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE from the WAL and materializes it into lb_<table>_history, batched and flushed every ~15 s. It is implemented by the hadron/pgxn/wal2delta/ extension.

17.1 Components and how they interact

The output plugin runs in the walsender (producer); the bgworker consumes the slot over loopback and flushes every ~15 s; the bridge writes the Delta in UC. The control plane only configures — the hot dataflow doesn't pass through it.

17.2 The logical decoding output plugin — wal2delta.rs

It's a standard Postgres logical decoding output plugin. _PG_output_plugin_init registers the callbacks startup_cb → wal2delta_startup, begin_cb → wal2delta_begin_txn, change_cb → wal2delta_change, commit_cb → wal2delta_commit_txn. On each change (wal2delta_change): if the table doesn't have REPLICA IDENTITY FULL it skips it (register_skipped_table); it filters system namespaces; if the table is new, it extracts its schema and registers it with the control plane (register_table); and it buffers the change with its CDC metadata (append_change_to_buffer/append_cdc_metadata).

Why REPLICA IDENTITY FULL: by default Postgres only logs the PK on UPDATE/DELETE; with full it records the row before and after in the WAL — necessary to build the complete change history.

17.3 The background worker — bgworker.rs

Registered with BackgroundWorkerBuilder::new("wal2delta") running wal2delta_main (tokio runtime). Its loop: it connects to the local Postgres (loopback) (wal2delta.loopback_postgres_connstring), looks for the slot with plugin='wal2delta', runs START_REPLICATION SLOT {slot} LOGICAL 0/0 and consumes a ReplicationStream (it's a walsender client that receives the already-decoded stream). A force_commit_ticker = wal2delta.refresh_interval (default 15 s) forces the flush. On disable, it kills the walsender and removes the slot (pg_terminate_backend, pg_drop_replication_slot).

17.4 Writing to Delta, snapshot, and orchestration

  • Writing (delta_writer.rs): the Committer has two implementations — BridgeCommitter (gRPC to the lakebase-dataplane-bridge, which writes the managed Delta in UC) and DirectStorageCommitter (direct to object storage).
  • Initial snapshot (initial_snapshot.rs/resnapshot.rs): for tables with data, it copies the current state in batches (wal2delta.snapshot_batch_size, default 1,000,000) before the incremental capture.
  • Orchestration (universe/brickstore/scheduler/.../ForwardEtlHandler.scala): handleConfigureForwardEtl validates the feature, resolves the bridge in the workspace's DP region, builds ForwardEtlConfig (pg db/schema → uc catalog/schema) and calls bridgeClient.configureSchema(...). neon-cloud (operationsv2/.../spec.go): ensureWal2DeltaInSpec adds wal2delta to shared_preload_libraries; the keep_wal2delta GUC keeps it loaded on disable without restarting.
  • GUCs (gucs.rs): wal2delta.forward_etl_mode, dataplane_bridge_url, dataplane_bridge_token, refresh_interval (15), checkpoint_interval (200), snapshot_batch_size (1M), loopback_postgres_connstring, storage_version (3), alter_table_enabled, workspace_id.

17.5 Target table, requirements, and limits (official doc)

Target: lb_<table>_history in the chosen UC catalog/schema. System columns: _pg_change_type (insert/delete/update_preimage/update_postimage), _pg_lsn, _pg_xid, _timestamp, _sort_by.

  • Requirement: REPLICA IDENTITY FULL on the source tables.
  • Only tables in the databricks_postgres database; no partitioned tables; empty tables are skipped until they have ≥1 row; no target catalogs with default storage.
  • It's enabled by a workspace admin (preview) and configured at the schema level in the UI.
Accuracy note: the plugin callbacks, the bgworker loop, the GUCs, the ForwardEtlHandler flow, and the shape of the target table are verified (code from hadron/universe/neon-cloud + official CDF doc). The lakebase-dataplane-bridge service and its role come from the internal docs + the gRPC client. (Separately, Lakeflow Connect for Postgres→Delta is an emerging product path in Private Preview, complementary to this native mechanism.)

18. Reverse ETL / Synced tables (UC → Lakebase) — code

It's the opposite direction to §5.17: it brings data from Unity Catalog / Delta into a read-only Postgres table in Lakebase, to serve it to apps with low latency. The conceptual part (snapshot/triggered/continuous modes, CDF, PK, limits) is in §4.5; here is the how on the inside. Unlike Forward ETL (which lives in the compute), Reverse ETL is run by a Lakeflow Spark Declarative Pipeline in the data plane.

18.1 Components and how they interact

The control plane creates the pipeline; the Spark pipeline reads UC (+ CDF) and writes to Postgres by two paths: incremental SQL/COPY, or generation of PG pages uploaded to S3 for the bulk snapshot.

18.2 Orchestration (control plane) — SyncedTableHandler.scala

In universe/brickstore/scheduler/src/handlers/SyncedTableHandler.scala: handleCreateSyncedTable / handleCreateSyncedDatabaseTable create the synced table and the Lakeflow pipeline via OltpSinkPipelineSpec (proto com.databricks.api.proto.deltapipelines), with ResourceControlOptions for the resources. canUseAutoCdf(sourceTableInfo) decides whether it can use Auto CDF (requires row tracking and write CDF) or whether it directs the user to snapshot mode. It also handles Stop/Delete/Get of the pipeline and the table.

18.3 The Spark pipeline — spark/pipelines/execution/extensions/brickstore/

DatabaseTableSyncPipelineExecutionExtension (a data plane extension object) builds the graph with loadGraph(...) → DataflowGraph. OltpDataFrameTransformations converts the source (or its CDF) into row operations (upsert/delete). The target is an OLTP sink (OltpSink/OltpPgSink); the sink is shared infrastructure with an Engine enum (MySQL/TiDB/Postgres) — Lakebase uses Engine.Postgres (BIGSERIAL).

18.4 The two write mechanisms (the key detail)

(a) OLTP PG Sink — incremental, via SQL/COPY:

  • OltpPgSink.scala / OltpSink.scala: writes to the compute's live Postgres using ManagedConnection, PgConnectionUtils and ExceptionPreservingCopyManager (Postgres's COPY) to load; resolves identities with getTenantTimelineIds()/getProjectBranchIds() and authenticates with OltpAuthTokenProvider (OAuth token).
  • It uses up to 16 connections per sync; rate ~150 rows/s per CU. Ideal for triggered/continuous (applies only the CDF changes: insert/update/delete).

(b) Direct-to-Storage — bulk snapshot, generating PG pages:

  • The performance trick: instead of millions of SQL INSERTs, the Spark executors run a local PG with the reverse_etl_worker extension that directly produces the page files (heap, indexes, TOAST) and uploads them to S3; Lakebase's pageserver serves them.
  • PgDirectToStorageTask.scala (with DtsTableLayout, DirectToStorageUtils): produces page slices (SliceInfo{path, numBlocks, startBlockForChecksum}), index and TOAST columns, and uploads them (DirectToStoragePartitionResult, IndexUploadResult). IndexBuildTask builds the indexes; PgPartitionCopyTask copies partitions.
  • The hadron/pgxn/reverse_etl_worker/ extension: retl_heapam.c implements a table access method (retl_heapam_handler, init_retl_heapam) that assigns controlled TIDs (retl_heapam_tid_attnum) and builds indexes (retl_heapam_index_build_range_scan); retl_checkpoint_flush_all flushes and uploads the heap/index files to S3. It is not installed in the normal compute (a separate Docker layer), only in the PG of the Spark workers.
  • Rate ~2,000 rows/s per CU (much higher than the incremental SQL). It's the snapshot path.

18.5 Modes, CDF, PK, and limits (doc + code)

  • Modes (§4.5): snapshot (full copy, atomic replacement — usually goes through direct-to-storage), triggered (incremental on-demand/cron), continuous (real-time incremental, min ~15 s). Triggered/continuous require CDF on the source.
  • Auto CDF (canUseAutoCdf): if the source table has row tracking and is not write CDF, it can be enabled automatically; if not, it is directed to snapshot.
  • Primary key required (upsert/dedupe); nulls in the PK are excluded; optional timeseries key to break ties on duplicate PKs.
  • Limits (doc): up to 16 connections per sync; a cap of 8 TB logical in total across all synced tables; ~150 rows/s/CU (incremental) vs ~2,000 (snapshot). Read-only target; the UC schema is mapped to the Postgres schema.
Accuracy note: the orchestration (SyncedTableHandler, OltpSinkPipelineSpec, canUseAutoCdf), the Spark pipeline classes (DatabaseTableSyncPipelineExecutionExtension, OltpPgSink, PgDirectToStorageTask) and the reverse_etl_worker extension (retl_heapam, retl_checkpoint_flush_all) are verified in the code (universe + hadron); the rates/limits come from the official doc. The "direct to storage" reverse ETL reuses the OLTP sink that historically served the online tables (TiDB/MySQL); Lakebase uses the Postgres engine.

19. The proxy and connection routing

What it is: the proxy (hadron/proxy/) is the entry point to Lakebase. Because the compute is stateless and may be turned off (scale-to-zero), no client connects "directly" to a Postgres: it first goes through the proxy, which identifies which endpoint you want, authenticates you, wakes the compute and routes the bytes. It is the piece that makes scale-to-zero and multi-tenancy possible.

The proxy identifies the endpoint by SNI/connection string, authenticates against the control plane, wakes the compute and then just relays bytes. Without it there would be no scale-to-zero.

How it works internally (verified in proxy/src/)

  1. TLS + endpoint identification: the client connects over TLS; the proxy terminates it and, by SNI (pg_sni_router.rs: aaa--bbb--1234.domain → internal host:port) or by the connection string, extracts the endpoint id / project. SNI is client-controlled, so parsing is strict (a wrong route would leak across tenants).
  2. Authentication: queries the control plane for the endpoint rules via the ControlPlaneApi trait (control_plane/client/mod.rs): get_endpoint_access_control, get_role_access_control and get_endpoint_jwks (to validate Databricks JWT/OAuth). Supports SCRAM (scram/, sasl/) and token. The real implementation is cplane_proxy_v1.rs.
  3. wake_compute: before connecting, ensures the compute is alive (proxy/src/proxy/wake_compute.rs): it calls the control plane with retries and caching (CachedNodeInfo, key = endpoint + options). Calls are throttled with ApiLocks/WakeComputePermit so as not to overwhelm the control plane. If it is at zero, this starts the NeonVM (via compute_manager/autoscaling).
  4. connect_compute + dispatch: opens the connection to the compute's Postgres (connect_compute.rs) and from there relays the bytes in both directions (dispatch.rs). Query cancellation is routed via Redis (cancellation.rs, redis/) to find the right proxy/compute.
  5. Serverless path: serverless/ + http_sql.rs expose the Lakebase Data API (SQL over HTTP), which reuses the same auth/wake/routing backend.

20. The pageserver ingesting WAL

What it is: the read side of the compute/storage separation. The pageserver does not receive the WAL from the compute directly: it takes it from the safekeepers (which already made it durable, §5.4), decodes it and applies it to its layers so it can serve pages (§5.5). The code's own comment summarizes the pipeline: WAL receiver → wal_decoder → WalIngest → Repository.

The pageserver discovers the safekeeper with the freshest WAL via the broker, streams it, decodes and ingests it into its layers, and returns feedback on how far it got so the WAL can be trimmed.

How it works internally (verified in pageserver/src/)

  1. Discovery (storage_broker): safekeepers publish their timelines' state into the broker. The connection_manager subscribes (subscribe_for_state_updates) and accumulates those states to pick the "leader" safekeeper (the one with the highest LSN); it watches that the connection does not go stale and reconnects if necessary (30s reset interval).
  2. Streaming (walreceiver_connection): opens a Postgres connection in physical replication mode to the chosen safekeeper, runs identify_system and START_REPLICATION PHYSICAL {startpoint}, and consumes a ReplicationStream. It processes PrimaryKeepAlive (updates commit_lsn) and the WAL data. It sends PageserverFeedback with its LSNs so the safekeeper knows how far it consumed (and can trim WAL).
  3. Decoding (wal_decoder): the raw WAL is decoded into InterpretedWalRecord / NeonWalRecord. Neon uses an "interpreted WAL" protocol (plain WAL is no longer ingested directly), and it is filtered per shard: each pageserver only receives the records for the keys it owns.
  4. Application (WalIngest): WalIngest::ingest_record(...) (pageserver/src/walingest.rs) takes the interpreted records and stores them in the Repository via DatadirModification (pgdatadir_mapping): put_rel_wal_record, put_rel_page_image, plus SLRU and relation sizes. It accumulates the changes and calls commit(), which writes them into the open InMemoryLayer of the Timeline and advances last_record_lsn. From there §5.5 applies (freeze → DeltaLayer → S3).

21. Lakebase Change Data Feed (product view)

The CDF engine (the wal2delta extension, the output plugin, the bgworker and the dataplane-bridge) is in §5.17. This section looks at it from the product side: what it gives you, how to enable it, and how to consume it.

  • What it is: a native mechanism that captures every INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE on a Lakebase Postgres table from the WAL and materializes it as rows in a managed Delta table in Unity Catalog, batched and flushed every ~15 s. It is the way to "unlock" your operational data for lakehouse pipelines, models and apps.
  • Destination table: lb_<table>_history in the UC catalog/schema you choose. Same shape as Delta Change Data Feed: each row carries _pg_change_type (insert/delete/update_preimage/update_postimage), _pg_lsn, _pg_xid, _timestamp and _sort_by (a monotonic key to order all changes).
  • How to enable it: (1) a workspace admin enables the preview; (2) you set REPLICA IDENTITY FULL on the tables you want in the feed; (3) you start CDF in the Lakebase app choosing source database/schema and destination UC catalog/schema. The data appears as lb_<table>_history tables.
  • How to consume it: they are regular Delta tables in UC → read them with Spark/SQL/notebooks; typically you rebuild the state by taking, per PK, the latest version by _sort_by, or process the incremental change stream to feed pipelines/feature tables/auditing.
  • Requirements and limits: REPLICA IDENTITY FULL; tables only in the databricks_postgres database; partitioned tables unsupported; empty tables are skipped until they have ≥1 row; destination catalogs with default storage are not supported.
  • Use cases: operational CDC to the lakehouse, change auditing/history, feeding feature tables or models with fresh data, and analytics over transactional data without touching the OLTP database.
Relation to the rest: CDF is the product face of Forward ETL / ZeroETL (§5.17); its twin in the opposite direction is synced tables / Reverse ETL (§4.5 and §5.18).

22. 🔁 Forward ETL & Reverse ETL — the complete picture (synthesis)

This section zooms back out from the code (§10, §17, §18) and the product views (§4.5, §21) to teach the two data-movement directions between Lakebase and the lakehouse as one coherent story — the what, why, how, and when — so you can read the rest of this document and fully understand where each piece fits.

22.1 The mental model: ETL, forward ETL, reverse ETL

Think of two worlds: the operational world (Lakebase / Postgres — fast, transactional, serves apps) and the analytical world (the lakehouse — Unity Catalog / Delta — big scans, ML, BI). Data moves between them in three named ways:

  • ETL / ELT (classic): operational sources → lakehouse, to analyze.
  • Forward ETL (Lakebase → UC): push your operational changes into the lakehouse as Delta so analytics/ML/pipelines see them. In Lakebase this is the Change Data Feed (CDF).
  • Reverse ETL (UC → Lakebase): push lakehouse-computed results back into an operational Postgres table so apps can serve them at low latency. In Lakebase these are synced tables.
🧒 For a 5-year-old. Forward ETL = you tell the big family notebook everything you did today, so later everyone can count it. Reverse ETL = the family already figured out your favorite toy, so they put a sticker on your toy box so you grab it super fast.
👦 Technical explanation. Forward ETL / CDF = change-data-capture of your Postgres writes, materialized as append-only Delta history (lb_<table>_history) in UC — feeds pipelines, feature engineering, audit, analytics. Reverse ETL / synced tables = materialize a governed Delta table into a read-only Postgres table for millisecond serving to apps/agents — the "activation" step that closes the loop back to production.

The closed loop (this is why both exist — data makes a full round trip):

  📱 App/agent ──writes (OLTP)──▶ 🐘 Lakebase Postgres
        ▲                                │
        │ serves (ms lookups)            │ ① FORWARD ETL / CDF  (wal2delta · ~15s · change history)
        │                                ▼
   🐘 Lakebase ◀──③ REVERSE ETL / synced tables──── 🏞️ Unity Catalog / Delta
        (Lakeflow Spark · read-only PG table)        ② compute: features · segments · scores · aggregates

22.2 Forward ETL (Lakebase → UC) in depth — the Change Data Feed

Goal: get every operational change out of Postgres and into the lakehouse without touching/loading the OLTP DB yourself. The engine is wal2delta (full code in §5.17); here is the concept to retain.

  Postgres write (INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE) ──▶ WAL
        │
        ▼
  wal2delta output plugin (in the walsender)   ← needs REPLICA IDENTITY FULL
        │
        ▼
  logical replication slot ──▶ wal2delta bgworker (buffers + flush every ~15s)
        │
        ▼
  dataplane bridge (gRPC) ──▶ Delta  lb_<table>_history  in UC
     columns: _pg_change_type · _pg_lsn · _pg_xid · _timestamp · _sort_by
  • What you get: an append-only change log — a row per change tagged insert / delete / update_preimage / update_postimage, plus _pg_lsn, _pg_xid, _timestamp, _sort_by. Essentially SCD-2-style history.
  • How to consume it: (a) incremental pipelines / feature tables — process only new rows; (b) current-state view — deduplicate per primary key keeping the latest _sort_by (reconstructs "the table as of now"; this is exactly the deduplication LTAP's snapshot removes — §8); (c) audit/history.
  • Why REPLICA IDENTITY FULL: on UPDATE/DELETE Postgres logs only the PK by default; FULL logs the whole row before and after, so the feed carries pre- and post-image.
  • Latency ~15 s. Requirements/limits: REPLICA IDENTITY FULL; only the databricks_postgres database; no partitioned tables; empty tables skipped; no destination catalogs with default storage.
  • When to use: downstream ETL/pipelines, feature engineering, streaming consumers, auditing, analytics that must not hit the OLTP DB directly.

22.3 Reverse ETL (UC → Lakebase) in depth — synced tables

Goal: take a result you computed in the lakehouse (a segment, a fraud score, an aggregate, a feature vector) and make it serveable by an app at millisecond latency from Postgres. The engine is a managed Lakeflow Spark Declarative Pipeline (full code in §5.18); powered under the hood by LTAP Direct Writes (§8).

  UC / Delta table (+ CDF for incremental)
        │
        ▼
  Lakeflow Spark Declarative Pipeline (serverless)
        │
        ├─ incremental (COPY/SQL) · OltpPgSink · ≤16 conns ─────────────┐
        └─ bulk snapshot (Direct-to-Storage) · reverse_etl_worker ──────┤
                                    → page files → S3 → pageserver       ▼
                                                    🐘 synced Postgres table (READ-ONLY · mandatory PK)
                                                          └──▶ 📱 App reads: ms point lookups

Three modes (freshness ↔ cost):

ModeBehaviorCDFBest forThroughput
SnapshotFull copy, atomic in-place swap; re-run manually❌ not requiredHigh-churn tables (>10% per cycle) or no-CDF sources (views, Iceberg)~15,000 rows/s per CU (Provisioned) · ~2,000 (Serverless)
TriggeredInitial snapshot, then only changes, on-demand/cron✅ requiredNear-real freshness without always-on cost; predictable schedule (>~5 min freq may cost more than continuous)~1,200 rows/s per CU (Provisioned) · ~150 (Serverless)
ContinuousInitial snapshot, then always-on incremental pipeline✅ requiredLowest lag (min. ~15 s), higher cost~1,200 rows/s per CU (Provisioned) · ~150 (Serverless)
  • Two write mechanisms (§5.18): (a) incremental via OltpPgSink (COPY/SQL, ≤16 connections) for triggered/continuous; (b) bulk Direct-to-Storage via reverse_etl_worker/retl_heapam, which generates Postgres page files and uploads them to object storage for the pageserver to serve — this is LTAP Direct Writes (up to ~100× faster, zero impact on the primary OLTP compute).
  • Requirements/limits: mandatory primary key (nulls in PK excluded; used for upsert/dedup), only additive schema changes reflected, target is read-only, and an 8 TB logical cap across all synced tables.
  • When to use: serving app/agent reads, key-value lookups, feature serving. ⚠️ For analytics-style queries on fresh data, prefer Lakehouse//RT (§8) over bulk-loading a synced table.

22.4 Side-by-side comparison

Dimension➡️ Forward ETL / CDF⬅️ Reverse ETL / Synced tables
DirectionLakebase → UC (Postgres → Delta)UC → Lakebase (Delta → Postgres)
Product nameChange Data Feed (CDF) (a.k.a. ZeroETL/ForwardETL)Synced tables
Enginewal2delta extension, in the compute (§5.17)Lakeflow Spark pipeline, in the data plane (§5.18)
TriggerContinuous capture from the WALsnapshot · triggered · continuous
Latency~15 s batchessnapshot (manual) → ~15 s (continuous)
Output / targetlb_<table>_history Delta table in UCa read-only Postgres table in Lakebase
What it deliversA change log (history of every row change)The current state, served for lookups
Primary keyNot required (needs REPLICA IDENTITY FULL)Mandatory (upsert/dedup)
Schema changesCaptured (full row image)Only additive reflected
Typical consumerPipelines, feature tables, audit, analyticsApps, agents, key-value / feature serving
Writable?Delta output is managed (read for analytics)Target is read-only
Copies data?✅ yes (into Delta)✅ yes (into Postgres)

22.5 How this relates to LTAP and Federation (the big map)

All four bridge the operational and analytical worlds — but with different copy trade-offs:

  What do you need?
    ├─ Get operational CHANGES into the lakehouse   → FORWARD ETL / CDF          (copy · ~15s history)
    ├─ SERVE lakehouse results from Postgres        → REVERSE ETL / synced tables (copy · read-only)
    ├─ Query an EXTERNAL DB without copying          → QUERY FEDERATION (§9)       (zero-copy · read-only)
    └─ UNIFY OLTP+OLAP with no pipelines at all       → LTAP (§8)                   (one logical copy)
  • Forward + Reverse ETL are today's copy-based bridges (two directions, both materialize a second copy).
  • Query Federation (§9) is zero-copy but only for reading external databases.
  • LTAP (§8) is the future direction: keep one logical copy in storage and drop the pipelines — it even removes the CDF-deduplication trick from §22.2.

22.6 Gotchas to remember

  • Forward ETL: forget REPLICA IDENTITY FULL → the table is silently skipped; the feed is history, so deduplicate by PK for current state; partitioned/empty tables aren't captured.
  • Reverse ETL: the synced table is read-only — writing to it breaks integrity with the source; no PK → null-PK rows dropped; only additive schema changes flow; watch the 8 TB cap.
  • Both copy data — for always-fresh, no-copy access reach for Federation (external) or LTAP (Lakebase).
Cross-references: §4.5 (synced tables product), §5.10 (wal2delta code), §5.17 (forward ETL engine/flow), §5.18 (reverse ETL code), §21 (CDF product), Part 8 (LTAP), Part 9 (Query Federation).

PART 6 — The journey of a SELECT, INSERT, UPDATE and DELETE in Lakebase 🚂

Track B · Lakebase essentials · 🟢 Beginner→🟡 Intermediate · ▶ next: Part 7

Imagine you mail a letter to a giant library run by robots. We follow four letters: one that asks (SELECT), one that adds (INSERT), one that changes (UPDATE) and one that erases (DELETE). For each one we watch every robot that touches it, its name in the code, what it receives and what it does — until the answer travels back to you.

This builds on Parts 4 and 5; the new thing is that we follow the data itself, step by step, for each operation. Engine names (neon_*, WalProposer, WalIngest, Timeline…) are verified in Part 5; Postgres-core names (exec_simple_query, heap_insert, XLogInsert…) are standard PostgreSQL internals, kept so you can locate them.

0. The cast (the robots your letter meets) 🎭

#Robot (analogy)In the codeRepo / file
1🚪 The doorman — knows your reading room, checks your badge, turns the lights onproxy: pg_sni_router.rs, wake_compute.rs, connect_compute.rs, dispatch.rshadron/proxy/src/
2🧠 The librarian's brain — reads your letter and decides how to workbackend: exec_simple_query → parser → planner → ExecutorRunPostgres core (compute)
3🪪 The badge checker — confirms your Databricks identitydatabricks_auth: DatabricksIdentity, token_auth.chadron/pgxn/databricks_auth/
4🗄️📚 The desk (RAM) and near shelf (NVMe) where hot books sitshared_buffers + LFC file_cache.c (lfc_read/lfc_write)compute
5📮 The page mailman — fetches a book the desk lackssmgr pagestore_smgr.c (neon_read) + communicator.c (communicator_read_at_lsnv)hadron/pgxn/neon/
6✍️ The town crier — shouts your change so it can never be lostwalproposer walproposer.c (WalProposer, WalProposerBroadcast)hadron/pgxn/neon/
7👥 The three trusted friends — each writes it down; once most agree, it's permanentsafekeepers: SafeKeeper, AcceptorState, quorum (n/2)+1hadron/safekeeper/
8📣 The notice board — tells the warehouse who has the newest pagesstorage_brokerhadron/storage_broker/
9🏭 The warehouse — rebuilds any book from base + list of changespageserver: WalReceiver, WalIngest, Timeline, LayerMap, ImageLayer/DeltaLayerhadron/pageserver/
10📦 The deep vault (cloud) — keeps every layer foreverRemoteTimelineClient → S3 (remote_storage)hadron/libs/remote_storage/

The common first leg (all four letters start the same way)

Whatever the letter says, it always travels the same first stretch: you → doorman → librarian's brain.

The proxy identifies the endpoint, authenticates, wakes the compute (scale-to-zero) and relays bytes; then the backend parses, plans and executes — identical to vanilla Postgres. The Lakebase magic is below the executor.

1. 🔎 SELECT — the journey of a letter that asks

A SELECT reads and writes nothing: it generates no WAL and talks to no safekeeper. Its whole adventure is finding the right pages.

shared_buffers (RAM) → LFC (NVMe) → pageserver. The pageserver keeps no finished pages: it rebuilds the exact 8 KB page from a base ImageLayer + DeltaLayers (recursing into a parent branch if needed). Then MVCC shows you only the versions visible to you.

Step by step: the executor (SeqScan/IndexScan) asks the buffer manager (ReadBuffer) for a page by (relation, fork, block). On a miss, neon_read() (pagestore_smgr.c) computes the read LSN with neon_get_request_lsns() (a neon_request_lsns struct: request_lsn, not_modified_since, effective_request_lsn) — ⚠️ this LSN is a storage/cache-coherence device, not your MVCC snapshot: on a primary it is UINT64_MAX ("latest materialized page"), only on a replica the replay LSN — checks prefetch and the LFC (file_cache.c), and on a miss sends a NeonGetPageRequest {rinfo, forknum, blkno} to the pageserver. There get_vectored_reconstruct_data walks the LayerMap, the wal-redo process applies the deltas, and a NeonGetPageResponse comes back. On a branch it recurses to the ancestor (capping the LSN). Finally MVCC (xmin/xmax + CLOG via HeapTupleSatisfiesMVCC) filters versions — which is why reading never blocks writing. Since the compute is stateless, losing the LFC only costs a "cold cache".

📗 Read-your-writes, even though ingest is async. A SELECT right after your COMMIT still sees its own write because the pageserver wait_lsns: if the requested LSN hasn't been ingested yet, it waits for the WAL to catch up (default timeout 300 s) before answering — consistent read, at the cost of a tiny wait on the very freshest pages.
🎯 Two LSNs, two decisions — the one thing to internalize about reads in Lakebase. The page-read LSN (primary = latest / replica = replay) selects which physical 8 KB page the pageserver reconstructs; the MVCC snapshot (xmin/xmax + CLOG) selects which tuple versions on that page are visible to you. Storage hands you the freshest physical page; MVCC then filters the row versions — which is why reading never blocks writing and the read is still correct for your point in time.
🧒 Like a 10-year-old: "I ask for a page. If it's on the desk, great. If not, the page mailman runs to the warehouse, which doesn't keep finished books — it keeps a first draft plus a list of edits and rebuilds my exact page on the spot. Then I only keep the versions that belong to me."

2. ➕ INSERT — the journey of a letter that adds

An INSERT changes the world, so the town crier and the three friends appear. The big surprise: the commit becomes permanent without writing to any local disk — it's permanent when most safekeepers say "got it".

heap_insert creates a new version (xmin = my xid). The WAL record is the recipe, not the result. At COMMIT the walproposer sends it to the safekeepers; once a quorum (n/2)+1 acks, it is durable — no local fsync. Asynchronously the pageserver ingests the WAL into layers and uploads them to S3.

The Lakebase twist: in vanilla Postgres commit = fsync the WAL to local disk; in Lakebase the walproposer (WalProposerBroadcast()) sends an AppendRequest to the safekeepers (SafeKeeper/AcceptorState), each replies AppendResponse with its flush_lsn, and once a quorum (n/2)+1 acks the commit is durable — only then does the client see INSERT 0 1. The dirty page in shared_buffers is never flushed to a local data file; neon_wallog_page() only guarantees the WAL of an evicted page. Later the storage_broker points the pageserver at the newest safekeeper, the wal_decoder turns it into an InterpretedWalRecord, and WalIngest::ingest_record stores it via DatadirModification (put_rel_wal_record) into the open InMemoryLayer, advancing last_record_lsn; the layer later freezes into a DeltaLayer uploaded to S3.

🧒 Like a 10-year-old: "I write my new line on the page on my desk, but I don't glue the page into the book. The town crier shouts the change to three trusted friends. As soon as two of the three wrote it down, it can never be lost — that's my 'done!'. Later the warehouse quietly updates its copy."

3. ✏️ UPDATE — the journey of a letter that changes

An UPDATE is an INSERT of a new version plus a mark on the old one. The commit path is identical to §2 (walproposer → safekeeper quorum). The new ideas are MVCC versioning and HOT.

heap_update does NOT overwrite: it stamps v1 with xmax = my xid (a dead version old snapshots still see) and writes a new v2. If no indexed column changed and there's room, a HOT update skips the indexes. Commit and async ingest are exactly like §2; v1 is reclaimed later by VACUUM.

The Lakebase twist: the cleanup still happens in the compute, but the freed space and the dead-tuple WAL also flow to the safekeepers/pageserver like any other change.

🧒 Like a 10-year-old: "I don't erase the old line — I cross it out (so friends who started reading before me still see it) and write a fresh line below. If I only changed something the index doesn't care about, I don't bother the index. The crossed-out line gets swept away later."

4. ❌ DELETE — the journey of a letter that erases

A DELETE doesn't physically remove anything right away — it just marks the row as deleted (a tombstone). The commit path is again identical to §2.

heap_delete sets xmax = my xid on v1; it is not physically removed and no new version is created. Older snapshots still see v1. Commit and async ingest are like §2. Real removal happens later via VACUUM — that's why a DELETE commits fast but frees disk space later.

🧒 Like a 10-year-old: "I don't tear the page out — I just write 'deleted by me' next to the line. Friends who started reading earlier still see it; new friends skip it. The cleaning robot tears it out for good once nobody can still be reading it."

5. 🔭 The four journeys side by side

SELECT 🔎INSERT ➕UPDATE ✏️DELETE ❌
Executor nodeSeqScan/IndexScanExecInsertExecUpdateExecDelete
Heap function(read only)heap_insertheap_updateheap_delete
Touches xmin/xmaxreads themnew row: xmin=xidv1 xmax=xid + new v2v1 xmax=xid
Generates WAL?NoYes (XLogInsert)YesYes
Talks to safekeepers?NoYes — quorum (n/2)+1YesYes
How it becomes durablen/asafekeeper quorum (no local fsync)samesame
Reaches the pageserveron read (NeonGetPageRequest)async ingest (WalIngest)async ingestasync ingest
Creates dead tuples (bloat)?NoNoYes (old v1)Yes (tombstoned v1)
Cleaned by——VACUUM/autovacuumVACUUM/autovacuum

The single most important Lakebase idea here: in standard Postgres a write is durable when the WAL is fsync-ed to local disk; in Lakebase a write is durable when a quorum of safekeepers has the WAL — the compute keeps no permanent data on its own disk, and pages are rebuilt on demand by the pageserver from a base image + WAL deltas (possibly recursing into a parent branch).

Reads (SELECT) take the page path and never touch the WAL. Writes (INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE) become durable via the safekeeper quorum and reach the pageserver layers asynchronously.

PART 7 — Registering the Lakebase database in Unity Catalog 🗂️

Track C · Lakebase ↔ Lakehouse integrations · 🟡 Intermediate · ▶ next: Part 10

So far we've seen how Lakebase works (Parts 4–6). This part answers a different question: how do you make a live Lakebase Postgres database visible, discoverable and governed inside Unity Catalog (UC)? The mechanism is a special UC object called a Lakebase database catalog (also called a Postgres catalog or registered catalog).

0. What it is (and what it is not)

Registering a database creates a read-only UC catalog that is a live window onto your Postgres database. It is federation by reference, not a copy:

  • It does NOT move data. No bytes are duplicated into Delta. When you SELECT through the catalog, the query runs against the live Postgres at that instant.
  • It IS governance + discovery. The Postgres database now appears in Catalog Explorer, obeys UC permissions (USE CATALOG / USE SCHEMA / SELECT), shows up in lineage, and is captured by audit logs — exactly like any Delta table.

Do not confuse it with the other two Lakebase↔UC bridges from Part 4:

MechanismDirectionCopies data?Purpose
Registered database catalog (this part)UC reads Postgres live❌ NoGovern + query the live OLTP database from UC
Synced table (reverse ETL)UC → Lakebase✅ Yes (UC→PG)Serve Delta data from Postgres for low-latency apps
Lakebase CDF / forward ETLLakebase → UC✅ Yes (PG→Delta)Land Postgres changes as Delta history in UC

1. The mapping: three levels line up one-to-one

A Lakebase instance can host several Postgres databases. Each registered catalog binds exactly one Postgres database to exactly one UC catalog, and the two namespaces line up level by level:

  Lakebase (Postgres)                 Unity Catalog
