  ───────────────────                 ─────────────
  instance  "my-instance"
    └─ database  "databricks_postgres"  ───►  catalog  "my_catalog"
         └─ schema   "public"           ───►    schema   "public"
              └─ table  "orders"        ───►      table   "orders"
  • One UC catalog = one Postgres database. To expose several databases, register each one separately (you get several catalogs).
  • Postgres schemas become UC schemas; Postgres tables/views become UC tables. The discovery is lazy: when you expand the catalog → schema → table in Catalog Explorer, UC reads Postgres's own metadata (information_schema / pg_catalog) on demand and reflects it. New Postgres objects may be cached in the UI — hit refresh to force a re-read.

2. Prerequisites (check these first) ✅

  • A UC-enabled workspace and a running Lakebase database instance in it.
  • CREATE CATALOG privilege on the Unity Catalog metastore (the catalog creator needs it). To delete a catalog later you must be its owner or a metastore admin.
  • A serverless SQL warehouse. This is a hard requirement to view/query the catalog — Pro and Classic warehouses return PERMISSION_DENIED.
  • Legal names. The Postgres database name must be alphanumeric or underscore only — no hyphens. The UC catalog name must follow standard UC naming rules. (Note the trap: instance names can contain hyphens like my-instance, but database names cannot.)

3. The technical process, step by step

You can register through the UI, the CLI, the Python SDK, or the REST API — all four call the same control-plane operation (CreateDatabaseCatalog on the LBM).

3.1 UI (Catalog Explorer / Lakebase app)

  1. Open the Lakebase app and select your Provisioned instance.
  2. Go to the Catalogs page and click Add catalog (equivalently, in Catalog Explorer click + → Create a catalog and choose Lakebase Postgres as the type).
  3. Enter the catalog name.
  4. Either select an existing Postgres database, or type a new name and let it be created automatically (the "create if not exists" option).
  5. Confirm. The catalog appears immediately; expanding it triggers the metadata sync.

3.2 CLI

# databricks database create-database-catalog <catalog_name> <instance_name> <database_name>
databricks database create-database-catalog my_catalog my-instance databricks_postgres

3.3 Python SDK

from databricks.sdk import WorkspaceClient
from databricks.sdk.service.database import DatabaseCatalog

w = WorkspaceClient()

w.database.create_database_catalog(
    DatabaseCatalog(
        name="my_catalog",                 # the UC catalog name
        database_instance_name="my-instance",
        database_name="databricks_postgres",
        create_database_if_not_exists=True, # create the PG database if missing
    )
)

3.4 REST API

The SDK/CLI wrap the same LBM endpoint — a POST that creates the DatabaseCatalog object with { name, database_instance_name, database_name, create_database_if_not_exists }.

4. What happens inside when you register 🔬

Registration is a metadata / control-plane operation — no data is scanned:

  1. The LBM records a binding. The control plane (Lakebase Manager, from Part 5) creates a UC catalog object of type Lakebase Postgres whose payload is the pair (database_instance, postgres_database_name). If create_database_if_not_exists is set and the Postgres database is missing, the LBM issues a CREATE DATABASE on the instance first.
  2. UC stores the catalog, not the schema. The metastore now knows the catalog exists and where it points. It does not eagerly crawl every table.
  3. Lazy reflection. The first time you open a schema/table, UC connects to the live Postgres endpoint and reads information_schema/pg_catalog to learn the schemas, tables, columns and types, then presents them as UC objects. Results are cached in the UI (hence the refresh button).

5. What happens when you query through the catalog 🚂

SELECT * FROM my_catalog.public.orders from a notebook or the SQL editor travels like this:

  Serverless SQL Warehouse
        │  (1) resolves my_catalog.public.orders in UC
        │  (2) UC checks USE CATALOG / USE SCHEMA / SELECT for the caller
        ▼
  Lakebase proxy (pg_sni_router)   ◄── (3) opens a Postgres connection over SSL,
        │                                  authenticating with an OAuth token minted
        │                                  for the requesting Databricks identity
        ▼
  wake_compute  ─────────────────►  (4) if the instance was scaled to zero, it is woken
        ▼
  Live Postgres compute (backend) ─ (5) runs the SQL against the *current* data
        ▼
  Rows stream back up to the warehouse and to you

The important consequence: because it reads live Postgres, you always see the current committed state — but the read path goes through the OLTP engine (Parts 5–6), not a Delta scan, and it counts against your Postgres connection budget and compute.

6. Governance, permissions and observability 🔐

Once registered, the catalog is a first-class UC securable:

  • Permissions are pure UC grants: GRANT USE CATALOG ON CATALOG my_catalog TO ..., then USE SCHEMA, then SELECT. A user who lacks the grant cannot read the Postgres table through UC even if the underlying Postgres role could — UC is the gate.
  • Ownership follows the usual UC rules; when the database and catalog are created together, ownership follows the standard object-creation guidelines.
  • Discovery, lineage and audit logs work like any other catalog — the Postgres tables show up in search, in column/table lineage, and every access is logged.

7. Limits and gotchas ⚠️

  • Read-only. You cannot INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE through the UC catalog. To write, use the Lakebase SQL editor or connect to Postgres directly (Part 6).
  • Serverless only. Pro/Classic SQL warehouses → PERMISSION_DENIED.
  • One catalog = one database. Register each database separately.
  • Single workspace. An instance is scoped to one workspace; cross-workspace access to the registered catalog is not supported.
  • Naming. Database names: alphanumeric + underscore, no hyphens.
  • Deletion. You can drop the catalog via UI or CLI. Databricks recommends removing synced tables first (a source table can back up to 20 synced tables), and full cleanup can take up to three days.

8. 🧒 Explained for a 10-year-old

Imagine your family has two notebooks.

  • One is the big family library binder — everything the whole family keeps, neatly labeled, with rules about who's allowed to read which page. That's Unity Catalog.
  • The other is your little brother's live diary that he's always writing in, second by second. That's the Lakebase Postgres database.

Now you want the family library binder to also know about your brother's diary — so it shows up in the index, and the same "who can read what" rules apply. But you do not want to photocopy the diary, because he keeps changing it every second and the copy would be old immediately.

So instead you tape a magic bookmark into the binder that says: "Brother's diary lives over there." This is registering the database in Unity Catalog. When someone with permission flips to that bookmark, a little robot runs to the real diary, reads the exact page right now, and reads it out loud — the binder never keeps its own copy.

The rules of the magic bookmark:

  • 👀 You can only read, never write. You may read your brother's diary through the bookmark, but you can't scribble in it that way — if you want to write, you have to go to the actual diary.
  • 🎫 You need the special fast helper. Only the quick "serverless" helper knows how to follow the bookmark; the slower helpers just say "not allowed."
  • 🔖 One bookmark per diary. If your brother has three diaries, you tape in three separate bookmarks.
  • 🧹 Taking the bookmark out is slow. You should first put away anything that was copied out from it, and even then the full cleanup can take a few days.
  • ✍️ Neat names only. The diary's name can use letters, numbers and underscores — but no dashes.

Sources

  • Register your database in Unity Catalog — Databricks on AWS
  • Register a Lakebase database in Unity Catalog — Azure Databricks
  • Work with Unity Catalog (Lakebase) — Databricks on AWS
  • Query from the SQL editor — Databricks on AWS
PART 8 — LTAP: Lake Transactional/Analytical Processing 💧

Track D · Vision & deep internals · 🔴 Advanced / reference · ▶ next: Part 5

⚠️ Internal note. LTAP is an architecture Databricks is building toward — not a single shippable feature. The parts already usable (Direct Writes, Real-Time Analytics) are called out as Beta / Private Preview / Roadmap below. Source: the internal Lakebase: FAQ [INTERNAL ONLY] and the public blog From monolith to Lakebase to LTAP. This part builds directly on the Lakebase storage engine explained in Part 4 (safekeepers / pageservers / LSN) and Part 5 (hadron, ImageLayer/DeltaLayer, wal2delta).

0. 🧒 LTAP for a 5-year-old

Imagine you have two toy boxes:

  • 📦 The Fast Box (OLTP): holds the toy you're playing with right now. You grab it and put it back in one second. That's your transactional database (Postgres / Lakebase) — the app that writes "Juan just bought an ice cream."
  • 📚 The Big Box (OLAP): holds millions of toys and is for counting them all and finding the favorite. That's your Lakehouse — analytics, dashboards, reports.

The old problem: every time you put a toy in the Fast Box, you had to copy it by hand (or with a tired robot called CDC) into the Big Box. The robot got tired, fell behind, and sometimes broke. 😩

The LTAP magic: what if both boxes shared the same secret bottom? You save the toy once, and underneath — without anyone copying anything — it shows up in both boxes: in the Fast Box as-is, and in the Big Box already tidied up for counting. No copy-robot. No lag. Nothing to break.

The one line to remember: don't say "LTAP is one copy of data." Say it is one logical copy. Underneath, the bytes may physically live in more than one shape (Postgres pages for the Fast Box, Parquet/Delta/Iceberg for the Big Box) — but you only ever manage, secure and reason about one dataset.
👦 Technical explanation. Traditionally you run two systems: an OLTP database (Postgres) for your app's transactions and an OLAP system (a columnar warehouse/lakehouse) for analytics, glued by a fragile CDC/ETL pipeline that constantly copies and reshapes data — introducing lag, cost, and drift. LTAP removes that pipeline by unifying the two at the storage layer. The same data is kept as one logical copy, transparently available in row form (fast transactions) and open columnar formats (Delta/Iceberg) for analytics — the row→column conversion happens inside storage, off the transactional critical path. Net effect: pick the right engine per workload over a single governed copy, with no pipelines to babysit. (A Databricks/Lakebase architecture; deep mechanics in the rest of Part 8.)

1. What LTAP is (the precise definition)

LTAP (Lake Transactional/Analytical Processing) is a data-processing architecture that unifies Postgres and open columnar formats (Iceberg/Delta) directly at the storage layer, preserving Postgres for transactions and specialized Lakehouse engines for analytics.

Three things it lets you do:

  1. One logical copy of data across all OLTP + OLAP workloads, governed by Unity Catalog.
  2. Best-in-class engine per workload — Postgres for transactions, Lakehouse//RT / Spark for analytics — with no performance compromise on either side.
  3. Protect the primary OLTP by offloading compute-heavy work (analytics, bulk loads, index builds) off the primary Postgres.

Three things it is not:

  • ❌ Not a new proprietary hybrid format. Postgres stays Postgres; Iceberg/Delta stay Iceberg/Delta.
  • ❌ Not physical de-duplication (storage does not get cheaper — see §8). The value is logical unification.
  • ❌ Not CDC. The unification happens in storage, asynchronously and isolated, never on the OLTP compute (§7).

The big picture (one diagram to hold in your head)

Before the mechanics, here is the whole architecture on one page. One logical copy lives in storage, projected into two physical shapes, each read by the engine that fits the workload, all governed by one Unity Catalog:

One Catalog governs many engines, which read one logical copy that storage keeps in two shapes from one WAL history. Everything below is how each arrow works.

2. How LTAP works under the hood — the two paths

LTAP adds two data paths on top of the Lakebase engine (Part 4). The crucial property of both: they completely bypass the primary Postgres compute, so there is zero performance impact on the transactional workload.

Path A — Transcoding (Lakebase → Lake) — Private Preview / Roadmap

When data is written into Lakebase, the pageserver is designed to transcode the row-oriented Postgres pages and materialize them into open columnar Parquet (Delta/Iceberg) on object storage. ⚠️ Maturity: this current-state columnar projection is Private Preview (native, via Lakehouse / Real-Time analytics) and roadmap for external engines on the Iceberg projection (~10 min freshness) — see §8; do not present it as GA. It is distinct from the append-only change history that wal2delta/CDF produces (§7, Part 11).

  App: INSERT / UPDATE
        │
        ▼
  POSTGRES (compute) ── OLTP critical path — UNTOUCHED
        │ ① WAL stream
        ▼
  SAFEKEEPERS  (WAL durability · Paxos quorum)
        │ ② consume WAL
        ▼
  PAGESERVER ───③ serve PG pages (hot cache, by LSN)──▶ POSTGRES
        │
        │ ④ TRANSCODE rows → columns   (async · ISOLATED · zero OLTP impact)
        ▼
  OBJECT STORAGE (Lake)
     ├─ PG data pages     → for OLTP reconstruction
     └─ Parquet Delta/Iceberg → Lakehouse//RT · DuckDB · Spark (analytics)

Fine-grained technical facts about transcoding:

  • What is transcoded: row-level mutations → highly compressed columnar Parquet, before writing to object storage.
  • What is not transcoded: Postgres indexes. They are kept, served and rebuilt strictly in the hot cache tier (the pageserver) so transaction execution stays fast (mirrors §5.5 / Part 4).
  • Lossless / 100% Postgres-compatible: the storage preserves the exact Postgres byte representation, so all bits can be reinterpreted natively by Postgres — no information loss.
  • Async & isolated: transcoding runs in the storage tier, outside the primary. It consumes machine resources but has zero OLTP impact — unlike CDC, which steals resources from your primary (§7).
  • Two physical shapes, one logical copy: PG pages (for OLTP) and Parquet (for OLAP) coexist in the lake, but they are one logical dataset.
  • Billing: no upcharge planned for transcoding — handled natively by the storage infrastructure.

Path B — LTAP Direct Writes (Lake → Lakebase) — Beta today

The reverse direction: analytical engines (Spark) write directly into Lakebase storage (pageserver + object storage), bypassing the primary OLTP compute, to serve data with low latency.

  COMPUTE  ┌───────────────────────────────────────────────┐
           │  POSTGRES  (does NOT take the load; serves       │
           │            lookups afterward, by LSN) ◄────────┐ │
           └────────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │
  STORAGE  ┌───────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │
           │  PAGESERVER ── SAFEKEEPER                        │─┘  serve low-latency reads
           │      ▲                                           │
           │      │ bulk load pages (full parallelism)        │
           │   SPARK ◄── OBJECT STORAGE (Lake): Delta 🔺 / Iceberg 🧊
           └───────────────────────────────────────────────┘
  • Bulk load direct to pageserver + object storage using full Spark parallelism → up to 100× faster than a bulk load into vanilla Postgres, with zero impact on the primary.
  • Today this powers Synced Tables (Delta/Iceberg → Lakebase — see Part 4 §5 and Part 5 §18). Behind the scenes it is the reverse_etl_worker / retl_heapam direct-to-storage path: Spark generates PG heap/index pages and uploads them to object storage, which the pageserver then serves.

3. Deep dive — PageServer/Neon internals: reconstructing a page from the WAL

LTAP does not replace the Lakebase storage engine; it rides on it. So to reason about LTAP freshness, branching and PITR you must understand how the pageserver turns a stream of WAL into an 8 KB page (expands Part 4 §0 and Part 5 §5/§15).

3.1 The addressing model: Key × LSN

The pageserver stores data in a two-dimensional space:

  • Key — what page (relation + fork + block number → an 8 KB Postgres page, plus non-relational keys like CLOG/SLRU).
  • LSN (Log Sequence Number) — when, i.e. the exact byte offset in the WAL stream. The LSN is the clock of the whole system; every page version is addressed by the LSN at which it became valid.

A read is therefore always get_page_at_lsn(key, lsn) — "give me the state of this page as of that point in history." This single primitive is what makes branching and PITR fall out for free (§4).

3.2 The layers: images + deltas

The pageserver never stores "the current page" as a mutable cell. It stores immutable layer files in a per-timeline LayerMap:

LayerContentsRole
ImageLayerA full snapshot of a key-range at one LSN (materialized 8 KB pages).The base image — the starting point.
DeltaLayerThe WAL changes for a key-range across an LSN interval (a diff).The deltas applied on top of a base.
InMemoryLayerThe recent, still-open WAL held in memory.Freshest writes: Open → Frozen → flushed to a DeltaLayer.

Over time the pageserver compacts many deltas into new image layers (so reconstruction stays cheap) and garbage-collects layers older than the retention window (which bounds how far back PITR can go — §4).

3.3 The reconstruction algorithm (Timeline::get)

To answer get_page_at_lsn(key, LSN_req):

1. Start at LSN_req in this timeline's LayerMap. Walk DOWNWARD in LSN for `key`.
2. Collect every DeltaLayer record that touches `key`, newest→oldest,
   until you hit an ImageLayer for `key` (the base image) — the search STOPS there.
3. If this timeline has no base image low enough, RECURSE into the parent timeline
   at the branch point LSN (this is the branching hook — §4, Part 5 §15).
4. Hand (base image + ordered WAL deltas) to the WAL-redo apply:
   replay each WAL record on top of the base image, in LSN order,
   using Postgres's own redo functions (run in a dedicated wal-redo process).
5. The result is the exact 8 KB page as of LSN_req. Cache it (RAM → local NVMe LFC).
  get_page_at_lsn(key, LSN_req)
        │
        ▼
  Walk LayerMap DOWN from LSN_req for key
        │
        ▼
  Collect DeltaLayer WAL records  (newest → oldest)
        │
        ▼
  Hit an ImageLayer (base image) for key? ──no, none in this timeline──▶ RECURSE into
        │ yes                                                            PARENT timeline
        ▼                                                                @ branch-point LSN (⭐ branching)
  WAL-redo apply: replay deltas on base image
  (Postgres redo fns · wal-redo process)
        │
        ▼
  Exact 8 KB page @ LSN_req  ──▶  cache: RAM (shared_buffers) → NVMe (LFC)

Why this matters for LTAP: the same WAL-fed history that reconstructs OLTP pages is what the transcoder reads to materialize the columnar (Delta/Iceberg) representation. There is one source of truth (the WAL history addressed by LSN) feeding two physical projections (PG pages + Parquet) — the literal mechanism behind "one logical copy."

3.4 Where durability lives (recap from Part 4)

  • On COMMIT, durability = a safekeeper Paxos quorum acknowledging the WAL — not a local fsync.
  • Full Page Writes are disabled by design (no local page to tear) → ~94% less WAL traffic.
  • The compute is stateless; LSN is the contract between compute and storage.

3.5 🧮 Two ways to measure size: logical size vs synthetic size

Once storage is copy-on-write layers shared across branches (§3.2, §4), the innocent question "how big is my database?" stops having one answer. Neon defines two distinct measures, and confusing them is a classic source of "why is my bill/quota not what I expected?" (Part 15 §15.2/§15.9).

Logical size — the Postgres-visible size of one branch, at one LSN.

Logical size is the size of a branch at a given point in time: the total size of all tables and indexes in all databases on that branch (what \l+ shows in psql), plus the Postgres SLRUs (CLOG, etc.) and a little metadata.
  • It's a property of one branch at one LSN — it changes as you insert/delete (and as MVCC dead tuples come and go until VACUUM, Part 1 §5).
  • It is the number Lakebase uses for quotas (the 16 TB / 2 TB caps, Part 15 §15.2) and what the sizing query estimates (SELECT sum(len(to_csv(struct(*))) + 32) …).
  • It is not what's physically stored: it ignores the WAL history, the retention window, and the fact that branches share bytes. A 10 GB branch has ~10 GB logical size no matter how much history sits behind it.

Synthetic size — one number that models a whole project's storage for billing.

Because copy-on-write branching makes "how large is my database" ambiguous, Neon computes a synthetic size for the whole project (all branches together). It is a logical model, independent of the actual physical bytes, built so that shared history is never double-counted.

For the simple case — a single branch with a fixed PITR/retention window — the model is:

  synthetic size  =  logical size at the START of the PITR window   (a base snapshot)
                  +  the WAL accumulated across that window          (the history)

Example (straight from Neon's model): a 10 GB database into which you then insert 5 GB has a synthetic size of 10 GB (snapshot) + 5 GB (WAL) = 15 GB — even though the current logical size is 15 GB and the history lets you rewind. With branches, the cleverness is de-duplication: Neon stores one snapshot before the branch point (the shared data) + the WAL on each branch afterward, so two branches that share history are not billed as two full copies. Only when branches diverge a lot does keeping separate per-branch snapshots become cheaper — Neon evaluates that trade-off per branch point.

Summing per-branch logical sizes double-counts shared history; synthetic size keeps one base snapshot + each branch's unique WAL, so shared bytes are billed once.

Logical sizeSynthetic size
Scopeone branch, at one LSNthe whole project (all branches)
MeasuresPostgres-visible data (tables+indexes+SLRUs)a billing model of stored data + history
Includes history/WAL?❌ no✅ yes (WAL within the retention window)
Counts shared branch data?each branch counts its full viewshared data counted once (copy-on-write aware)
Used forquotas (16 TB / 2 TB), sizingbilling intuition (GB-month meters, Part 15 §15.9)
Grows withyour live datalive data + retention window length + branch divergence
🔑 The mental model: logical size answers "how much data does Postgres see on this branch?"; synthetic size answers "how much is this project really costing to store, counting history but not double-counting what branches share?" This is why, in Part 15, increasing the retention window does not consume your logical-size quota (it grows synthetic/physical, not logical) and why expiring dev branches are cheap (they add only their own WAL deltas, not a second full copy).

4. Branching & PITR — and how they map onto the Iceberg/Delta representation

4.1 Branching / PITR on the Postgres (OLTP) side — solved today

Because every page is addressed by Key × LSN (§3.1) and history is immutable layers (§3.2):

  • A branch is a new timeline that points at parent@LSN. Reads reuse the parent's history up to that LSN (recursion in §3.3 step 3) and layer only the branch's own deltas on top. Instant, cheap, copy-on-write — the parent takes no load (Part 4 §6, Part 5 §14/§15).
  • PITR is branching: "go back in time" = attach/position a timeline at an earlier LSN, as long as it's within the history/retention window GC has not yet reclaimed. No data is copied.

4.2 Branching / PITR on the analytical (Iceberg/Delta) side — partly roadmap ⚠️

This is the honest state per the internal FAQ:

"Parquet transcoding does not replace the core Lakebase storage engine, so all native branching and PITR capabilities remain fully supported. The specific mechanics of how these branches will be exposed in the analytical (Iceberg/Delta) format are still being designed."
  • ✅ Native branching/PITR keep working on the operational data — transcoding is additive and does not disturb the timeline/layer machinery.
  • ✅ One logical copy survives a restore or a branch — customers never lose their unified OLTP+OLAP representation after a snapshot restore or a branch. Native Postgres storage mechanics (needed for PITR) are not eliminated.
  • 🔩 The natural conceptual mapping (not yet a committed contract): open table formats already have their own versioning — Iceberg snapshots and Delta versions are immutable, time-travelable points. An LSN in the Postgres timeline conceptually corresponds to an Iceberg snapshot / Delta version of the transcoded table, so "branch/PITR at LSN X" could surface as "read Iceberg snapshot N." How exactly this is exposed is under design — do not promise a specific UX to customers.
  Shared LSN history  (copy-on-write · not duplicated)
              (branch LSN / fork point)
                 │                 │
                 ▼                 ▼
   main timeline            dev branch
   PG pages + own deltas    PG pages + own deltas
                 │
                 ╎ transcode (async)
                 ▼
   Iceberg/Delta snapshots (analytical projection)
   ⚠️ branch/PITR exposure: UNDER DESIGN

   PITR = attach compute to a past LSN  ····▶ (into the shared history)

5. When to use which engine — Lakebase vs. Lakehouse//RT vs. Databricks SQL

LTAP's whole point is "use the right engine for the workload over one logical copy." Here is how to pick.

  What is the shape of the workload?
        │
        ▼
  Point lookups / single-row reads & writes?  ──yes──▶  🐘 LAKEBASE (Postgres OLTP)
  (app, agent, key-value)                                ms-level P95, high QPS,
        │ no                                             transactional, reads+WRITES
        ▼
  Low-latency analytics on FRESH operational  ──yes──▶  ⚡ LAKEHOUSE//RT
  data? (live dashboards, BI, ad-hoc,                   sub-second→seconds P95, reads the
  high concurrency)                                     up-to-date Lakebase state, high concurrency
        │ no
        ▼
  🏭 DATABRICKS SQL (DBSQL): large-scale batch analytics, big scans/joins,
     ETL, warehouse — throughput-optimized
Dimension🐘 Lakebase (OLTP)⚡ Lakehouse//RT🏭 Databricks SQL (DBSQL)
WorkloadTransactions, point lookups, key/value, app/agent stateReal-time analytics on live operational dataLarge-scale/batch analytics, ETL, data-warehouse queries
Read patternSingle-row / few-row, indexedSelective→medium scans, aggregations, joinsMassive scans & joins across the lakehouse
Writes✅ Full transactional read+writeRead-focused (queries current state)Batch loads / transforms
Latency (order of magnitude)~ms P95~sub-second to low-seconds P95seconds→minutes (throughput first)
ConcurrencyVery high QPS, many small txnsHigh-concurrency dashboards/queriesFewer, heavier queries
Freshness on Lakebase dataLive (source of truth)Near-real-time (~seconds via Lakehouse//RT; ~10 min via external engines on the Iceberg projection — Roadmap)Whatever is materialized in the lake
Mental image"the app" — save/fetch one order"the live dashboard" — read a lot, fast, fresh"the warehouse" — crunch everything
In LTAP termsThe OLTP path (§2/§3)Reads the transcoded/live state (Path A)Reads the columnar lake projection

Rules of thumb:

  • Need to write transactionally, or do point lookups for an app/agent → Lakebase.
  • Need fresh operational data with low latency at high concurrency (dashboards, BI, ad-hoc) → Lakehouse//RT (and you can often skip bulk loads entirely — query live state directly).
  • Need heavy, throughput-bound analytics/ETL over the broader lakehouse → DBSQL.
  • ⚠️ If a customer is using Synced Tables purely to serve analytics-style queries, Lakehouse//RT is usually the better fit — it removes the bulk-load step and queries live data directly.

6. How to position & sell LTAP by persona

PersonaLead withConcrete talking points
Platform Teams / Data Engineers / Leadership (CIO, CTO) "Kill the brittle pipelines between OLTP and the rest of your estate." • No pipeline design around schema changes / type mappings. • Everyone builds apps without juggling two data systems + CDC. • App teams add analytics, feature stores, search; non-technical users "vibe-code" with Genie App Builder. • Data engineers stop hand-building Bronze tables / reverse-ETL scripts. • One pane of glass for auditability & governance across the entire estate (OLTP + OLAP) via Unity Catalog.
OLTP Teams / Architects "Protect and speed up your primary Postgres; build richer apps without bolting on new databases." • Offload analytics, bulk loads, index builds, replication handling off the primary → more reliable, more performant OLTP. • Agents/apps run transactions + search + analytics over one governed copy, each on the right engine. • Lower TCO — no separate specialized tools. • Ship faster — instant provisioning, pay-as-you-go, branching for rapid iteration, natural data gravity to the lake.

Persona-agnostic anchors:

  • The differentiator vs. everyone else: unified Catalog (UC) + best-in-class OLTP (Lakebase Postgres, compute/storage separation) + best-in-class OLAP (open-format Lakehouse) + the right engines (Spark for bulk, Lakehouse//RT for fast analytics). This is the honest answer to "Lakebase vs. …".
  • Start today, no migration: starting on Lakebase now is a seamless path to the full LTAP vision; capabilities are incrementally adoptable — adopting more LTAP features is not expected to require a data migration.

7. LTAP vs. CDC vs. Lakebase CDF (don't conflate them)

LTAP vs. traditional CDC — the storage-level vs. compute-level distinction:

  Traditional CDC (e.g. Debezium)
    POSTGRES PRIMARY ──continuous poll (steals OLTP resources)──▶ CDC stream
                                                                    │ separate transform pipeline
                                                                    │ ⚠ manual schema/type mapping
                                                                    ▼
                                                                  OLAP (columnar)

  LTAP (storage level)
    POSTGRES PRIMARY ──WAL──▶ PAGESERVER (async · ISOLATED) ──transcode rows→columns──▶ Delta/Iceberg
    ✓ zero impact
Traditional CDCLTAP
Where it runsExternal compute, polling the primaryStorage tier (pageserver)
Impact on OLTPHigh (competes for resources)Zero (async, isolated)
Schema / type mappingManual, fragileAutomatic (preserves PG representation)
Failure pointsPoller + transform pipelineNone of those
What it deliversA stream of changes (history)A snapshot (current state)

LTAP does not replace Lakebase CDF — they are complementary (both give an Iceberg/Delta view, but of different things):

DeliversNative enginesExternal engines
LTAP (snapshot)Point-in-time current state for analyticsLakehouse//RT (up-to-date Lakebase state)(Roadmap) DuckDB, OSS Spark on the Iceberg projection, ~10 min freshness
Lakebase CDF (stream of changes)Sequential row-level change log for downstream ETL / feature engineeringSDP, DBSQL / Materialized Views (incremental, handles schema changes)Spark Structured Streaming (manual schema-drift + pipeline upkeep)
Rule: need the latest state to analyze → LTAP. Need the history of changes for pipelines / feature engineering → CDF (Part 5 §17/§21). See Part 4 §5 (synced tables) and Part 5 §17 for the CDF code path.

8. What exists today vs. the roadmap

Available today (for testing):

  1. LTAP Direct Writes — Beta. Bulk-loads directly to Lakebase storage (pageserver + object storage), bypassing OLTP compute; full Spark parallelism, up to 100× faster loads, zero OLTP impact. Powers Synced Tables today. (Beta on AWS; Azure in ~weeks.)
  2. Real-Time Analytics on Lakebase — Private Preview. Analytical queries against the live operational state. Near-term: ~seconds freshness via Lakehouse//RT. Longer-term: external engines (DuckDB, OSS Spark) on the auto-generated Iceberg projection at ~10 min freshness.

Roadmap (next few quarters — scope evolving):

  1. Lakehouse//RT querying Lakebase → GA.
  2. Lakebase as a UC Managed Table — UC as the authoritative representation of a Lakebase table across all engines (Lakebase, Lakehouse//RT, …); investigating native fine-grained policies (ABAC, RLS, CLM) directly on Lakebase tables (builds on Part 7 — today UC governs only external/analytical access).
  3. Lakebase data in the customer's own bucket (today it lives in Databricks' cloud account).
  4. Longer term: data tiering (keep only hot data on the OLTP path, offload cold/historical to the lakehouse) and an open-source LTAP library to let external engines transcode into Iceberg/Delta.

Pricing / storage reality check:

  • No upcharge for LTAP with Lakebase.
  • Storage price does not drop — Lakebase storage cost is driven by the in-memory + local-NVMe caches at the pageserver (what gives OLTP-level performance), not by bytes in object store. Do not pitch storage savings as an LTAP benefit today.

9. 🌐 LTAP vs. HTAP and the rest of the market

If you've read about databases before, LTAP will remind you of HTAP (Hybrid Transactional/Analytical Processing). They target the same pain — running transactions and analytics over one dataset — but they solve it in different layers, and that difference is the whole point.

ApproachWhere it unifiesTypical examplesTrade-off
Classic two-system + CDCNowhere — you glue two engines with a pipelinePostgres + Snowflake + DebeziumLag, cost, drift, pipeline ops (§7)
HTAP (single-engine)In one compute engine that keeps a row store and a column store togetherSingleStore, TiDB, SAP HANA, Oracle In-MemoryOne engine must be good at both; analytics can contend with OLTP; usually a proprietary stack
LTAP (storage-level)In the storage layer, under separate best-in-class enginesDatabricks Lakebase + Lakehouse//RT + DBSQLAnalytics/OLTP are physically isolated (async transcoding, §2); data stays in open formats; you pick the engine per workload (§5)

The essential distinction: HTAP puts the "hybrid" in the engine; LTAP puts it in the storage, and keeps the engines separate and specialized. That is why LTAP can promise zero OLTP impact from analytics (transcoding runs in storage, off the primary — §2/§7) and open formats (the columnar projection is plain Delta/Iceberg any tool can read), which single-engine HTAP systems generally cannot.

🧭 How to answer "isn't this just HTAP?" — "Same goal, different layer. HTAP fuses row+column inside one engine and usually one proprietary product. LTAP unifies at storage, so transactions and analytics run on isolated, specialized engines over one logical copy in open formats, governed by one catalog."

10. 🔄 A concrete end-to-end flow (follow one row through LTAP)

To make the abstraction tangible, trace a single order from an app write to an analytics read.

One write, two shapes, isolated engines — no CDC pipeline, no manual schema mapping, no second copy to sync.

Walk-through:

  1. The app commits id=42. Durability is a safekeeper quorum (Part 4 §0/§8, §3.4), so COMMIT returns in milliseconds. The OLTP path is done here.
  2. The WAL flows to storage. The pageserver ingests it and can reconstruct the row page on demand via get_page_at_lsn (§3.3) for the next point lookup.
  3. Storage transcodes the new row into the columnar Delta/Iceberg projection — asynchronously and isolated from the primary (§2 Path A). The app never waited for this.
  4. Analytics reads the fresh state. Lakehouse//RT queries sum(amount) over the columnar projection (§5) — seconds-level freshness, zero load on the OLTP Postgres.

11. ❓ Common misconceptions & FAQ

MisconceptionReality
"LTAP is one physical copy, so storage is cheaper."❌ It's one logical copy in two physical shapes (PG pages + Parquet). Storage cost is driven by the pageserver caches, not object bytes — no storage savings to pitch today (§8).
"It's a new hybrid file format."❌ Postgres stays Postgres; Delta/Iceberg stay open standards. Unification is in the storage layer, not a format (§1).
"Analytics will slow down my app."❌ Transcoding and analytical reads run in storage / separate engines, off the primary — zero OLTP impact by design (§2/§7).
"It's just Databricks' version of CDC."❌ CDC runs on compute and steals primary resources; LTAP runs in storage, async and isolated, and delivers a snapshot (current state), not a change stream (§7).
"LTAP replaces Lakebase CDF."❌ Complementary: LTAP = latest state to analyze; CDF = history of changes for pipelines/features (§7).
"I must migrate to adopt LTAP."❌ Starting on Lakebase today is the on-ramp; LTAP features are incrementally adoptable, no data migration expected (§6).
"Analytical branching/PITR on the Iceberg side works now."⚠️ Under design. Native OLTP branching/PITR work today; how they surface on the analytical projection is not yet a committed contract (§4.2) — don't promise a UX.
"Real-Time Analytics is GA."⚠️ Private Preview today; Lakehouse//RT-querying-Lakebase → GA is roadmap (§8).

Quick FAQ:

  • Do I need to choose a format? No — you write Postgres normally; storage produces the columnar projection automatically (§2 Path A).
  • How fresh is the analytical view? ~seconds via Lakehouse//RT; ~10 min via external engines on the Iceberg projection (roadmap) (§5/§8).
  • Who governs access? One Unity Catalog plane across OLTP + OLAP (§1 big-picture diagram; Part 7).
  • What powers this today? LTAP Direct Writes (Beta) already backs Synced Tables (§2 Path B, §8).

12. 🧒 LTAP recap for a 10-year-old

You have a toy chest with two lids. The small lid is for grabbing one toy super fast (the app). The big lid is for dumping everything out on the floor to count and sort it (the analytics). The clever part: it's the same chest — one pile of toys underneath. When you drop a toy in through the small lid, a quiet helper inside the chest also lines it up neatly so it's ready to count through the big lid — and it does this without ever bothering you while you play. So you never have to copy your toys into a second chest, they're never out of date, and the same house rules (who's allowed to touch which toys) apply to both lids. That "same chest, two lids, quiet helper inside" is LTAP.

13. 🧠 LTAP mental rule

Postgres stays Postgres. Iceberg/Delta stay Iceberg/Delta. LTAP unifies them in the storage layer, not in a new format and not on the OLTP compute. One logical copy (physically projected as PG pages and Parquet), fed by one WAL history addressed by LSN, governed by one Unity Catalog — so you can point the right engine (Lakebase / Lakehouse//RT / DBSQL) at each workload with zero pipelines and zero impact on the primary.

Sources

  • Lakebase: FAQ [INTERNAL ONLY] — internal Databricks doc (LTAP section). Internal use only.
  • From monolith to Lakebase to LTAP: rethinking the database from storage up — Databricks blog
  • Engine selection: When to use which engine — Lakebase vs. Lakehouse//RT vs. Databricks SQL (internal Lakehouse//RT FAQ).
  • Cross-references: Part 4 (Lakebase storage engine), Part 5 (hadron, layers, wal2delta), Part 7 (Unity Catalog registration).
PART 9 — Query Federation: querying an external PostgreSQL from Databricks 🔗

Track C · Lakebase ↔ Lakehouse integrations · 🟡 Intermediate · ▶ next: Part 8

This part is about Lakehouse Federation (a.k.a. query federation): reading a remote, external PostgreSQL (or MySQL, Snowflake, Redshift…) from Databricks without ingesting the data. Do not confuse it with Part 7 (registering a Lakebase Postgres in Unity Catalog) — §9.10 draws the exact line. Based on the Databricks docs: Query federation for databases and Run federated queries on PostgreSQL.

9.0. 🧒 Query federation for a 5-year-old

Imagine your toys are in your room (Databricks / the lakehouse) but your favorite dinosaur 🦕 lives at grandma's house (an external PostgreSQL database far away). You have two choices:

  1. Move the dinosaur to your room (copy all the data over — ingestion / ETL). Slow, and now you have two dinosaurs that can drift apart.
  2. Use a magic phone ☎️. You call grandma and say "tell me only the GREEN dinosaurs." Grandma looks in her own house and reads back just the green ones. You never move the toys, and you always hear the newest answer.

Query federation is the magic phone. Databricks phones the external Postgres, hands it as much of your question as Postgres can answer on its own side (that's pushdown), and Postgres does the searching in its own house and sends back only the rows you asked for. One important rule: you can only LOOK, never change grandma's toys (federation is read-only).

The one-line idea: federation queries data where it lives, instead of copying it first.
👦 Technical explanation. Query federation runs SQL from Databricks against an external database (like PostgreSQL) without ingesting or replicating its data. Databricks translates as much of your query as it can into the source's native SQL and pushes it down over JDBC; the source executes that and returns only the results, and whatever can't be pushed runs afterward in Spark. It's read-only and shines for ad-hoc, exploratory, or proof-of-concept work. Versus building an ETL/replication pipeline: no copy and always-fresh data, but you're bound by the source DB's capacity and by how much the connector can push down (§9.5). It's the "borrow, don't move" strategy.

9.1. 🎓 What query federation is (the precise definition)

Lakehouse Federation lets Unity Catalog present an external database as a read-only catalog. Queries are pushed down to the foreign database over JDBC; execution is split between the remote engine (PostgreSQL) and Databricks compute (Spark/Photon).

Two Unity Catalog objects make it work:

  1. Connection — a UC securable that stores where the external system is and how to authenticate (host, port, user, password/secret). Reusable across many catalogs.
  2. Foreign catalog — a UC catalog that mirrors one external database. Expanding it lazily reflects the remote schemas/tables as read-only UC tables.

9.2. 🗺️ Architecture and the three-part namespace

The remote database's namespace lines up with Unity Catalog's three levels:

  External PostgreSQL                      Unity Catalog
  ───────────────────                      ─────────────
  database  "shop"        ──USING CONNECTION──►  foreign catalog  "pg_shop"
    └─ schema   "public"                    ─►     schema   "public"
         └─ table  "Orders"                 ─►        table   "orders"   ← lowercased! (§9.8)
  Databricks (Unity Catalog + compute)
    SELECT … FROM pg_shop.public.orders WHERE …
        │  UC resolves the foreign catalog + checks grants
        ▼
    Spark / Photon (Catalyst planner, JDBC data source)
        │  ① generated SQL (pushed-down) over JDBC
        ▼
    External PostgreSQL: postmaster → backend → planner → executor
        │  ② only the requested rows (JDBC ResultSet)
        ▼
    Spark/Photon ③ finishes any non-pushed work → result to you
  • Connection = the credentials/path object. Foreign catalog = the mirror. Both are securable in UC.
  • The JDBC driver opens a normal Postgres connection — from Postgres's point of view Databricks is just another client (postmaster fork()s a backend for it — Part 1 §10.1 / Part 3).

9.3. 🛠️ Setup — CREATE CONNECTION and CREATE FOREIGN CATALOG

Requirements: a UC-enabled workspace; Databricks Runtime 13.3 LTS+ (Standard/Dedicated access mode) or a Pro/Serverless SQL warehouse (2023.40+); network reachability from compute to the Postgres host; CREATE CONNECTION on the metastore; and CREATE CATALOG + connection ownership (or CREATE FOREIGN CATALOG).

Step 1 — the connection (prefer secrets over plaintext):

-- Plaintext (fine for a quick test, NOT for production)
CREATE CONNECTION pg_conn TYPE postgresql
OPTIONS (
  host 'prod-db.example.com',
  port '5432',
  user 'readonly_user',
  password 's3cr3t'
);

-- Recommended: pull credentials from a Databricks secret scope
CREATE CONNECTION pg_conn TYPE postgresql
OPTIONS (
  host 'prod-db.example.com',
  port '5432',
  user     secret('my_scope','pg_user'),
  password secret('my_scope','pg_password')
);

Step 2 — the foreign catalog (one per external database):

CREATE FOREIGN CATALOG IF NOT EXISTS pg_shop
USING CONNECTION pg_conn
OPTIONS (database 'shop');

Now query it like any UC table (three-part name):

SELECT customer_id, SUM(total)
FROM pg_shop.public.orders
WHERE order_date >= DATE '2026-01-01'
GROUP BY customer_id;

Authentication today is basic auth (user + password, ideally via secrets). No OAuth/token auth for the PostgreSQL connector. If the Postgres server requires TLS, connectivity must be arranged at the network level.

9.4. ⚙️ The query lifecycle & the pushdown engine (deep dive)

The magic is pushdown: Databricks' Catalyst optimizer rewrites as much of the plan as possible into a single SQL statement it hands to PostgreSQL, so PostgreSQL does the heavy filtering/aggregation before any rows cross the network. Whatever can't be pushed runs afterward in Spark/Photon.

  Your Spark SQL / DataFrame
        │
        ▼
  Catalyst logical plan
        │  for each operator: can the JDBC/PostgreSQL dialect express it?
        ├── YES → fold it into the generated remote SQL (SELECT … WHERE … GROUP BY … LIMIT …)
        └── NO  → keep it as a Spark operator (runs locally after the fetch)
        │
        ▼
  JDBC: send generated SQL → PostgreSQL executes it
        (its OWN parser → planner → executor; uses indexes / seq scan — Part 6)
        │
        ▼
  ResultSet: only matching rows stream back → Spark/Photon runs the non-pushed operators → result

See exactly what got pushed — the single most useful debugging tool:

EXPLAIN FORMATTED
SELECT customer_id, SUM(total) FROM pg_shop.public.orders
WHERE order_date >= DATE '2026-01-01' GROUP BY customer_id;
-- Look at the "Scan JDBCRelation" / foreign data source node:
-- it prints the EXACT SQL sent to PostgreSQL (its WHERE, GROUP BY, LIMIT).

You can also read the foreign data source scan node in the Query Profile UI to see the generated remote SQL and how many rows came back.

9.5. ✅ What gets pushed down to PostgreSQL (and what doesn't)

CategoryPushed down
Filters (WHERE)✅ all compute types
Projections (column pruning)✅ all compute types
Aggregates (SUM, COUNT, AVG, MIN, MAX, GROUP BY)✅ all compute types
Limit & Offset✅ all compute types
Date / Time / Timestamp functions⚠️ partial — filter expressions only
Mathematical functions⚠️ partial — filter expressions only
String functions⚠️ partial — filter expressions only
Miscellaneous (Cast, Alias, SortOrder)⚠️ partial — filter expressions only
TABLESAMPLE✅ (without replacement)
Arithmetic + - * % /✅ requires ANSI mode enabled
Boolean = <=> < <= > >=✅
Bitwise & | ~✅

What this means in practice (the PostgreSQL angle):

  • A pushed-down WHERE order_date >= … becomes a real Postgres predicate → Postgres can use its index (B-tree/BRIN), do an Index Scan, and return few rows. Fast. (This is literally the flow of Part 6 — a SELECT arriving from a JDBC client.)
  • Something not pushed (e.g. a complex Spark-only expression, or a join between two different connections) forces Spark to pull the rows first and compute locally → potentially a full table scan on the Postgres side and a lot of network traffic. This is where federated queries get slow.
  • Rule of thumb: shape queries so the selective WHERE, the GROUP BY and the LIMIT are things PostgreSQL can do; make sure those predicate columns are indexed in PostgreSQL.

9.6. 🐘 PostgreSQL deep dive — what actually happens on the database side

When a federated query runs, from PostgreSQL's perspective nothing exotic happens — it's a normal client session (this is why everything in Parts 1, 3 and 6 applies unchanged):

  1. The JDBC driver opens a connection → the postmaster fork()s a dedicated backend (Part 1 §10.1). One federated scan = one (or a few) Postgres backend(s).
  2. The backend authenticates via pg_hba.conf (the readonly_user), then receives the generated SQL.
  3. That SQL goes through the parser → planner/optimizer → executor (Part 2 §6.6). The planner uses statistics and indexes exactly as for any query — a pushed-down filter on an indexed column is an Index Scan; an unfiltered fetch is a Seq Scan.
  4. Rows stream back over the wire as a JDBC ResultSet; MVCC means this read doesn't block writers on the source and sees a consistent snapshot (Part 1 §10.4 / Part 2 §6.7).

Consequences you should design for:

  • Give the federation role least privilege (GRANT SELECT only; a dedicated read-only role). It shows up in pg_stat_activity like any backend and counts against max_connections on the Postgres side.
  • Long/large federated scans hold a backend and a snapshot open → they can hold back VACUUM (the global xmin horizon, Part 3 §3) and pin resources, just like any long-running SELECT.
  • Indexing on the source is your main performance lever — federation cannot invent an index; it can only push a predicate that Postgres then serves well if an index exists.

PostgreSQL → Databricks (Spark SQL) type mapping

The federated table's columns are surfaced with mapped types. The standard mapping (verify against the current docs, as the exact list evolves):

PostgreSQL typeDatabricks / Spark SQL type
booleanBOOLEAN
smallint / int2SHORT
integer / int4INT
bigint / int8LONG
real / float4FLOAT
double precision / float8DOUBLE
numeric / decimal(p,s)DECIMAL(p,s)
char(n) / varchar(n) / textSTRING
byteaBINARY
dateDATE
timeSTRING (no exact Spark equivalent)
timestamp / timestamptzTIMESTAMP
uuidSTRING
json / jsonbSTRING
arrayARRAY<element>
enumSTRING
Types with no clean Spark equivalent (e.g. time, uuid, json, ranges, network types) generally arrive as STRING. If you need structured access to jsonb, parse it on the Databricks side after the fetch.

9.7. 🔄 Metadata & refresh

Unity Catalog auto-refreshes foreign metadata at query time — you don't register tables by hand. The first query after creating the catalog triggers a full metadata refresh (so it can feel slow); you can pre-warm or schedule refreshes:

REFRESH FOREIGN CATALOG pg_shop;                       -- whole catalog
REFRESH FOREIGN SCHEMA  pg_shop.public;                -- one schema
REFRESH FOREIGN TABLE   pg_shop.public.orders;         -- one table

Schedule these with a Databricks Job when the remote schema changes on a known cadence.

9.8. 🔐 Governance, limits & performance

Governance (Unity Catalog): the foreign catalog is a first-class securable — GRANT USE CATALOG / USE SCHEMA / SELECT, plus lineage, search and audit logs, exactly like a Delta table. UC is the gate even if the underlying Postgres role could see more.

Key limitations (know these before you pitch it):

  • Read-only. No INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE through the foreign catalog.
  • No result/disk caching — every run re-queries the source.
  • Names are lowercased in UC; tables/schemas whose names are invalid UC identifiers are ignored; case-sensitive identifiers are a known problem for some connectors.
  • Executor memory: if the result set (or a non-pushed operation's input) is too large, the Spark executor can OOM — because the rows had to be pulled locally.
  • Best fit: ad-hoc reporting, PoCs, exploration, and incremental-migration bridges — not high-volume production ingestion. For heavy or repeated use, ingest (or materialize) instead.

Performance: concurrency follows the Databricks SQL concurrent-query limit (no per-connection cap across warehouses). Materialized views can be built on top of a federated query to snapshot the data:

CREATE MATERIALIZED VIEW shop_daily AS
SELECT order_date, SUM(total) AS revenue
FROM pg_shop.public.orders GROUP BY order_date;

9.9. 🔑 The five defining principles of Lakehouse Federation (deep dive)

These five properties are what you must internalize — the first three are its super-powers, the last two are the constraints that come with them. Each is explained for a 5-year-old, a more technical explanation, and then in depth.

9.9.1 Zero-copy querying

Federation reads the source live; it never makes a copy in the lakehouse.

  • 🧒 For a 5-year-old. You don't carry grandma's toys home. You look at them through the phone while they stay in her house. Nothing is ever moved.
  • 👦 Technical explanation. A foreign table is not materialized in the lakehouse — no Delta files, no ingestion job, no second physical copy. Every query reads the live source at execution time, so you always see the current committed state and pay zero extra storage. This is the opposite of ingestion/CDC/replication, where you maintain a copy that costs storage and can drift.
  • 🎓 Deep technical. The foreign "table" is a metadata pointer (a Spark JDBCRelation) held in Unity Catalog, not data. At scan time the executor opens a JDBC connection and streams rows on demand; no bytes are persisted in the lakehouse. Consequences: (1) freshness = whatever the source committed, bounded by its MVCC snapshot (Part 1 §10.4); (2) lakehouse storage cost = 0; (3) no amortization — every query re-reads the source (see §9.9.5). Contrast: synced tables (Part 4 §5) and CDF (Part 5 §17) do copy; federation is the only zero-copy path.
  ZERO-COPY (federation)                    vs     INGESTION / CDC (copy)
  ─────────────────────                            ──────────────────────
  UC foreign table = pointer                       UC managed table = Delta files
        │ at query time                            ┌── pipeline copies + transforms ──┐
        ▼                                           ▼                                  │
  live external Postgres ──rows──▶ you              external Postgres ──▶ Delta copy ──┘──▶ you
  no storage · always fresh ·                       storage cost · can lag ·
  every query hits the source                       query hits the local copy (fast, cacheable)

9.9.2 Foreign catalogs provide unified governance

A registered external DB obeys the same Unity Catalog rules as your Delta data — one governance plane.

  • 🧒 For a 5-year-old. The big family rulebook that says "who may read which page" also covers the borrowed book. Same rules for all the books, yours and borrowed.
  • 👦 Technical explanation. Once you CREATE FOREIGN CATALOG, that external database is a first-class UC securable: the same GRANT USE CATALOG / USE SCHEMA / SELECT model, the same lineage, search, audit logs, and tags as any Delta table. UC is the single gate — a user without the grant can't read the foreign table even if the underlying Postgres role could. End-users never touch DB credentials; the Connection object holds them.
  • 🎓 Deep technical. Governance is layered: the caller is checked against UC grants before any SQL is sent; only then does the query run using the single stored credential on the Connection (credential brokering — end users never see the Postgres user/password, enabling least-privilege: one read-only DB role behind many UC-governed users). The foreign catalog participates in column/table lineage, search/discovery, and audit logs like Delta. Note the asymmetry: UC can be more restrictive than the source (UC is the gate) but never loosens the source's own permissions; row filters / column masks / ABAC are enforced at the UC layer.
  User ──(1) UC checks grants: USE CATALOG / USE SCHEMA / SELECT──▶ [allowed?]
                                                                     │ yes
                                                                     ▼
  Unity Catalog ──(2) uses the CONNECTION's stored credential──▶ external Postgres
   (governance,          (end-user never sees the DB user/password;     (its own roles
    lineage, audit)       one read-only role behind many UC users)       still apply)

9.9.3 Query pushdown reduces data transfer

The source does the filtering/aggregating; only the small result crosses the network.

  • 🧒 For a 5-year-old. You tell grandma "only the green ones," so she reads back just a few toys — instead of mailing you the whole toy box to sort at home.
  • 👦 Technical explanation. Pushdown folds your WHERE, projected columns, GROUP BY, and LIMIT into the SQL sent to PostgreSQL, so the source reduces the data first and only the small result travels back. Without it, Spark would pull the entire table over the network and filter locally — huge transfer and executor-memory pressure. It's the single biggest lever on federated performance and cost.
  • 🎓 Deep technical. Uses Spark's Data Source V2 pushdown APIs (SupportsPushDownFilters, SupportsPushDownAggregates, SupportsPushDownLimit, column pruning). The PostgreSQL dialect translates Catalyst expressions into native SQL; what it can't translate stays a Spark operator run after the fetch (partial pushdown). Quantitatively: a pushed GROUP BY returns N group rows instead of M source rows (N ≪ M); a pushed selective WHERE on an indexed column becomes a Postgres Index Scan returning a handful of rows. Confirm the exact remote SQL with EXPLAIN FORMATTED (§9.4). This is why source-side indexing (§9.6) is your main tuning lever.
  WITHOUT pushdown:  Postgres ──[10,000,000 rows]──▶ network ──▶ Spark filters → 12 rows   (huge transfer)
  WITH pushdown:     Postgres runs WHERE + GROUP BY → 12 rows ──▶ network ──▶ Spark          (tiny transfer)
                                  (uses its index)

9.9.4 Federated tables behave differently from Delta tables

A foreign table is a live JDBC view of another system — not a managed Delta table you own.

  • 🧒 For a 5-year-old. The borrowed book isn't your book: you can't draw in it, you can't keep your own copy, and you can only read it when the other library is open.
  • 👦 Technical explanation. A foreign table looks like a table in UC, but it's a pointer to a live external system, so it lacks a Delta table's powers: it's read-only (no INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE/MERGE), has no Delta features (time travel, VACUUM, OPTIMIZE/Z-order/liquid clustering, constraints, controlled schema evolution), no result/disk caching, its metadata is lazily reflected (names lowercased, invalid identifiers ignored), and its performance/availability depend on the remote DB. Treat it as a window, not storage you own.
  • 🎓 Deep technical. The contrast, dimension by dimension:
DimensionDelta / managed tableFederated (foreign) table
What it physically isParquet files + transaction log you ownA JDBC pointer; data stays in the source
WritesFull INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE/MERGERead-only
ACID transactionsYes (Delta log)No (you don't control source txns)
CachingResult Cache + Disk CacheNone — re-queried every run
Time travel / historyYes (VERSION AS OF)No
OPTIMIZE / Z-order / clusteringYesNo
Statistics / CBODelta stats drive the plannerRely on the source's stats + pushdown
Schema evolutionYou control itReflected from source (lazy, lowercased)
Streaming sourceYesNo
FreshnessAs of last write to DeltaLive source state (zero-copy)
Failure domainThe lakehouseThe remote DB (down / slow / at capacity)

Why: a Delta table is files + a log in your lakehouse (so Databricks can cache, version, optimize, and transact on it); a foreign table is a reference to another engine's live state, so those file-level capabilities simply don't exist for it.

9.9.5 Latency trade-offs apply

Federation is great for small, selective, ad-hoc queries — and poor for big or repeated ones.

  • 🧒 For a 5-year-old. One quick phone question to grandma is instant. But asking her to read the whole encyclopedia takes forever — and the first call each day is slow because she has to find her glasses first.
  • 👦 Technical explanation. Every federated query pays overhead a local Delta scan doesn't: JDBC connection setup, the source executing under its own load, network round-trips, mostly single-threaded result streaming, and — crucially — no caching, so every run pays full cost. The first query on a new catalog also triggers a full metadata refresh. So: excellent latency for small, selective, pushdown-friendly queries; painful for large scans or high-frequency dashboards → then ingest or materialize instead.
  • 🎓 Deep technical. Latency = metadata refresh (first-query cost; pre-warm with REFRESH) + connection establishment + remote planning/execution (bounded by source capacity, indexes, its own concurrency) + network transfer (proportional to the non-pushed volume) + Spark-side residual compute. There is no Result Cache / Disk Cache; concurrency follows the DBSQL concurrent-query limit; each scan is a Postgres backend counting against the source's max_connections and possibly holding back its VACUUM (§9.6). Mitigations: write pushdown-friendly queries, index the source, pre-warm metadata, and for hot repeated queries build a materialized view or scheduled ingestion — deliberately trading zero-copy back for a cached copy.
  Small · selective · pushdown-friendly   →  FEDERATE   (fresh · no copy · low latency)
        │
        ▼  query grows: big scans? repeated dashboards? joins across sources?
  Large · repeated · not-pushable         →  MATERIALIZE / INGEST
        (materialized view · synced table · scheduled copy → cacheable & fast, but a copy)

9.10. 🧭 Federation vs. the other Lakebase↔UC bridges (don't confuse them)

MechanismDirectionCopies data?Writable?EngineUse it for
Query Federation (this part)UC reads external Postgres live over JDBC❌ No❌ Read-onlyRemote Postgres + Spark (pushdown)Ad-hoc/PoC on an external DB you don't own in Databricks
Registered database catalog (Part 7)UC reads a Lakebase Postgres live❌ No❌ Read-onlyLakebase Postgres (serverless SQL warehouse)Govern + query your own Lakebase DB from UC
Synced table (Part 4 §5)UC → Lakebase (reverse ETL)✅ YesRead-only targetLakeflow Spark pipelineServe Delta data from Postgres for low-latency apps
Lakebase CDF / forward ETL (Part 5 §17)Lakebase → UC✅ Yes—wal2deltaLand Postgres change history as Delta in UC
LTAP (Part 8)Storage-level unification❌ (one logical copy)✅ (OLTP path)Pageserver transcodingUnify OLTP+OLAP with no pipelines

The clean mental split: an external Postgres you can't run inside Databricks → Query Federation (JDBC, pushdown, this part). A Lakebase Postgres (Databricks-managed) → Part 7 (native UC catalog) or LTAP/CDF/synced tables.

9.11. 🧒 Recap for a 10-year-old

Federation is a video call to another library 📞📚. Instead of carrying every book home, you call the other library and ask your question. The smart part is that you tell the other librarian to do the hard work — "only the green books, counted by author" — so they flip through their own shelves (using their card catalog = the Postgres index) and read back just the short answer. You never carry books home, you always get today's shelf, and you're only allowed to read, never to scribble in their books. If you ask something the other librarian can't do, you have to borrow a big pile of books and count them yourself at home — slower, and your backpack (the Spark executor) might not be big enough. So: ask in a way the other librarian can answer, and make sure their catalog (index) covers it.

Sources

  • Query federation for databases — Databricks on AWS
  • Run federated queries on PostgreSQL — Databricks on AWS
  • Cross-references: Part 1 §10 (postmaster, MVCC), Part 3 (backends/processes), Part 6 (the journey of a query), Part 7 (registering a Lakebase DB in UC).
PART 10 — Synced tables: serving lakehouse data inside Lakebase 🔄

Track C · Lakebase ↔ Lakehouse integrations · 🟡 Intermediate · ▶ next: Part 11

This part is the hands-on, operational guide to synced tables — the UC → Lakebase (reverse ETL) bridge that materializes a governed Unity Catalog table into a read-only Postgres table you can serve to apps and agents in milliseconds. The internals of this engine (the Lakeflow Spark pipeline, OltpPgSink, the Direct-to-Storage reverse_etl_worker) are in Part 5 §18, and the forward vs. reverse ETL big picture is in Part 5 §22 — this part is the how-to: modes, keys, creation, scheduling, limits, governance. Based on the Databricks docs: Sync data to synced tables (Lakebase) and Serve lakehouse data with synced tables.

10.0. 🧒 Synced tables for a 5-year-old

Your big toy warehouse 🏭 (the lakehouse) has every toy you own, neatly counted and organized. But the warehouse is far away, so grabbing a toy from there takes a while.

Right next to your bed you have a small toy basket 🧺 (Lakebase Postgres). A helper robot 🤖 looks at the warehouse and copies your favorite toys into the basket so you can grab them instantly when a friend asks.

  • A synced table is that basket: a copy of the warehouse's toys, kept fresh by the robot.
  • You choose how often the robot refreshes: once (snapshot), when you press the button (triggered), or all the time (continuous).
  • One rule: you may only take toys out of the basket, never put your own toys in — the robot would throw them away on the next refresh. The basket only mirrors the warehouse (read-only).
The one-line idea: a synced table copies a governed lakehouse table into a fast, read-only Postgres table so apps can serve it in milliseconds.
👦 Technical explanation. A synced table is a managed materialization of a Unity Catalog table (Delta, Iceberg, view, or materialized view) into a read-only Postgres table in a Lakebase instance. A managed Lakeflow (Spark Declarative) pipeline keeps the Postgres copy in step with the UC source using one of three scheduling policies — SNAPSHOT (full replace), TRIGGERED (incremental on demand/schedule), or CONTINUOUS (streaming, ~seconds). You pick a primary key (required — it's how rows are addressed and upserted) and optionally a timeseries key (to deduplicate rows sharing a primary key). This is the "activation" / reverse-ETL step: it pushes results you computed in the lakehouse back into the operational tier so a low-latency app, a feature-serving path, or an AI agent can read them at Postgres speed with full ACID reads and up to ~1,000 concurrent connections. It is the mirror image of Lakebase CDF / forward ETL (Part 5 §17, §22), which flows the other way (Lakebase → UC).

10.1. 🎓 What a synced table is (the precise definition)

A synced table is a Unity Catalog object that references a sync pipeline, plus the read-only Postgres table that pipeline materializes inside a Lakebase database. Creating one gives you two artifacts: (1) a synced table in Unity Catalog (the governed handle + pipeline reference), and (2) a read-only Postgres table in Lakebase, queryable by your applications over the Postgres wire protocol.

Example mapping: the UC table analytics.gold.user_profiles is served in Postgres as gold.user_profiles_synced.

Supported sources (what you can sync from):

Source typeSnapshotTriggered / Continuous
Managed & external Delta tables✅✅ (needs CDF)
Managed & external Iceberg tables✅❌ (snapshot only)
Views and materialized views✅❌ (snapshot only)
🔑 Why the split: Triggered and Continuous are incremental — they read only what changed, so the source must expose a Change Data Feed (CDF). Views and Iceberg tables don't, so they can only do a full Snapshot.

10.2. 🔁 The three sync modes (scheduling policies)

This is the single most important decision. The mode is the scheduling_policy field on the spec.

ModeWhat the pipeline doesLatency / freshnessCostChoose it when…
SNAPSHOTRuns once per refresh: copies the entire source and replaces the Postgres table in place, atomically. No CDF needed.Whole-table lag; refresh on demand/scheduleCheapest per run for high churn; ~10× more efficient than incremental when >10% of rows change per cycleSource changes a lot per cycle (>10%), or has no CDF (Iceberg, views)
TRIGGEREDRuns on demand or on a schedule; each run pulls only changes since the last run (inserts/updates/deletes) and applies them. Requires CDF.Freshness = how often you triggerGood cost/lag balance; running more often than ~5 min can cost more than ContinuousRows change incrementally on a known cadence
CONTINUOUSPipeline runs continuously; changes stream to Postgres in near real time. Requires CDF.Lowest lag (~seconds); min interval 15 sHighest (compute always on)Changes must appear near-instantly

Enable CDF on the source before using Triggered/Continuous:

ALTER TABLE your_catalog.your_schema.your_table
  SET TBLPROPERTIES (delta.enableChangeDataFeed = true);
💡 Rule of thumb. Start with Triggered (best default). Move to Snapshot if each cycle rewrites most of the table or the source can't emit CDF. Move to Continuous only when sub-minute freshness genuinely matters — and remember that triggering more often than ~5 minutes usually means you should just go Continuous.

10.3. 🔑 Primary key & timeseries key

Primary key — required.

  • It's how rows are addressed and upserted into Postgres. The UI auto-detects a candidate; verify it.
  • Primary-key columns are NOT NULL in the synced table. Rows with a NULL in any PK column are excluded from the sync (silently dropped).

Timeseries key — optional, but critical when the source has duplicate PKs.

  • If two source rows share the same primary key, the pipeline fails unless you give it a timeseries key.
  • With one set, the synced table keeps only the row with the latest timeseries value per primary key (last-writer-wins). Classic use: a CDC/event source keyed by id with an updated_at/event_time column.

10.4. 🛠️ Creating a synced table

⚠️ Two product surfaces, two SDK namespaces. Lakebase ships as Serverless / Autoscaling (the newer projects/branches model → SDK w.postgres.*) and as Provisioned instances (→ SDK w.database.*). The concepts are identical; the spec fields and some numeric limits differ (§10.7). Both are shown below.

Prerequisites: a workspace with Lakebase enabled and a Lakebase project/instance (+ a Postgres database); a UC table to sync with USE_SCHEMA + CREATE_TABLE on the target schema; for Triggered/Continuous, CDF enabled on the source (§10.2).

UI (Catalog Explorer): Catalog → select the source UC table → Create → Synced table → set table name, database instance/project + Postgres database, sync mode, primary key (verify auto-detected), optional timeseries key, new or existing pipeline, optional Serverless usage policy → Create. Watch Catalog → Overview; when status is Online, query it.

Python SDK — Serverless / Autoscaling (projects & branches):

from databricks.sdk import WorkspaceClient
from databricks.sdk.service.postgres import (
    SyncedTable, SyncedTableSyncedTableSpec,
    SyncedTableSyncedTableSpecSyncedTableSchedulingPolicy as Policy,
)

w = WorkspaceClient()
synced = w.postgres.create_synced_table(
    synced_table_id="my-catalog.sales.orders",           # catalog.schema.table
    synced_table=SyncedTable(spec=SyncedTableSyncedTableSpec(
        source_table_full_name="main.sales.orders",
        branch="projects/my-project/branches/production",
        primary_key_columns=["order_id"],
        scheduling_policy=Policy.TRIGGERED,               # SNAPSHOT | TRIGGERED | CONTINUOUS
        postgres_database="mydb",
        create_database_objects_if_missing=True,
    )),
).wait()
print(f"Synced table created: {synced.name}")

Python SDK — Provisioned instances:

from databricks.sdk import WorkspaceClient
from databricks.sdk.service.database import (
    SyncedDatabaseTable, SyncedTableSpec, NewPipelineSpec, SyncedTableSchedulingPolicy,
)

w = WorkspaceClient()
synced = w.database.create_synced_database_table(
    SyncedDatabaseTable(
        name="database_catalog.schema.synced_table",
        spec=SyncedTableSpec(
            source_table_full_name="source_catalog.source_schema.source_table",
            primary_key_columns=["id"],
            scheduling_policy=SyncedTableSchedulingPolicy.TRIGGERED,
        ),
        new_pipeline_spec=NewPipelineSpec(
            storage_catalog="storage_catalog",
            storage_schema="storage_schema",
        ),
    )
)

CLI:

databricks postgres create-synced-table my-catalog.sales.orders \
  --json '{
    "spec": {
      "source_table_full_name": "main.sales.orders",
      "branch": "projects/my-project/branches/production",
      "primary_key_columns": ["order_id"],
      "scheduling_policy": "SNAPSHOT",
      "postgres_database": "mydb",
      "create_database_objects_if_missing": true
    }
  }'

REST API (async — poll the returned name until done: true / Online):

# Serverless / Autoscaling
curl -X POST \
  "https://$WORKSPACE/api/2.0/postgres/synced_tables?synced_table_id=my-catalog.sales.orders" \
  -H "Authorization: Bearer $DATABRICKS_TOKEN" -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{ "spec": { "source_table_full_name": "main.sales.orders",
        "branch": "projects/my-project/branches/production",
        "primary_key_columns": ["order_id"], "scheduling_policy": "SNAPSHOT",
        "postgres_database": "mydb", "create_database_objects_if_missing": true } }'

# Provisioned instances
curl -X POST "https://$WORKSPACE/api/2.0/database/synced_tables" \
  -H "Authorization: Bearer $DATABRICKS_TOKEN" -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{ "name": "catalog.schema.synced_table",
        "spec": { "source_table_full_name": "source_catalog.source_schema.source_table",
                  "primary_key_columns": ["id"], "scheduling_policy": "TRIGGERED" },
        "new_pipeline_spec": { "storage_catalog": "storage_catalog",
                               "storage_schema": "storage_schema" } }'

10.5. ⏱️ Scheduling & triggering the pipeline

The initial sync runs automatically on creation. After that, Snapshot and Triggered modes need something to fire subsequent runs. Two ways:

A) A Lakeflow Job with a Database Table Sync pipeline task. Workflows → Create job → Tasks → + Add task type → Ingestion and Transformation → Database Table Sync pipeline → pick your synced table's pipeline → Schedules & Triggers → Add trigger, then choose Table update (fires when the source UC table changes — pairs with Triggered) or Scheduled (cron + timezone — pairs with Snapshot).

B) Programmatically — grab the pipeline id off the synced table and start an update:

table = w.database.get_synced_database_table(name="catalog.schema.synced_table")
pipeline_id = table.data_synchronization_status.pipeline_id
w.pipelines.start_update(pipeline_id=pipeline_id)

10.6. 💸 Cost, compute & throughput

A synced table is powered by a managed Lakeflow pipeline; each sync opens up to 16 connections to the Lakebase database (they count toward the instance's connection limit). Write throughput depends on mode and product surface:

SurfaceContinuous / Triggered writesSnapshot writes
Serverless / Autoscaling~150 rows/s per Capacity Unit (CU)up to ~2,000 rows/s per CU
Provisioned instances~1,200 rows/s per CU~15,000 rows/s per CU
  • Snapshot is far cheaper per row than incremental — hence ">10% churn → use Snapshot".
  • Full-refresh transiently doubles storage: the old Postgres version isn't dropped until the new sync completes, so both versions briefly count toward the logical size quota (§10.7).
  • Continuous keeps compute always on → highest cost; Triggered more often than ~5 min usually costs more than just going Continuous.

10.7. 📊 Limits & capacity planning

LimitServerless / AutoscalingProvisioned instances
Total logical size across all synced tables16 TB (contact support for more)2 TB
Recommended max size per refresh-based table~1 TB~1 TB
Synced tables per source tableup to 20up to 20
Connections per syncup to 16up to 16
Min Continuous interval15 s15 s
Concurrent Postgres connections (serving side)up to ~1,000instance-dependent
  • Naming: database/schema/table identifiers must match [A-Za-z0-9_]+ — no hyphens/special chars; uppercase or special chars in the source UC name must be quoted in Postgres.
  • Schema evolution: only additive changes (new columns) are picked up in Triggered/Continuous.
  • Duplicate PKs without a timeseries key → the pipeline fails (§10.3).

10.8. 🔤 Type mapping & invalid characters

Unity CatalogPostgresUnity CatalogPostgres
BIGINTBIGINTSMALLINT/TINYINTSMALLINT
INTINTEGERSTRINGTEXT
DOUBLEDOUBLE PRECISIONBINARYBYTEA
FLOATREALBOOLEANBOOLEAN
DECIMAL(p,s)NUMERICDATEDATE
TIMESTAMPTIMESTAMP WITH TIME ZONETIMESTAMP_NTZTIMESTAMP WITHOUT TIME ZONE
INTERVALINTERVALARRAY/MAP/STRUCTJSONB

Unsupported: GEOGRAPHY, GEOMETRY, VARIANT, OBJECT. Null bytes (0x00) in STRING break Postgres TEXT/JSONB and fail the sync — sanitize at the source:

SELECT REPLACE(column_name, CAST(CHAR(0) AS STRING), '') AS cleaned_column FROM ...
-- or cast the column to BINARY to preserve raw bytes

10.9. 🔐 Governance, ownership & permissions

  • Owner is not you. The Postgres table is owned by an internal role, databricks_writer_<dbid> — the pipeline, not the creator. So owner-only Postgres commands can't be run directly.
  • The creator is auto-granted on the table: SELECT, DELETE, TRUNCATE; on the schema: USAGE, CREATE. Never INSERT/UPDATE — the pipeline owns the data and would overwrite your writes on the next refresh. (Access is tied to the creating identity; changing the pipeline's Run as doesn't reassign it.)
-- databricks_superuser manages access
GRANT USAGE ON SCHEMA synced_schema TO some_user;
GRANT SELECT ON synced_schema.synced_table TO some_user;
REVOKE SELECT ON synced_schema.synced_table FROM some_user;

-- authorize DDL (CREATE/ALTER/DROP INDEX, DROP TABLE) on a synced table
CREATE EXTENSION IF NOT EXISTS databricks_auth;
SELECT databricks_synced_table_add_manager('"synced_schema"."synced_table"'::regclass, '["some_user"]');
SELECT databricks_synced_table_remove_manager('synced_table', '["some_user"]');
SELECT * FROM databricks_synced_table_managers;

10.10. ✅ Allowed operations & deletion

In Postgres, only do (anything else fights the pipeline): read-only queries, create indexes (to speed up serving queries), and drop the table (to reclaim space after removing it from Unity Catalog).

Delete in two steps: remove it from Unity Catalog (stops refreshes; via UI kebab → Delete, or w.postgres.delete_synced_table(...).wait() / the w.database equivalent / DELETE REST), which also drops the Postgres table. If a stray copy remains, DROP TABLE synced_schema.synced_table reclaims the space.

10.11. 🚀 Optimization strategies (general considerations)

Keeping a synced table fast and cost-efficient comes down to four levers: the sync mode, the primary key, the connection/pipeline layout, and the Postgres-side indexes. None of these is exotic — they are the same OLTP tuning instincts from Parts 2–3, applied to a table a pipeline owns.

  • Pick the right sync mode — cost follows freshness. Use Snapshot for tables that change infrequently or are fully replaced each cycle: at >10 % churn it is ~10× cheaper per row than the incremental modes (up to 15,000 rows/s per CU vs ~1,200; §10.2, §10.6). Reserve Continuous for when you genuinely need the lowest lag (~seconds, min. 15 s): it is the most expensive mode because it keeps the managed Lakeflow compute running 24/7 and holds its database connections open continuously (up to 16, against the instance's connection budget). Triggered run more often than ~5 min usually costs more than simply going Continuous.
  • Use smart primary keys — the PK is your physical access path. In Postgres the primary key is not just a uniqueness constraint: it is the B-tree the pipeline upserts through and the one your app's point lookups traverse, so it should match your query patterns and spread writes evenly. The trade-off is real:
