    • Monotonic keys (bigserial, timestamps) give excellent insert locality — Postgres's B-tree fastpath (PG11+) caches and revalidates the rightmost leaf page, so monotonic appends usually avoid re-descending the tree and cause fewer page splits. But under the pipeline's concurrent writers (up to 16 connections) that one rightmost page becomes a write hot spot (buffer/lock contention).
    • Random keys (uuid v4) spread inserts across the whole index → no single hot page, but more page splits, worse cache locality, and index bloat.
    • Even distribution avoids skew and keeps point-lookups predictable; a time-ordered UUID v7 is a common middle ground. (Remember: rows with a NULL in the PK are dropped — §10.3 — and a timeseries key for duplicate PKs carries a dedup performance penalty.)
  • Plan connection usage. Each table sync opens up to 16 connections to the Lakebase instance, and they count against its connection limit (and, on the serving side, the ~1,000 concurrent-connection ceiling; §10.6, §10.7). Budget those 16-per-sync into capacity planning so refreshes don't starve your application's own connections, and front the app with a pooler (Part 4 §9).
  • Add Postgres indexes that match your query patterns. Synced-table data is read-only, but you may — and should — CREATE INDEX on it to serve your app's access paths (§10.9 authorizes the DDL; §10.10 lists it as allowed). Validate with EXPLAIN (ANALYZE, BUFFERS). Indexes add write amplification on every refresh, so index only the columns your serving queries actually filter/join/sort on.
  • Bundle several table syncs into one pipeline. Every synced table is backed by a managed Lakeflow pipeline with fixed overhead (compute + up to 16 connections). Where the product lets you group multiple tables under one pipeline / table group, do it: you amortize that per-pipeline overhead and connection footprint across tables instead of paying it N times — lower infrastructure cost and simpler scheduling.
👉 In short: Snapshot unless you need freshness; a PK that matches your reads and distributes writes; index for your query paths; and consolidate pipelines to keep the connection/compute overhead flat.

10.12. 🧭 Where synced tables sit among the Lakebase ↔ UC bridges

BridgeDirectionCopies data?In this doc
Synced tables (this part)UC → Lakebase (reverse ETL)✅ read-only PG copyPart 5 §18 (internals), §22 (big picture)
Lakebase CDF / forward ETLLakebase → UC✅ Delta history in UCPart 5 §17, §22
Registered database catalogUC window onto live Lakebase❌ live view, no copyPart 7
Query / Lakehouse FederationUC window onto an external DB❌ live, pushdown over JDBCPart 9
🧠 Mental rule. Synced table = a copy that flows into Lakebase and is kept fresh by a pipeline (you read it fast, you never write it). If instead you want a live window with no copy, that's a registered catalog (Part 7, same instance) or federation (Part 9, external DB). If you want to flow Postgres changes out to the lakehouse, that's CDF / forward ETL (Part 5 §17).

10.13. 🧒 Recap for a 10-year-old

The warehouse (lakehouse) has all your toys, perfectly counted — but it's far. Your bedside basket (Lakebase) holds just the toys you need right now, and a robot keeps copying the newest ones in. You tell the robot how eager to be: copy once, copy when I press the button, or copy all the time. You promise the robot two things: (1) every toy gets a name tag (the primary key) so it never puts two of the same toy in, and (2) you'll only take toys out, never sneak your own toys in — because the robot would toss them on the next trip. That's a synced table: a fast, always-fresh, read-only copy of the warehouse, right where your apps can grab it.

PART 11 — Lakebase Change Data Feed (CDF): streaming Postgres changes into the lakehouse ⏩

Track C · Lakebase ↔ Lakehouse integrations · 🟡 Intermediate · ▶ next: Part 9

This part is the hands-on, operational guide to Lakebase CDF — the Lakebase → UC (forward ETL) bridge that captures every INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE from the Postgres WAL and materializes it as an append-only Delta history table in Unity Catalog, flushed every ~15 s. It is the mirror twin of synced tables (Part 10, which flows the other way, UC → Lakebase). The engine internals (the wal2delta extension, its logical-decoding output plugin, the bgworker, the dataplane bridge) are in Part 5 §17, and the product view in §21; the forward vs. reverse ETL synthesis is in §22. This part is the how-to. Based on the Databricks doc: Lakebase Change Data Feed. (Public Preview at time of writing.)

11.0. 🧒 CDF for a 5-year-old

You have a toy diary 📔 (your Lakebase Postgres table). Every time you add a toy, swap a toy, or give one away, you want the whole family to know — but you don't want to keep shouting it.

So a tiny helper 🐿️ sits next to you and writes down every single change on a long roll of paper 📜 that lives in the family library (the lakehouse / Unity Catalog). It never erases anything — it only adds a new line for each change: "added a red car," "the blue ball became a green ball" (it writes both the before and the after), "gave away the teddy." Every ~15 seconds it tears off what it wrote and files it in the library.

  • CDF is that helper who keeps an append-only history of everything that happened to your table.
  • The family can read the roll to rebuild the current state ("how many toys do I have now?") or to audit ("what changed yesterday at 3pm?").
  • You never write on the roll yourself — the helper owns it, and it only grows.
The one-line idea: CDF captures every change to your Postgres table and appends it as Delta history in the lakehouse, ~every 15 seconds.
👦 Technical explanation. Lakebase CDF is change-data-capture built into Lakebase. The wal2delta extension uses logical decoding to read the write-ahead log and, for each committed INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE, appends a row to a Unity Catalog–managed Delta table named lb_<table>_history, batched and flushed every ~15 s. Each row carries the source columns plus CDC metadata — operation type (_pg_change_type), WAL position (_pg_lsn), transaction id (_pg_xid), a processing timestamp (_timestamp), and a monotonic ordering key (_sort_by). Updates emit two rows (a update_preimage = old state and a update_postimage = new state), which is why source tables need REPLICA IDENTITY FULL (so Postgres logs the whole old row, not just the PK). The history is append-only, so downstream materialized views, Lakeflow pipelines, and Structured Streaming jobs process only what changed. It's the forward-ETL direction — the exact mirror of synced tables (Part 10) — turning your operational database into a governed, incremental bronze source for the lakehouse with no replication pipeline to write.

11.1. 🎓 What Lakebase CDF is (the precise definition)

Lakebase CDF automatically records every insert, update, and delete on Lakebase Postgres tables by reading them from the write-ahead log (via the wal2delta extension's logical decoding) and storing each change as a new row in a Unity Catalog–managed Delta table, batched and flushed every ~15 seconds.
  • Schema-level scope. You enable CDF on a schema; it then covers all current and future tables in that schema.
  • It creates, per source table, a destination Delta table lb_<table_name>_history in the UC catalog/schema you pick.
  • Append-only. Nothing is ever updated in place — every change is a new row. That's what makes downstream incremental processing cheap.
  • Open format. The destination is a governed Delta table in Unity Catalog, so non-Databricks / external readers can consume it too.

11.2. 🧬 The history table: schema & change patterns

Each lb_<table>_history table contains all the source columns plus these system columns:

ColumnTypeMeaning
_pg_change_typeTEXTThe operation: insert, delete, update_preimage (row before), or update_postimage (row after).
_pg_lsnBIGINTPostgres Log Sequence Number — the WAL position of the change.
_pg_xidINTEGERPostgres transaction id that made the change. ⚠️ Stored as signed 32-bit (the engine reinterprets the u32 xid as i32), so xids above 2³¹ show up negative, and xids wrap around — treat it as a grouping key within a short window, not a durable global clock.
_timestampTIMESTAMPWhen the change was processed (no timezone).
_sort_byBIGINTA monotonic key that totally orders all changes — order by this, never by _pg_lsn alone.
Why _sort_by and not just _pg_lsn? An UPDATE emits its preimage and postimage at the same LSN, so _pg_lsn cannot order them. _sort_by adds a tie-break bit so the postimage always sorts after its preimage (verified in wal2delta's compute_sort_by). When you need per-row change ordering, sort by _sort_by.

The change patterns — memorize these, every query depends on them:

Source operationRows emitted into history
Initial snapshot (existing rows when CDF starts)one row per row, _pg_change_type = 'insert'
INSERTone row, insert
UPDATEtwo rows: update_preimage (old values) and update_postimage (new values)
DELETEone row, delete
🔑 The preimage/postimage pair is why REPLICA IDENTITY FULL is mandatory (§11.3): to log the complete old row, Postgres must include every column in the WAL — not just the primary key (its default).

11.3. ✅ Prerequisites

  • Lakebase Autoscaling project running Postgres 17.
  • Source tables must live in the databricks_postgres database (current limitation).
  • REPLICA IDENTITY FULL on every source table (§11.4) — without it the table won't appear in the feed.
  • Unity Catalog perms on the destination: the configuring identity needs USE CATALOG, USE SCHEMA, CREATE TABLE. The Postgres role needs CAN MANAGE on the Lakebase project (project owners have it).
  • Destination catalogs with default storage are not supported.
  • A workspace admin must enable the Lakebase Change Data Feed preview from the Previews page.

11.4. 🛠️ Setup

Set REPLICA IDENTITY FULL — one table:

ALTER TABLE <table_name> REPLICA IDENTITY FULL;

All existing tables in a schema (example public):

DO $$
DECLARE r record;
BEGIN
  FOR r IN
    SELECT table_schema, table_name
    FROM information_schema.tables
    WHERE table_schema = 'public' AND table_type = 'BASE TABLE'
  LOOP
    EXECUTE format('ALTER TABLE %I.%I REPLICA IDENTITY FULL;', r.table_schema, r.table_name);
  END LOOP;
END $$;

Auto-apply to future tables with an event trigger:

CREATE OR REPLACE FUNCTION public.set_full_replica_identity()
RETURNS event_trigger LANGUAGE plpgsql AS $$
DECLARE obj record;
BEGIN
  FOR obj IN SELECT * FROM pg_event_trigger_ddl_commands() WHERE command_tag = 'CREATE TABLE'
  LOOP
    EXECUTE format('ALTER TABLE %s REPLICA IDENTITY FULL;', obj.object_identity);
  END LOOP;
END $$;

CREATE EVENT TRIGGER set_full_replica_identity_on_create
  ON ddl_command_end WHEN TAG IN ('CREATE TABLE')
  EXECUTE FUNCTION public.set_full_replica_identity();

Verify what each table is set to:

SELECT n.nspname AS table_schema, c.relname AS table_name,
       CASE c.relreplident WHEN 'd' THEN 'default' WHEN 'n' THEN 'nothing'
                           WHEN 'f' THEN 'full'    WHEN 'i' THEN 'index' END AS replica_identity
FROM pg_class c JOIN pg_namespace n ON n.oid = c.relnamespace
WHERE c.relkind = 'r' AND n.nspname = 'public'
ORDER BY 1, 2;

Start CDF (UI): open Lakebase Postgres from the app switcher → select project + branch → Branch overview → Change Data Feed tab → Start → configure source (Database defaults to databricks_postgres; Schema) and destination (To Catalog; Schema) → Start. Destination tables appear as lb_<table_name>_history.

11.5. 📈 Monitoring & status

  • UI. The Schemas tab lists source schema → destination catalog/schema + status. The Tables tab shows destination name, status (Streaming or Snapshotting), Committed LSN, and Last update.
  • SQL (inside Postgres): SELECT * FROM wal2delta.tables; → table_oid | status (STREAMING | SNAPSHOTTING) | committed_lsn | last_write_time.

SNAPSHOTTING = initial full load in progress; STREAMING = steady-state incremental capture.

11.6. 🔎 Querying the change feed

Because the history is append-only with the change-type columns of §11.2, you reconstruct current state or process changes incrementally. All three patterns replay a lb_stock_movements_history CDF stream where each source row is a stock movement of a quantity (receipts arrive as insert, shipments as delete, corrections as update), using the intuitive sign convention — adding stock (insert/update_postimage) contributes +quantity, removing it (delete/update_preimage) contributes −quantity — so the running SUM is the on-hand quantity and stays ≥ 0 in a healthy feed. Three canonical patterns:

Pattern 1 — a materialized view for current state:

CREATE MATERIALIZED VIEW inventory_levels AS
SELECT item_id,
       SUM(CASE WHEN _pg_change_type IN ('insert','update_postimage') THEN  quantity
                WHEN _pg_change_type IN ('delete','update_preimage')  THEN -quantity
                ELSE 0 END) AS on_hand,
       MAX(_timestamp) AS last_movement_ts,
       MAX(_sort_by)   AS last_sort_by      -- order by _sort_by, never _pg_lsn alone (see §11.2)
FROM lb_stock_movements_history
GROUP BY item_id;

Pattern 2 — Lakeflow (Spark Declarative Pipelines):

import dlt
from pyspark.sql import functions as F

@dlt.table
def inventory_adjustments():
    return (spark.readStream.table("<catalog>.<schema>.lb_stock_movements_history")
        .withColumn("delta",
            F.when(F.col("_pg_change_type").isin("insert","update_postimage"),  F.col("quantity"))
             .when(F.col("_pg_change_type").isin("delete","update_preimage"),  -F.col("quantity"))
             .otherwise(0))
        .select("item_id","delta","_timestamp"))

@dlt.expect_or_drop("non_negative_stock", "on_hand >= 0")
@dlt.table
def inventory_levels():
    return (spark.read.table("LIVE.inventory_adjustments")
        .groupBy("item_id").agg(F.sum("delta").alias("on_hand")))

Pattern 3 — Structured Streaming with foreachBatch + Delta MERGE (assumes the target Delta table inventory_levels already exists):

from pyspark.sql import functions as F
from delta.tables import DeltaTable

def update_inventory(batch_df, batch_id):
    deltas = (batch_df
        .withColumn("delta",
            F.when(F.col("_pg_change_type").isin("insert","update_postimage"),  F.col("quantity"))
             .when(F.col("_pg_change_type").isin("delete","update_preimage"),  -F.col("quantity"))
             .otherwise(0))
        .groupBy("item_id").agg(F.sum("delta").alias("delta")))
    (DeltaTable.forName(spark, "<catalog>.<schema>.inventory_levels").alias("t")
        .merge(deltas.alias("s"), "t.item_id = s.item_id")
        .whenMatchedUpdate(set={"on_hand": F.expr("t.on_hand + s.delta")})
        .whenNotMatchedInsert(values={"item_id": "s.item_id", "on_hand": "s.delta"})
        .execute())

(spark.readStream.table("<catalog>.<schema>.lb_stock_movements_history")
    .writeStream.foreachBatch(update_inventory)
    .option("checkpointLocation", "/Volumes/<catalog>/<schema>/checkpoints/inventory_levels")
    .start())

11.7. 🔤 Type mapping (PostgreSQL → Delta)

PostgreSQLDeltaNotes
BOOLEANBOOLEAN
SMALLINT/INT/BIGINTSMALLINT/INT/BIGINT
TEXT/VARCHAR/CHARSTRING
FLOAT/DOUBLEFLOAT/DOUBLE
NUMERIC/DECIMALDECIMAL or STRINGuses source precision/scale; falls back to STRING if precision >38 or undefined
DATEDATE
TIMESTAMPTIMESTAMP_NTZ
TIMESTAMPTZTIMESTAMP
JSONBSTRINGJSON string
ENUMSTRINGenum label
PostGIS (geometry/geography), pgvector (vector), composite/hstore/mapSTRINGserialized

11.8. ⚙️ Operational behavior (the gotchas)

  • Naming collisions. If sales.users and marketing.users both map to lb_users_history, the first wins the name and later ones are auto-suffixed (lb_users_history_1, …). You may rename destination tables in UC without breaking the feed.
  • Empty tables are skipped until they have ≥1 row.
  • Dropping a source table in Postgres preserves its destination Delta table in UC.
  • Schema changes (add/drop column, change type) trigger a re-snapshot: CDF re-reads the whole table and rewrites the destination.
  • Renaming a source table (ALTER TABLE users RENAME TO customers) lets the feed continue; the destination name stays lb_users_history.

11.9. 🛑 Disabling CDF — and the data-gap warning

Disable via Lakebase Postgres → project/branch → Branch overview → Change Data Feed → Disable (confirm twice).

⚠️ Critical. Disabling stops the feed for all schemas in the project. If you re-enable later, CDF does not re-snapshot — any changes made while it was disabled are permanently missing from the destination Delta tables. (Disabling does not restart compute.)

11.10. 📊 Limitations & latency

  • Latency: changes flush ~every 15 s; downstream work is proportional to what changed (append-only Delta).
  • Partitioned tables are not supported — a partitioned table in the schema fails.
  • Empty tables are skipped until non-empty.
  • Default-storage destination catalogs are not supported.
  • Source tables must be in databricks_postgres.
  • A table without REPLICA IDENTITY FULL silently won't appear — check wal2delta.tables / the CDF tab.

11.11. 🧭 Where CDF sits among the Lakebase ↔ UC bridges

BridgeDirectionCopies data?In this doc
Lakebase CDF (this part)Lakebase → UC (forward ETL)✅ append-only Delta historyPart 5 §17 (engine), §21 (product), §22 (big picture)
Synced tablesUC → Lakebase (reverse ETL)✅ read-only PG copyPart 10, §18, §22
Registered database catalogUC window onto live Lakebase❌ live view, no copyPart 7
Query / Lakehouse FederationUC window onto an external DB❌ live, pushdown over JDBCPart 9
🧠 Mental rule. CDF flows changes out of Lakebase into governed Delta history (append-only, you read it downstream). Its exact twin, synced tables (Part 10), flows a governed table into Lakebase as a read-only copy. A live window with no copy is a registered catalog (Part 7) or federation (Part 9).

11.12. 🧒 Recap for a 10-year-old

Your Postgres table is a diary you keep changing. CDF is a helper who, every ~15 seconds, copies every change — added, swapped (writing both the before and the after), or removed — onto a never-erased roll of paper in the family library (the lakehouse). Later, anyone can read the roll to figure out what you have now or what changed and when — for reports, for machine learning, or just to keep an honest record. You don't write on the roll; the helper does, and it only ever grows. That's CDF: your database's changes, streamed as permanent, query-friendly history into the lakehouse.

Sources

  • Lakebase Change Data Feed — Databricks on AWS
  • Cross-references: Part 5 §17 (the wal2delta engine — output plugin, bgworker, dataplane bridge), §21 (CDF product view), §22 (forward vs. reverse ETL synthesis), Part 10 (synced tables — the reverse-ETL twin), Part 1 §10 / Part 5 §20 (WAL, LSN, logical decoding).
PART 12 — Schemas, objects, references & privileges (and how they map onto Unity Catalog) 🗂️🔐

Track B · Lakebase essentials · 🟢 Beginner→🟡 Intermediate · ▶ next: Part 6

Parts 4–11 covered how Lakebase runs and how it bridges to Unity Catalog. This part zooms into the inside of a single Postgres database: how it is organized (schemas and the objects they hold), how you name things (fully-qualified vs. unqualified references), and how it is secured (the ACL-based privilege model). At every step we compare with Unity Catalog (UC), because a Lakebase user constantly lives in both worlds: a three-level UC namespace on the outside, and a Postgres schema tree on the inside.

12.0. 🧒 Schemas, names and permissions for a 5-year-old

Imagine one big toy room 🏠 (that's a database). Inside the room there are several labeled boxes 📦: a "cars" box, a "dolls" box, a box the grown-ups keep the instructions in. Each box is a schema — a way to group toys so two different toys can even share the same name ("red" the car and "red" the doll) as long as they live in different boxes.

  • To ask for a toy exactly, you say the box and the toy: "the cars box → red". That's a fully-qualified name (cars.red).
  • If you're lazy you just shout "red!" and a helper looks through your boxes in a fixed order until it finds one. That's an unqualified name, and the order is the search path.
  • Not everyone can open every box. Each box (and each toy) has a little list on the lid saying who may look inside, who may add toys, who may take toys out. That list is the permissions (the ACL). The kid who made the box is the owner and can always change its list.
The one-line idea: a schema is a named box of objects inside one database; a name is "box.object" (or just "object" resolved by the search path); and every object carries an access-control list saying who may do what.
👦 Technical explanation. A schema is a namespace inside a database — a logical container for tables, views, sequences, functions, types and more. Names resolve either fully-qualified (schema.object) or unqualified (resolved against the session's search_path). Access is governed by a discretionary, ACL-based model: every object has an owner and an access-control list of (grantee, privileges, grantor) entries populated by GRANT/REVOKE. Unity Catalog wraps the same conceptual ideas — a namespace hierarchy plus GRANT-based securables — but with three fixed levels (catalog.schema.object), a metastore-wide admin, and centralized governance/lineage/audit instead of per-database aclitem arrays.

12.1. 📚 Schemas in Postgres — what they are and the full catalog of built-in ones

A schema is a namespace inside a single database. The hierarchy is:

  cluster (one Postgres instance)
   └─ database        e.g. "databricks_postgres"        ← you connect to exactly one
        └─ schema      e.g. "public", "pg_catalog"       ← namespaces inside it
             └─ object e.g. table "orders", view, func   ← the actual things

Two facts that trip people up:

  • You connect to one database at a time. Cross-database references (otherdb.public.t) are not allowed in a single query — that's what Federation/foreign tables are for. So the practical qualified name inside Postgres is two-part: schema.object.
  • Schemas let names repeat. sales.customers and hr.customers are two different tables. Without schemas every object would fight for one flat namespace.

The built-in schemas (present in every database):

SchemaPurposeNotes
publicThe default schema where objects land if you don't qualify them and haven't changed search_path.Historically world-writable; PostgreSQL 15+ locked this down — PUBLIC no longer gets CREATE on public by default (§12.5).
pg_catalogThe system catalog: every built-in table (pg_class, pg_attribute, pg_namespace, pg_proc, pg_type…), built-in functions, operators and data types.Always searched first, implicitly, even if not in search_path. Why count(), int4, = resolve unqualified.
information_schemaThe SQL-standard, portable metadata views (tables, columns, table_privileges, role_table_grants…).A standardized view over pg_catalog; use it for cross-DBMS portable introspection.
pg_temp (→ pg_temp_N)The current session's temporary objects. pg_temp aliases this backend's real pg_temp_<n>.Session-local; dropped at disconnect. Searched before other schemas for temp objects.
pg_toastStorage for TOASTed (oversized) column values stored out-of-line.System-managed; you never write here directly.
pg_toast_temp_NTOAST storage for temp tables.System-managed.
🎓 The implicit search order. Even with an empty search_path, Postgres always looks in pg_temp first (for temp objects), then pg_catalog. You can override this by putting pg_catalog explicitly later in search_path — rarely a good idea.

Schemas you'll additionally see in Lakebase (Postgres-on-Neon + Databricks extensions, Parts 4–5):

  • The default database is databricks_postgres; its default working schema is public.
  • Extension schemas — CREATE EXTENSION ... SCHEMA ... puts an extension's objects in its own schema. Lakebase-specific extensions (databricks_auth, wal2delta, reverse_etl_worker, neon; Part 5) register catalog objects/functions in pg_catalog or their own schema.
  • Synced-table target schemas — a synced table (Part 10) lands its read-only Postgres table in a schema you choose (e.g. gold.user_profiles_synced), an ordinary Postgres schema maintained by a managed pipeline.
  • pg_stat_statements and other observability objects if enabled.
⚠️ Reminder from Part 7: the Postgres database name feeding a UC catalog must be alphanumeric/underscore (no hyphens). Schema names follow normal Postgres rules; when reflected into UC they are lowercased, and any schema/table whose name isn't a legal UC identifier is ignored (Part 9 §9.6 / Part 7).

12.2. 🧱 Schema objects — what actually lives in a schema (and what does not)

These object kinds are schema-scoped (fully-qualified name schema.name):

Object kindWhat it isCatalog table
Tables (ordinary, partitioned, foreign, unlogged, temp)The row storage.pg_class (relkind r/p/f/t…)
Views / materialized viewsA stored query / a stored and materialized query.pg_class (v / m)
SequencesAuto-increment counters (SERIAL/IDENTITY back-ends).pg_class (S)
IndexesAccess structures on a table (B-tree, GIN, GiST, BRIN…).pg_class (i) + pg_index
Functions / proceduresCallable routines (SQL, PL/pgSQL, C…).pg_proc
Data types & domainsUser-defined types, enums, composites, domains.pg_type
Operators / opclasses / familiesCustom operators and index support.pg_operator, pg_opclass
Aggregates & window functionsCustom SUM-like routines.pg_aggregate / pg_proc
TriggersFunctions fired on table events.pg_trigger (attached to a table)
Collations, conversions, FTS configsLocale/encoding/full-text-search objects.pg_collation, etc.

Global (cluster-wide) — NOT inside any schema:

Object kindScopeCatalog table
Roles / users / groupsWhole cluster — shared across all databases.pg_authid / pg_roles
DatabasesCluster.pg_database
TablespacesCluster.pg_tablespace
Replication slots, subscriptions (partly), settingsCluster / DB level.various
🔑 The single most important consequence: roles are cluster-global, objects and their grants are database/schema-local. You grant a global role privileges on a local object. (In Lakebase a Databricks identity maps to a Postgres role you create manually — no automatic sync; Part 4 §8.)

12.3. 🧭 Object references — fully-qualified vs. unqualified (and the search_path)

Fully-qualified references. Inside Postgres, the practical fully-qualified name is two-part: schema.object.

SELECT * FROM sales.orders;          -- schema-qualified: unambiguous, no search_path needed
SELECT public.now();                 -- schema-qualified function call

The SQL standard defines a three-part name catalog.schema.object, and Postgres accepts the database. prefix syntactically only when it equals the current database — you still cannot query across databases in one statement. So although you can write databricks_postgres.sales.orders, you cannot write otherdb.sales.orders. Cross-database/system reach is exactly what Federation and foreign tables exist for (Part 9).

Unqualified references and the search_path. An unqualified name (SELECT * FROM orders) is resolved by walking the search_path — an ordered list of schemas — and taking the first schema that contains a matching object.

SHOW search_path;                    -- typically: "$user", public
SET search_path TO sales, public;    -- session-level override
  • "$user" is a special entry meaning "a schema named like the current role"; if no such schema exists it's skipped.
  • pg_catalog is always searched first (implicitly), and pg_temp before that for temp objects — regardless of search_path.
  • Object creation (CREATE TABLE foo) lands in the first non-temp schema in the path (the creation schema), normally public.
🚩 Security footgun: a mutable search_path is a classic attack surface — a malicious object in an earlier schema can shadow a trusted one. Best practice for SECURITY DEFINER functions is to SET search_path explicitly (e.g. SET search_path = pg_catalog, pg_temp) so name resolution can't be hijacked.

How this compares with Unity Catalog references. UC uses a fixed three-level namespace and there is no search_path — resolution is governed by USE CATALOG/USE SCHEMA, not an ordered search list.

AspectPostgreSQLUnity Catalog
Full name shapeschema.object (2-part in practice; 3-part only if DB prefix = current DB)catalog.schema.object (3-part, always)
Cross-container query❌ Not across databases (use Federation)✅ Across catalogs/schemas within a metastore
Unqualified resolutionOrdered search_path (first match wins) + implicit pg_catalog/pg_tempCurrent catalog + current schema set by USE …; no search list
"Default landing" for new objectsFirst non-temp schema in search_path (usually public)The current schema after USE CATALOG x; USE SCHEMA y
Name collisionsAllowed across schemas; search_path picks oneAllowed across schemas; disambiguate with the 3-part name
Case handlingCase-sensitive if quoted; folded to lower-case otherwiseUC identifiers lowercased; illegal identifiers ignored when reflecting Postgres
🧩 The mental bridge (Part 7 again): when a Lakebase database is registered in UC, the mapping is instance → (database ↦ catalog) → schema ↦ schema → table ↦ table. So the Postgres two-part sales.orders becomes the UC three-part my_catalog.sales.orders. The catalog level is essentially "which Postgres database", injected by UC.

12.4. 🔐 The PostgreSQL privilege model — ACL-based, discretionary security

PostgreSQL's security is a discretionary access control (DAC) model built on three pillars: owners, privileges, and an access-control list (ACL) per object.

12.4.1 Owners. Every object has exactly one owner (initially its creator). The owner (and superusers, and members of the owning role) holds all privileges implicitly and can DROP/ALTER/GRANT on the object. The owner's rights cannot be revoked away — you change them via ALTER … OWNER TO or by dropping the object.

12.4.2 Privileges (the verbs), per object type. GRANT/REVOKE operate on a fixed set of privilege types that differ by object kind:

ObjectPrivileges
Table / viewSELECT, INSERT, UPDATE, DELETE, TRUNCATE, REFERENCES, TRIGGER (and column-level SELECT (col), INSERT (col), UPDATE (col))
SchemaUSAGE (look inside / resolve names), CREATE (create objects in it)
DatabaseCONNECT, CREATE (create schemas), TEMPORARY (create temp tables)
SequenceUSAGE, SELECT, UPDATE
Function / procedureEXECUTE
Type / domainUSAGE
Tablespace / FDW / foreign server / languageCREATE / USAGE as applicable
🔑 The two-lock rule people forget: to read sales.orders a role needs both USAGE on the schema sales and SELECT on the table. Granting table SELECT alone is useless if the role can't USAGE the schema.

12.4.3 The ACL — how a grant is actually stored. Every grantable object has an aclitem[] column (e.g. pg_class.relacl, pg_namespace.nspacl, pg_proc.proacl). Each entry encodes one grant:

  grantee=privileges/grantor
  ───────  ──────────  ───────
  role     verbs       who granted it

  Examples:
    alice=arwdDxt/bob     -- alice has a,r,w,d,D,x,t on this table, granted by bob
    =r/bob                -- an empty grantee means PUBLIC has SELECT (r)
    bob=arwdDxt*/bob      -- the * means "WITH GRANT OPTION" (bob may re-grant)

Privilege letters: r=SELECT, a=INSERT, w=UPDATE, d=DELETE, D=TRUNCATE, x=REFERENCES, t=TRIGGER, U=USAGE, C=CREATE, c=CONNECT, T=TEMPORARY, X=EXECUTE. A trailing * means grant option. A NULL acl means default privileges (owner has everything, PUBLIC has whatever the default is for that object type).

\dp sales.orders                              -- psql: show the ACL in a table
SELECT relname, relacl FROM pg_class WHERE relname = 'orders';
SELECT * FROM information_schema.role_table_grants WHERE table_name = 'orders';

12.4.4 Roles, membership and inheritance.

  • Roles are the unified concept for users and groups (a role with LOGIN is a "user"; one used as a container is a "group"). They are cluster-global.
  • Membership: GRANT sales_reader TO alice; makes alice a member of sales_reader.
  • Inheritance: with INHERIT (default), a member automatically uses the group's privileges. With NOINHERIT, the member must SET ROLE sales_reader to use them.
  • PUBLIC is an implicit pseudo-role meaning every role. Granting to PUBLIC grants to all.

12.4.5 Default privileges & PUBLIC hardening.

  • ALTER DEFAULT PRIVILEGES pre-declares grants for future objects: e.g. "every table Alice creates in sales is automatically SELECT-able by analysts." Existing objects are unaffected.
  • PostgreSQL 15+ removed the historical CREATE on public for PUBLIC and made the public schema owned so arbitrary users can't create objects there — a major hardening change Lakebase inherits.
  • PUBLIC also has CONNECT + TEMPORARY on new databases and EXECUTE on new functions by default — often worth revoking in locked-down setups.

12.4.6 Row-Level Security (RLS). Beyond table-level grants, ALTER TABLE … ENABLE ROW LEVEL SECURITY + CREATE POLICY filters which rows a role can see/modify — a finer, predicate-based layer on top of the ACL. (UC's analogue is row filters & column masks.)

12.4.7 In Lakebase specifically.

  • Databricks identities are not auto-synced to Postgres roles — you CREATE ROLE and GRANT manually (Part 4 §8; role_creation.c/user_management.c in Part 5).
  • Authentication is via short-lived OAuth tokens (used as the password) or native role passwords; but authorization inside the DB is still ordinary Postgres ACLs.
  • The engine adds guardrails: online_table_permissions.c and sql_restrictions.c (Part 5 §8) constrain what managed/synced objects allow — e.g. synced tables are read-only to you.
  • Superuser is not handed out in the managed model; you get a highly-privileged but bounded role (Part 4 §3).

12.5. ⚖️ PostgreSQL ACLs vs. Unity Catalog governance — the deep comparison

Both are GRANT-based, but they differ in scope, storage, identity source, and blast radius.

DimensionPostgreSQL (ACL model)Unity Catalog
Model typeDiscretionary (DAC): object owner decides.Discretionary grants + centralized metastore governance, lineage, audit, tags, ABAC-style row/column controls.
Securable hierarchycluster → database → schema → object (+ columns, rows via RLS)metastore → catalog → schema → table/view/volume/function/model (+ columns, rows)
Where grants liveaclitem[] arrays on each catalog row (relacl, nspacl…), per database.Central metastore (a governed control-plane store), one source of truth across workspaces.
Identity sourceCluster-global roles (pg_authid); users and groups are "roles".Account-level users, service principals & groups (SCIM/IdP-synced).
Grant verbsObject-specific: SELECT/INSERT/…, USAGE, CREATE, EXECUTE, CONNECT…USE CATALOG, USE SCHEMA, SELECT, MODIFY, CREATE …, EXECUTE, READ VOLUME, etc.
"Two-lock" traversalNeed USAGE on schema + privilege on object.Need USE CATALOG + USE SCHEMA + the object privilege — the same idea, three levels.
OwnershipOne owner role; owner has implicit full rights; change via ALTER … OWNER.One owner (user/group/SP); owner + metastore admin manage grants; ALTER … OWNER TO.
InheritanceRole membership + INHERIT; PUBLIC = everyone.Group nesting; privileges inherit down the hierarchy (grant on catalog ⇒ applies to its schemas/tables) — Postgres has no such downward cascade.
Admin of last resortSuperuser (bypasses ACLs). Not exposed in managed Lakebase.Metastore admin / account admin (no OS-level superuser concept).
Introspection\dp, pg_class.relacl, information_schema.*_privileges.SHOW GRANTS, Catalog Explorer, information_schema in UC, system tables.
Fine-grainedRLS policies + column privileges.Row filters + column masks + tags/ABAC.
Cross-system reachNone (single DB).Governs Delta, Iceberg, foreign catalogs, volumes, models, and the registered Lakebase catalog.
Audit / lineageNot built in (needs pgaudit, logs).Built in — every access logged; table/column lineage automatic.

The key conceptual differences to internalize:

  1. Downward inheritance. In UC, a grant on a catalog flows to its schemas and tables. In Postgres, a grant on a schema does not automatically grant on its tables — you grant each object (or use ALTER DEFAULT PRIVILEGES for future ones). This is the biggest behavioral gap.
  2. Where the truth lives. Postgres grants are inside each database. UC grants live in a central metastore spanning workspaces — so revoking access is one place, not per-database ACL surgery.
  3. Two gates when you register (Part 7/9). Reading a Lakebase table through a UC catalog is checked by UC grants first — a user who lacks USE CATALOG/SELECT in UC is blocked even if the underlying Postgres role could see the data. Connecting directly to Postgres, only the Postgres ACL applies. Same data, two independent gatekeepers depending on the door you use.
  4. Identity plumbing. UC identities come from the account IdP/SCIM; Postgres roles are local and, in Lakebase, created manually with no auto-sync — so a person can exist in UC yet have no Postgres role until you make one.

12.6. 🧒 Recap for a 10-year-old

Your database is a big room. Inside are labeled boxes (schemas) — a system box the grown-ups own (pg_catalog), a "questions in standard words" box (information_schema), your own play box (public), and a scratch box that gets emptied when you leave (pg_temp).

To grab a toy exactly you say "box → toy" (sales.orders). If you just shout the toy's name, a helper checks your boxes in a set order (the search path) and hands you the first match.

Every box and every toy has a sticker on the lid listing who may look, who may add, who may take away — that's the permissions list (ACL). The kid who built it (the owner) can always rewrite the sticker. To actually get a toy you need two oks: permission to open the box and permission to take the toy.

Unity Catalog is the whole house's rulebook: it has three levels (house → box → toy), one big list of rules that covers every room, and if a grown-up says "you may use this whole box" it counts for all the toys inside — something the Postgres room doesn't do on its own. And when you peek into the Postgres room through the house rulebook, the house's rules decide first.

Sources

  • Schemas — PostgreSQL documentation
  • The system catalog schema pg_catalog · information_schema
  • Privileges — PostgreSQL documentation
  • GRANT · REVOKE · ALTER DEFAULT PRIVILEGES
  • Database roles & membership · Row Security Policies
  • Unity Catalog privileges & securable objects — Databricks on AWS
  • Unity Catalog object model — Databricks on AWS
  • Cross-references: Part 4 §8 (roles ↔ Databricks identities, no auto-sync), Part 5 §8 (databricks_auth, online_table_permissions.c, sql_restrictions.c), Part 7 (registering the DB in UC — the two-gate governance), Part 9 §9.6/§9.9 (federation namespace, layered governance).
PART 13 — Lakebase versions: Provisioned (v1) vs. Autoscaling (v2) 🏷️

Track B · Lakebase essentials · 🟢 Beginner→🟡 Intermediate · ▶ next: Part 14

Throughout this document you've seen Lakebase referred to in two ways: sometimes as a Provisioned instance (Part 7), sometimes as a project/branch served by an Autoscaling pipeline (Parts 10–11). That's not sloppy wording — Lakebase genuinely ships in two platform generations, and Databricks is in the middle of unifying them into one. This part explains what each generation is, how they differ, the technical machinery behind the difference, and the migration story.
⚠️ Internal/timeline note. The version framing (v1/v2), the migration dates and the CU mapping below come from the Databricks docs (Upgrade to Lakebase Autoscaling) and internal planning material (PRD Lakebase v1 → v2 unification, Integrating with Lakebase Autoscaling). Dates and numeric limits are a moving target — verify against the current docs for your workspace before relying on them.

13.0. 🧒 Two versions of Lakebase for a 5-year-old

Imagine you rent a toy machine 🧸 that makes toys for your friends.

  • The old machine (Provisioned / v1) is one you must set to a fixed size before you turn it on. Pick "medium" and it always runs at medium — even at 3 a.m. when nobody's playing — and you pay for medium the whole time. If a huge crowd shows up, it can't grow on its own; you have to stop it, pick a bigger size, and start it again.
  • The new machine (Autoscaling / v2) is smart: it grows when lots of friends come, shrinks when few come, and even turns itself off when nobody's around (so you pay almost nothing) — then wakes up in a blink when the next friend arrives. It can also make instant copies of itself for you to experiment on (branches).
The one-line idea: v1 (Provisioned) is a fixed-size Postgres you pay for 24/7; v2 (Autoscaling) is a serverless Postgres that scales up, down and to zero — and adds branching and snapshots.
👦 Technical explanation. Lakebase v1 ("Provisioned") is the pre-Neon-acquisition architecture: a managed Postgres of fixed capacity (you pick a CU size; it runs 24/7 until you manually stop it). Lakebase v2 ("Autoscaling") is the current architecture, built on the full Neon platform (the hadron engine of Part 5 — separated compute/storage, safekeepers, pageservers). It adds compute autoscaling, scale-to-zero, fast cold starts, copy-on-write branching, and snapshots/PITR. The two also differ in their object model (flat instance vs. nested project → branch → endpoint), their APIs (w.database_instances.* / /database/* vs. w.postgres_projects.* / /postgres/*), and their pricing (fixed vs. usage-based). Databricks is converging the fleet onto v2 while keeping the v1 APIs and connection strings working via a "hybrid instance" bridge.

13.1. 🏛️ The two generations (the precise definitions)

v1 — "Provisioned"v2 — "Autoscaling"
LineageLegacy architecture, before the Neon acquisition.Next-gen architecture, built on the full Neon platform (the engine of Part 5).
ComputeFixed size (a CU you choose). Runs continuously until manually stopped.Elastic — autoscales within a [min CU, max CU] range; scale-to-zero when idle.
Branching / snapshots❌ Not supported.✅ Copy-on-write branches, snapshots, and PITR (Part 4 §6, Part 5 §14).
Cold startN/A (always on unless stopped).Fast wake from zero (warm pools + Neon's separated storage).
Object modelFlat: a single Database Instance.Nested: Project → Branch → Endpoint (§13.3).
SDK / APIw.database_instances.* · REST under /database/*.w.postgres_projects.* · REST under /postgres/*.
PricingFixed 24/7 per CU.Usage-based (elastic compute) + a small always-on baseline.
CU definition1 CU = 2 CPU / 16 GB RAM; values CU_1…CU_8.1 CU = 0.25 CPU / 2 GB RAM; values 0.5, 1, 2 … 64 CU (min/max range, with max − min ≤ 16 CU).
Connection stringGlobal: instance-<uuid>.database.cloud.databricks.com.Regional, per-endpoint: ep-<adjective>-<noun>-<hash>.database.<region>.cloud.databricks.com.
🔑 The customer-facing names are "Provisioned" (v1) and "Autoscaling" (v2). Internally they are the v1 platform and the v2 platform; the third term, "hybrid instance," is the bridge state (§13.5): an object created/managed through v1 APIs but physically hosted and executed on v2.

13.2. ⚙️ How each one works under the hood

v1 — Provisioned (fixed capacity). A Provisioned instance is a managed Postgres pinned to a chosen capacity unit:

  • Capacity is one of CU_1 / CU_2 / CU_4 / CU_8, where 1 CU = 2 vCPU + 16 GB RAM (so CU_8 = 128 GB).
  • Lifecycle is a coarse state machine (STARTING → AVAILABLE → UPDATING …). Creating an instance takes O(minutes) — a new "dblet" node/pod is provisioned. Resizing the CU or (re)starting is also O(minutes).
  • Always-on billing. You are charged for compute until you explicitly stop the instance; a stopped instance must be explicitly restarted before it can serve queries.
  • Read replicas are configured by enabling readable replicas and setting a node count > 1; a single read_only_dns load-balances reads across them with no per-endpoint control.
  • No branching, no snapshots, no scale-to-zero.
  v1 Provisioned  (flat)
  ─────────────────────
        Database Instance  "my-instance"   ← fixed size CU_1..CU_8, 24/7
          ├─ read_write_dns  → primary endpoint
          └─ read_only_dns   → load-balanced across N read replicas

v2 — Autoscaling (serverless, on the Neon platform). v2 runs on the separated compute/storage engine detailed in Part 5 (safekeepers make the WAL durable; pageservers reconstruct pages from an image + WAL deltas; the compute is a NeonVM that can scale live). That architecture is what makes the v2 features possible:

  • Autoscaling compute. An endpoint has a [min CU, max CU] range and scales within it based on load. 1 CU = 0.25 vCPU + 2 GB RAM; allowed 0.5…64 CU (capped lower during previews/free beta). Set min = max to effectively pin the size (disable autoscaling).
  • Scale-to-zero. When idle, compute suspends to zero (no compute bill); the next query wakes it automatically. New projects default to scale-to-zero on with a 24-hour inactivity timeout.
  • Fast operations. Because storage is separate and compute comes from warm pools, create/update is O(seconds) (vs. O(minutes) for v1), and cold start from zero is quick.
  • Branching & snapshots. A branch is an instant copy-on-write clone that shares storage with its parent up to the branch point (Part 4 §6 / Part 5 §14–15); snapshots + PITR fall out of the same Key × LSN storage model (Part 8 §3).

13.3. 🧬 The v2 object model — Project → Branch → Endpoint

The single biggest structural change: v1's flat instance becomes a three-level hierarchy in v2.

  v2 Autoscaling  (nested)
  ────────────────────────
  Database Project   "my-project"              ← the root managed Postgres
    ├─ Branch  "production"  (root, no parent)  ← isolated env; shares storage to the branch point
    │    ├─ Endpoint (read-write)   ep-snowy-pond-x1q2  ← exactly ONE R/W endpoint per branch
    │    └─ Endpoint (read-only)    ep-…              ← zero or more RO endpoints
    └─ Branch  "dev-pr-123"  (child of production)     ← copy-on-write clone (branching)
         └─ Endpoint (read-write)   ep-…
  • Project — the root managed Postgres instance; the top-level securable/billing object.
  • Branch — an isolated environment inside a project that shares storage with its parent up to the branch point. Every project has exactly one root branch (no parent); child branches are the copy-on-write clones.
  • Endpoint — a compute endpoint on a branch. One read-write endpoint per branch, plus any number of read-only endpoints. The endpoint (not the project) carries the connection host — to connect, you list the project's branches, then a branch's endpoints, and read the host off the endpoint you want.
🧩 The mapping that makes migration possible: a v1 Database Instance is exactly a v2 Project with a single fixed root branch and no ability to create child branches. That 1:1 correspondence is why a Provisioned instance can be lifted onto the v2 platform without changing its identity (§13.5).

13.4. 🔢 The CU mapping (v1 size → v2 range)

Because the CU unit is redefined between generations (16 GB/CU in v1 vs. 2 GB/CU in v2), an upgrade maps each fixed v1 size to a v2 min/max range that brackets the same memory:

v1 Provisionedv2 min CUv2 max CU
CU_1 (16 GB)4 (8 GB)8 (16 GB)
CU_2 (32 GB)8 (16 GB)16 (32 GB)
CU_8 (128 GB)48 (96 GB)64 (128 GB)
The upgraded instance can now scale down (saving cost when idle) and scale up to its old ceiling — where before it was pinned at a single point. Scale-to-zero is not auto-enabled on upgraded instances (only on brand-new v2 projects); enable it manually if you want it.

13.5. 🔀 The unification & migration path (v1 → v2)

Databricks is collapsing to a single platform (v2) while keeping v1 APIs/automation working. The bridge is the hybrid instance: an object created/managed through v1 APIs (SDK, Terraform, DABs) but physically running on v2. The rollout (dates aspirational — verify current docs):

  v1 API call ──► (interop) ──► creates/upgrades a HYBRID object ──► runs on v2 platform
  ▲                                                                         │
  └── your existing connection strings / Terraform / DABs keep working ─────┘
MilestoneWhat happens
Interop modev1 APIs stop creating true-v1 objects and instead create hybrid objects on v2. Terraform/SDK/DABs continue unchanged.
Self-serve upgradeA "Migrate to Autoscaling" button upgrades an existing instance with a seconds-long interruption.
Auto-upgradeRemaining v1 instances are automatically migrated to v2 to unify the fleet.
Regional connection stringsCustomers move from the global ingress host to regional per-endpoint hosts (better performance).
v1 API deprecation (aspirational)Once usage nears zero, v1 APIs are retired in favor of native v2 (/postgres/*) integrations.

What is preserved across the upgrade: all schemas, tables, indexes, roles and grants (Part 12), your connection strings, API calls, Terraform and DABs — connections just restart briefly. The instance name becomes the project_id; the root branch is created automatically.

What changes or has caveats:

  • New features live only on the v2 APIs. To create branches, tune autoscaling, or manage endpoints, use w.postgres_projects.* / /postgres/* (and the databricks_postgres_project Terraform resource).
  • After upgrade, both API families work on that object; brand-new v2 projects created via the UI/Postgres API are reachable only through the v2 APIs.
  • Limits v2 introduces: 500 Postgres roles and 500 databases per branch (v1 had no such cap — an over-limit instance upgrades fine but can't add more); the history/restore window is 30 days on v2 (v1's restore window was 35 days).
  • Postgres version: new v2 projects default to Postgres 17; the v1 Database-instance API path stays on Postgres 16 for compatibility.
  • Some Private-Preview features differ: e.g. Forward ETL / a PostgREST-style REST API on v1 are replaced by Lakebase CDF (Part 11) and the Data API on v2. Certain integrations (e.g. Apps database bindings that only accept a v1 instance_name) may need updating.

13.6. 🧭 Which one am I on? (quick tells)

  • Connection host — instance-<uuid>.database.cloud.databricks.com → v1; ep-<word>-<word>-<hash>.database.<region>.cloud.databricks.com → v2.
  • SDK namespace — w.database_instances.* / w.database.* → v1 surface; w.postgres_projects.* / w.postgres.* → v2 surface. (This is the "two SDK namespaces" note in Part 10 §10.4.)
  • Can you create a branch or see scale-to-zero settings? → you're on v2 (or a hybrid on v2).
  • Terraform resource — databricks_database_instance → v1; databricks_postgres_project → v2.

13.7. 🧒 Recap for a 10-year-old

Lakebase comes in two models. The old one (Provisioned) is a toy machine you set to one size; it runs all day at that size and you pay for all day, and if too many friends come it can't grow by itself. The new one (Autoscaling) is a smart machine: it grows when it's busy, shrinks when it's quiet, naps to save money when nobody's playing and wakes up instantly, and can make instant copies for you to try things on. Databricks is quietly swapping everyone's old machine for the new one — without unplugging anything you already set up — so eventually there's just one Lakebase, the smart one.

Sources

  • Upgrade to Lakebase Autoscaling — Databricks on AWS
  • Lakebase Postgres (projects overview) — Databricks on AWS · How database projects are organized
  • Internal (Databricks-only): PRD Lakebase v1 → v2 unification; Integrating with Lakebase Autoscaling; Lakebase: FAQ [INTERNAL ONLY].
  • Cross-references: Part 4 §7 (compute/storage autoscaling), Part 4 §6 / Part 5 §14–15 (branching), Part 5 (the Neon engine that powers v2), Part 8 §3 (Key × LSN storage behind snapshots/PITR), Part 10 §10.4 (the two SDK namespaces).
PART 14 — Lakebase connection methods: the PostgreSQL driver & the Data API 🔌

Track B · Lakebase essentials · 🟢 Beginner→🟡 Intermediate · ▶ next: Part 12

Part 4 §8 showed authentication (OAuth tokens vs. native passwords). This part answers the next question: once you're authenticated, how does your code actually talk to the database? There are two very different transports — the PostgreSQL wire protocol (a real driver, port 5432) and the Lakebase Data API (SQL-over-HTTP, port 443) — plus the built-in Databricks surfaces (SQL editor, notebooks). We explain each, when to pick it, and the technical machinery behind both, with a dedicated deep-dive on connection pooling for the driver and on the authenticator/role-assumption model for the Data API.
⚠️ Accuracy note. Endpoint shapes, SDK versions and limits evolve. The driver/pooling material is standard Postgres plus the Databricks docs; the Data API is PostgREST-compatible (built on the v2 Autoscaling platform). Verify specifics against the current docs for your workspace.

14.0. 🧒 Two doors into the database, for a 5-year-old

Your database lives in a house 🏠. There are two doors to get your questions answered:

  • The phone line ☎️ (the PostgreSQL driver). You call once, the line stays open, and you can chat back and forth as long as you like — fast, and you can say complicated things ("do all these steps, and if any fails, undo everything"). But keeping a phone line open costs a slot, so a smart house shares a few open lines among many callers (a connection pool).
  • The mail slot 📮 (the Data API). You post a letter with your question, a helper reads it, answers, and posts a letter back. No line to keep open — perfect if you only ask now and then, or if you're calling from very far away (a browser, a tiny serverless function) where keeping a line open is a pain.
The one-line idea: use the driver (a live SQL connection) for real apps and transactions; use the Data API (SQL over HTTP) for stateless, occasional, or browser/edge callers who don't want to manage connections.
👦 Technical explanation. Lakebase exposes the native Postgres wire protocol on :5432 (any Postgres driver — psql, psycopg, JDBC, node-pg, Go pgx …), authenticated with SSL where the password is an OAuth token. It also exposes a PostgREST-compatible Data API — SQL over HTTPS — where each table becomes a REST resource and auth is a Bearer OAuth token. The driver gives you sessions, transactions, prepared statements and pooling; the Data API gives you a stateless request/response with no connection to manage (ideal for serverless/edge/browser), at the cost of per-request HTTP overhead and RLS-based security. Both ride the same proxy → wake_compute → backend path from Part 5 §19 — one over the Postgres protocol, one over HTTP (serverless/http_sql.rs).

14.1. 🗺️ The full menu of connection methods

MethodTransport / portBest forNotes
PostgreSQL driver (psql, psycopg, JDBC, pg, pgx, DBeaver, pgAdmin…)Postgres wire, :5432, SSLReal applications, transactions, high throughput, poolingPassword = OAuth token (or native role). §14.2
Lakebase Data API (PostgREST)HTTPS :443, REST/JSONServerless, edge, browsers, occasional/stateless callersBearer OAuth token; RLS-driven security. §14.3
Databricks SQL editorWeb UI (server-side driver)Ad-hoc SQL, admin, explorationIn the Lakebase app; no local setup.
Databricks notebooksDriver from the clusterData science, pipelines, ETL glueSame driver pattern as §14.2 (SQLAlchemy + psycopg).
Databricks Apps / model servingDriver (often via a helper pool)App back-ends on the platformM2M service principal; e.g. @databricks/lakebase pool (§14.2).
                         ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────┐
   your code             │              Lakebase entry                 │
   ─────────             │                                             │
   psql / psycopg / ─────┼─▶ :5432  Postgres wire ─┐                   │
   JDBC / pg / pgx       │                          │                  │
                         │                          ├─▶ proxy (SNI) ──▶ wake_compute ──▶ Postgres backend
   fetch / curl / ───────┼─▶ :443   HTTPS (REST) ───┘   (Part 5 §19)      (Part 5–6)
   browser / lambda      │      Data API (PostgREST)                    │
                         └─────────────────────────────────────────────┘
        two transports, one engine — the Data API runs SQL against the same backend as the driver

14.2. 🐘 Using a PostgreSQL driver

PostgreSQL connection overview. A driver speaks the Postgres frontend/backend wire protocol over a TCP socket. The lifecycle (detailed in Part 2 §4 and Part 6):

  1. TCP + TLS. The client opens a socket to the endpoint host on :5432 and negotiates SSL (sslmode=require at minimum). In Lakebase the connection first hits the proxy (pg_sni_router), which routes by SNI to the right compute and, if it was scaled to zero, wakes it (Part 5 §19).
  2. Startup + auth. The client sends a startup packet (user, database); the server authenticates. For Lakebase the password is the OAuth token (or a native role password). On success the postmaster fork()s a dedicated backend process for this connection (Part 1 §10.1, Part 3 §2).
  3. Session. You now have a stateful session: run queries, use transactions (BEGIN/COMMIT), prepared statements, SET session GUCs, temp tables, LISTEN/NOTIFY, COPY, etc.
  4. Close. On disconnect the backend exits.
ParameterValue
hostThe endpoint host — v1: instance-<uuid>.database.cloud.databricks.com; v2: ep-<word>-<word>-<hash>.database.<region>.cloud.databricks.com (Part 13). From the SDK: instance.read_write_dns (or a read-only endpoint).
port5432
dbnamedatabricks_postgres (default)
userYour Postgres role (mapped from a Databricks identity — no auto-sync, Part 4 §8 / Part 12 §12.4.7)
passwordThe OAuth token (TTL ~1 h) or a native role password
sslmoderequire (or stricter)
# psql — the password is the OAuth token
export PGPASSWORD="$(databricks database generate-database-credential \
  --request-id $(uuidgen) --json '{"instance_names":["my-instance"]}' | jq -r .token)"
psql "host=<HOST> port=5432 dbname=databricks_postgres user=<pg_role> sslmode=require"
🔑 Why the token is special for connections. The ~1 h TTL is checked at login, not during the session — an already-open connection keeps working after the token expires (Part 4 §8). This is the fact that shapes pooling: long-lived connections are fine; the danger is minting new connections with a stale token.

Establishing a connection pool. Because every backend is a process (Part 3 §0), opening a connection per request is expensive — and with scale-to-zero and 1 h tokens there are extra wrinkles. A connection pool keeps a small set of warm connections and hands them out. Three rules specific to Lakebase:

  1. Reuse long-lived connections — amortize the fork + TLS + wake cost; keep the pool warm.
  2. Refresh the token before opening new connections — the pool must fetch a fresh OAuth token (or rotate the password) so new physical connections don't fail auth. Existing ones are unaffected.
  3. Size sanely — the concurrent-connection ceiling is version-dependent: up to ~4,000 on Autoscaling (v2, scales with compute size — ~3,993 at 64 CU) / ~1,000 on Provisioned (v1) (Part 15 §15.2); use a pooler (PgBouncer or the driver/app pool), and remember COMMIT latency depends on the safekeeper quorum (Part 4 §9) — batch writes.
   Connection pool  (N warm, long-lived connections)
   ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
     app request ─▶ [ borrow conn ] ─▶ run query ─▶ [ return conn ]     ← never re-auths
                         ▲
     token refresher ────┘  every ~15 min (or on `expiration_time`):
                            mint a fresh OAuth token, update the pool's password
                            → only *new* physical connections use it

Python — SQLAlchemy + psycopg (v3), with token refresh:

from sqlalchemy import create_engine, event, text
from databricks.sdk import WorkspaceClient
import uuid

w = WorkspaceClient()
name = "my-instance"
inst = w.database.get_database_instance(name=name)
host, port, database, user = inst.read_write_dns, 5432, "databricks_postgres", "<pg_role>"

def fresh_token() -> str:
    return w.database.generate_database_credential(
        request_id=str(uuid.uuid4()), instance_names=[name]
    ).token

engine = create_engine(
    f"postgresql+psycopg://{user}@{host}:{port}/{database}?sslmode=require",
    pool_size=8, max_overflow=4, pool_pre_ping=True, pool_recycle=3000,
)

# Inject a *fresh* token as the password each time a NEW physical connection is opened.
@event.listens_for(engine, "do_connect")
def _provide_token(dialect, conn_rec, cargs, cparams):
    cparams["password"] = fresh_token()

with engine.connect() as conn:
    print(conn.execute(text("SELECT version()")).scalar())

Node.js — the @databricks/lakebase pool (drop-in for pg.Pool):

import { createLakebasePool } from "@databricks/lakebase";
// A drop-in replacement for the standard `pg` pool that mints & refreshes the
// OAuth token for you (proactively, ~2 min before the 1 h expiry) and caches it.
const pool = createLakebasePool({
  host: "ep-….database.<region>.cloud.databricks.com",
  database: "databricks_postgres",
  endpoint: "projects/<p>/branches/<b>/endpoints/<e>",   // v2 endpoint path
  max: 10,             // pool size (default 10)
  idleTimeoutMillis: 30000,
});
const { rows } = await pool.query("SELECT * FROM users WHERE id = $1", [42]);
// integrates with Drizzle / Prisma / TypeORM; supports OpenTelemetry.
🧭 Rule of thumb. Use a maintained helper pool (@databricks/lakebase for Node; the SQLAlchemy do_connect hook for Python) so token refresh is automatic. Hand-rolled pools that cache the first token forever will start failing ~1 h in, when they try to open a new connection.

14.3. 🌐 The Lakebase Data API (SQL over HTTP, PostgREST-compatible)

The Data API is a REST interface auto-generated from your schema: every table/view becomes a URL, columns become JSON fields, functions become RPC endpoints — no Postgres driver required. It runs on the v2 Autoscaling platform (in Part 5 terms, the serverless/http_sql.rs path that reuses the same auth/wake/routing backend as the wire protocol).

Data API connection overview.

  HTTPS request                         Lakebase Data API (PostgREST)
  ─────────────                         ─────────────────────────────
  GET /public/clients?id=eq.2           ┌───────────────────────────────────────────┐
  Authorization: Bearer <OAuth>  ─────▶ │ 1. validate the Bearer OAuth token          │
                                        │ 2. the single `authenticator` role          │
                                        │    ASSUMES the caller's Postgres role        │
                                        │ 3. runs the generated SQL under that role    │
                                        │ 4. RLS policies filter the rows              │
                                        └───────────────────────────────────────────┘
                                                     │ JSON array/object
                                        ◀────────────┘
  • Endpoint shape: {REST_ENDPOINT}/{schema}/{table} — e.g. .../public/clients. The schema segment is explicit (the base URL doesn't include it).
  • Auth: a Databricks OAuth token as Authorization: Bearer …. There is no password/token in a connection string — it's a header on each request.
  • The authenticator model (the key technical detail): all HTTP traffic arrives on one Postgres role called authenticator, which then assumes the identity of the calling user (each Databricks identity needs a matching Postgres role, created via databricks_create_role() and granted TO authenticator). So table grants + RLS of the real user are what actually gate the request — even though the physical connection is shared. This is how the API multiplexes many users over a pooled backend without giving them all the same rights.

Establishing access to the Data API. Unlike the driver you don't open a "connection" — you grant a role and send requests. Setup once:

-- 1. create a Postgres role for the Databricks identity (idempotent helper)
SELECT databricks_create_role('user@databricks.com');

-- 2. let the API's authenticator role assume this user, and grant table access
GRANT "user@databricks.com" TO authenticator;
GRANT USAGE ON SCHEMA public TO "user@databricks.com";
GRANT SELECT, INSERT, UPDATE, DELETE ON ALL TABLES IN SCHEMA public TO "user@databricks.com";

-- 3. RLS is CRITICAL over HTTP: table grants alone let the user see EVERY row.
ALTER TABLE clients ENABLE ROW LEVEL SECURITY;
CREATE POLICY alice_clients ON clients
  TO "alice@databricks.com" USING (owner = current_user);

Then call it — CRUD maps to HTTP verbs, with PostgREST query syntax:

# READ with filter + sort + pagination
curl -H "Authorization: Bearer $DBX_OAUTH_TOKEN" \
  "$REST_ENDPOINT/public/clients?status=eq.active&id=gte.2&order=name.asc&limit=10&offset=20"

# READ with embedded related rows (foreign-key expansion)
curl -H "Authorization: Bearer $DBX_OAUTH_TOKEN" \
  "$REST_ENDPOINT/public/clients?select=id,name,projects(id,name)"

# CREATE
curl -X POST -H "Authorization: Bearer $DBX_OAUTH_TOKEN" -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{"name":"Acme","email":"a@acme.com"}' "$REST_ENDPOINT/public/clients"

# UPDATE (PATCH) matching rows
curl -X PATCH -H "Authorization: Bearer $DBX_OAUTH_TOKEN" -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{"status":"inactive"}' "$REST_ENDPOINT/public/clients?id=eq.2"

# DELETE
curl -X DELETE -H "Authorization: Bearer $DBX_OAUTH_TOKEN" "$REST_ENDPOINT/public/clients?id=eq.2"
  • Filter operators: eq, neq, gt/gte, lt/lte, like, in, … as ?col=op.value.
  • Response: JSON — an array of objects (or a single object); select= projects/embeds columns.
  • Functions → RPC: a Postgres function is called as POST {REST_ENDPOINT}/rpc/<function> with a JSON body.
  • Limits: a "Maximum rows" setting caps rows per response (protects performance + egress).
  • PostgREST caveats: Databricks OAuth replaces PostgREST's JWT config; not supported — passing GUCs to functions, pre-request functions, custom trace-header propagation.

14.4. ⚖️ Driver vs. Data API — how to choose

DimensionPostgreSQL driver (:5432)Data API (HTTPS)
TransportPostgres wire protocol, stateful sessionStateless HTTP request/response (REST/JSON)
AuthOAuth token (or native pw) as the connection password, checked at loginOAuth token as a Bearer header, checked per request
Transactions✅ Full multi-statement BEGIN/COMMIT, prepared statements, COPY, LISTEN⚠️ Per-request; no cross-request transaction/session state
Connection mgmtYou run a pool; refresh token for new connsNone — nothing to keep open
Security modelPostgres role's grants (+ optional RLS)The authenticator assumes the user role; RLS is essential
Latency profileLow per-query once warm; fork/TLS/wake on coldPer-request HTTP overhead; no pool to warm
Ideal callerLong-lived server apps, ETL, high throughputServerless/Lambda, edge, browsers, occasional/3rd-party HTTP
Overhead to adoptA driver + pooling codeJust an HTTP client + a token
🧠 Mental rule. Persistent server process doing real transactions → driver + pool. Stateless, occasional, or "I only have fetch" caller → Data API. They hit the same data and the same engine — the choice is purely about the transport and how you manage state.

14.5. 🧒 Recap for a 10-year-old

There are two ways to ask the database questions. One is a phone call (the driver): you dial in, the line stays open, and you can have a long back-and-forth — great for programs that talk a lot. Smart programs keep a few phone lines always open and share them (a pool), and they get a fresh dial code every so often so they can add new lines (the token refresh). The other way is a mail slot (the Data API): you drop a letter with your question and get a letter back — no line to keep open, perfect when you only ask once in a while or you're far away (a website or a tiny cloud function). Both ways reach the same librarian inside the same house; you just pick the phone or the mail slot depending on how you work.

Sources

  • Connect to your database (Lakebase) — Databricks on AWS
  • Lakebase Data API — Databricks on AWS
  • Use a notebook to access a database instance — Databricks on AWS
  • Authenticate to a database instance (Lakebase) — Databricks on AWS
  • @databricks/lakebase — a drop-in pg pool with OAuth (GitHub)
  • PostgREST — the REST spec the Data API is compatible with
  • Cross-references: Part 4 §8 (OAuth tokens / native roles), Part 5 §19 (proxy pg_sni_router + wake_compute, and serverless/http_sql.rs — the Data API over the same backend), Part 6 (a query's journey through a backend), Part 12 §12.4 (roles, grants & RLS), Part 13 (v1/v2 endpoints).
PART 15 — Operating Lakebase: the reference facts (limits, HA, backups, security, pricing) 🛡️

Track B · Lakebase essentials · 🟢 Beginner→🟡 Intermediate · 🏁 last in the learning path

Parts 4–14 explained how Lakebase works. This part is the operational reference: the numbers, limits, availability guarantees, recovery options, security posture and pricing model you need when you actually run Lakebase — or when you're deciding whether it fits a use case. It consolidates the facts scattered across the product docs and the internal Lakebase: FAQ into one place.
⚠️ Fast-moving facts. Every number here (limits, CU sizes, SLA %, GA dates) is a moving target — Lakebase ships constantly. Treat this as an orientation snapshot and verify against the current docs (go/lakebase/faq, the product docs) before you quote a number to a customer. Values reflect ~mid-2026.

15.0. 🧒 "Can it handle my stuff?" for a 5-year-old

Before you move into a new house you ask practical questions: How big is it? What if the power goes out? Can I get my things back if something breaks? Who has the keys? How much is the rent? This part answers exactly those questions for Lakebase — not how the plumbing works (that was Parts 4–14), but whether it fits, stays up, keeps your stuff safe, and what it costs.

👦 Technical explanation. This is the non-functional requirements reference: capacity limits, quotas, availability (HA / SLA), durability & recovery (PITR, DR), the managed-security model (databricks_superuser, CMK, encryption, auth), maintenance (autovacuum), what's not supported (logical replication, full superuser), and the billing model.

15.1. 🚦 Release status & where it runs

  • GA on AWS: January 22, 2026. GA on Azure: March 2, 2026. GCP: Beta in select regions.
  • GCP Beta limitations: no SLA; only 3 regions (europe-west3, us-central1, us-east4); instance size is capped.
  • PostgreSQL versions: Autoscaling → PG 16 & 17; Provisioned → PG 16 (no plans to add versions).
  • Who can create a database: every workspace user has Can Create.
  • Compliance: tracked at go/lakebase/compliance.

15.2. 📊 Limits & quotas (the numbers people ask for)

LimitAutoscaling (v2)Provisioned (v1)
Storage per instance16 TB2 TB (no plans to raise)
Compute unit (CU)0.5, 1, 2, … 64 CU (1 CU ≈ 2 GB RAM)1, 2, 4, 8 CU (1 CU ≈ 16 GB RAM)
Autoscaling rangeup to 16 CU wide (max − min ≤ 16)n/a (fixed)
Databases per workspace1000 (AWS); 20 on Azure during pre-GA free period10 instances/workspace
Concurrent connectionsup to 4000 (depends on compute size)1000 (no plans to raise)
Data retention vs quotaIncreasing retention does not count against the 16 TB quota—
🔑 Sizing rule of thumb: total logical data size across all tables must fit the storage cap. Delta is heavily compressed, so a synced source can be far larger logically than it looks — estimate with SELECT sum(len(to_csv(struct(*))) + 32) FROM your_table. Also factor in net-new daily writes.

15.3. 🟢 High availability, the SLA, and maintenance downtime

  • High Availability: Lakebase supports multi-AZ HA — compute can automatically fail over to one or more standby instances in another Availability Zone if the primary compute fails.
Endpoint typeMonthly uptime SLA
Single-compute (single-AZ)99.9 %
High Availability (multi-AZ)99.99 %

Measured per Lakebase endpoint, per calendar month, at 1-minute intervals (same methodology as AWS / Google / Azure). Applies to all users (PayGo and Commit). Remedy for a miss = service credits (the sole remedy), tiered by shortfall. Authoritative terms: databricks.com/legal/sla/lakebase-sla. Maintenance / scaling is designed to be non-disruptive; the v1→v2 upgrade restarts connections only briefly (Part 13 §13.5).

15.4. ♻️ Durability, backups & disaster recovery

  • Durability is the safekeeper Paxos quorum on COMMIT (Part 4 §0/§8; Part 8 §3.4) — not a local fsync — plus WAL/pages/branches persisted to object storage.
  • Point-in-time restore (PITR): restore to any LSN inside the history/retention window (Part 13: ~30 days v2, ~35 v1). Mechanically, PITR = branching at a past LSN (Part 8 §4.1) — no data is copied.
  • Cross-region disaster recovery (DR): work in progress — do not promise it today.
  • Storage redundancy is inherited from the Neon storage design (multi-copy, object-store-backed).

HA keeps compute available across AZs; PITR rewinds to any LSN within the retention window — both lean on the same durable, LSN-addressed storage.

15.5. 🔐 Security & the managed-privilege model

  • No real Postgres superuser. The instance owner gets the databricks_superuser role instead, holding: CREATEDB, CREATEROLE, BYPASSRLS, pg_read_all_data, pg_write_all_data, and GRANT ALL ON {TABLES, SEQUENCES} WITH GRANT OPTION in public (Part 4 §3; complements Part 12 §12.4).
  • AuthN: OAuth M2M (service principals), OAuth U2M (users) — tokens ~1 h — and native PostgreSQL username + password.
  • AuthZ: native Postgres grants/RLS (Part 12) plus UC-managed governance for the Databricks-native surfaces (Notebooks, REST API, DBSQL, Model Serving).
  • UC FGAC does not apply inside Postgres. Postgres uses its own RLS. A synced table whose UC source has FGAC fails by design; workaround = sync a SELECT * FROM … view in snapshot mode (syncs only rows the creator sees; the policy is not carried into PG).
  • Encryption at rest: always on (tenant-specific, workspace-scoped keys by default).
CMK component (Autoscaling only)What CMK covers
S3 (storage)Long-term WAL, pages, branches — all objects CMK-encrypted
PageserversAll on-disk "hot page" content CMK-encrypted
SafekeepersAll persisted WAL CMK-encrypted
Compute/PostgresEphemeral per-boot DEKs (OS/data disk, temp files, unlogged tables, query cache)

Revoke/delete the CMK → data at rest becomes cryptographically inaccessible to Databricks; new storage reads fail within minutes (≤ 1 h due to caching) and the compute control plane shuts the instance down. Status: Autoscaling UI → Settings → CMK. Audit via system.access.audit (e.g. action_name = 'deleteDatabaseInstance'; Gatekeeper-enforced deletions are not logged).

15.6. 🧹 Maintenance: autovacuum in the managed world

  • Autovacuum is ON by default in every instance (the MVCC bloat/wraparound machinery of Part 1 §5 / Part 2 §3 is fully alive).
  • Scale-to-zero is autovacuum-aware: the activity monitor ensures all autovacuum tasks finish before compute suspends.
  • Lakebase auto-tunes maintenance_work_mem, max_worker_processes, max_parallel_maintenance_workers to the compute size.
  • After bulk insert/delete, run VACUUM, VACUUM FULL, or pg_repack manually to reduce bloat.

15.7. 🚫 What is not supported (know before you promise)

Not supportedNotes / workaround
Logical replication (LR)Not supported today (tracked as DB-I-14785).
Full Postgres SUPERUSERManaged service → databricks_superuser instead (§15.5).
UC FGAC inside PostgresUse native Postgres RLS; synced tables with FGAC sources fail by design (§15.5).
Customer-specified partitioning of synced tablesSynced tables use one giant partition so views/permissions survive a full refresh (only the partition is swapped). Plain PG partitioning mainly helps dropping whole partitions, not performance (put the partition column first in the PK/index instead).
Cross-region DRWork in progress (§15.4).
Cross-workspace instance accessAn instance is scoped to one workspace (Part 7 §7).
ArcGIS geodatabaseHardcodes RDS-only roles (rds_admin); not compatible today.

15.8. 📈 Performance & benchmarks (what you can actually cite)

  • The architecture (Full Page Writes off, WAL to safekeeper quorum) delivers ~5× faster Postgres writes, ~94 % less WAL traffic, and up to ~2× lower read tail latency (Part 4 §0/§9; Part 8 §3.4).
  • Latency target: same-region access can hit < 10 ms; cross-region/cloud adds latency — co-locate app and database (Part 4 §9).
Compute size (HammerDB TPROC-C)Throughput (NOPM)
4-vCPU~94,891
16-vCPU~269,189
32-vCPU~439,300

15.9. 💸 Pricing & metering (the model, not the prices)

Start on Autoscaling unless a region forces Provisioned. Autoscaling is usage-based across meters:

MeterUnitNotes
ComputeCU-hourBilled on the CU size actually used as it scales; scale-to-zero → $0 compute while idle.
Storage (branch)GB-monthProduction (non-expiring) = total logical size; Dev/CI (expiring) = only the size of changes (deltas), capped at logical size → cheap ephemeral envs.

Provisioned bills a fixed 24/7 rate per CU (Part 13) — you pay for the fixed size whether busy or not. Example: 1 CU for 20 min → scale-to-zero 20 min → 2 CU for 20 min = 1 CU-hour total. Authoritative numbers on the public pricing page / go/lakebase/pricing/faq.

15.10. ✅ Is Lakebase the right fit? (qualification cheatsheet)

Position it for 🎯 — operational analytics: serving analytical/ML data to apps, and application state (interactive workflow sign-off, pipeline state, RAG-chatbot state).

Avoid (for now) 🚫 — large-scale OLTP migrations off proprietary vendors, and OLAP / warehouse workloads (that's DBSQL, not Lakebase — Part 8 §5).

Quick qualification questions:

  1. Dataset size? Must fit 16 TB logical (v2) / 2 TB (v1) across all tables (§15.2).
  2. Daily write volume? Factor net-new data against the same cap.
  3. Where do the app workloads run? Same region → < 10 ms; cross-region adds latency (§15.8).
  4. Concurrency? Up to 4000 connections (v2) — use a pooler and refresh tokens (Part 14 §14.2).
  5. Need logical replication / cross-region DR / full superuser? → not today (§15.7).

15.11. 🧒 Recap for a 10-year-old

Think of renting an apartment. Lakebase's apartment comes in sizes up to 16 TB, holds up to 4000 visitors at once, and can shrink to nothing when empty so you pay almost no rent — then grow back instantly. If the building's power on one floor fails, a backup floor takes over automatically (HA), and the landlord promises the lights stay on 99.9–99.99 % of the month (the SLA). If you knock something over, you can rewind time to before the mess (PITR). The landlord keeps a master key in a vault, but you can bring your own lock (CMK) and, if you take your key away, nobody — not even the landlord — can open the door. A tidy-up robot (autovacuum) cleans while you're away. And the rent is pay-for-what-you-use.
