PART 16 — Life of a query & the auth model, the Engineering-book view 🔬🔑

Track D · Vision & deep internals · 🔴 Advanced / reference · 🏁 last in the learning path

Part 6 traced a query through the code (proxy → backend → smgr → pageserver). This part re-tells the same journey at the systems level the Lakebase engineering handbook uses — the one an on-call or field engineer needs — and then does the same for authentication & authorization. It adds the pieces Parts 6/12/14/15 don't spell out: the V1-vs-V2 ingress split, the read-path cache hierarchy with its metrics, the double authentication (proxy and compute), how roles are kept in sync, and the three-role model behind the Data API.
📚 Source. This part distills the internal Lakebase Engineering book pages "Life of a Query in Lakebase" and "Understanding Lakebase Authentication and Authorization", plus the Lakebase glossary. Component names (GIG, RIG, HCM, LBM, NeonVM, lakebase-proxy, authenticator…) are the ones used internally — treat them as study pointers; names and details evolve.

16.0. 🧒 The query's road trip, for a 5-year-old

A query is like a letter you mail to the library. Before the librarian even reads it, the letter goes through a few checkpoints: a front gate that checks your ID, a sorting room that finds the right building, and a guard at the door. Only then does the librarian (Postgres) read your letter, look for the book on the nearest shelf (fast), then a bigger shelf (still fast), and — only if it's nowhere close — send a runner to the big warehouse (pageserver). Writing is different: the librarian shouts the change to three note-takers (safekeepers) and, the moment two of them write it down, your change is safe forever.

The one line: every query crosses network + auth checkpoints, then resolves through a cache ladder (shared_buffers → LFC → pageserver); every write is durable once a safekeeper quorum has the WAL.
👦 Technical explanation. A connection is routed by an ingress gateway (per-instance DNS + GIG in V1; regional wildcard DNS + RIG in V2), authenticated at the proxy (lakebase-proxy, OAuth JWT or SCRAM), forwarded to a compute (a pod in V1, a NeonVM in V2) which re-authenticates the same credential, then executes SQL. Reads climb a cache ladder — shared_buffers (RAM) → LFC (local NVMe) → GetPage@LSN to a pageserver that reconstructs the 8 KB page from image+delta layers. Writes stream WAL via the walproposer to 3 safekeepers; commit = quorum (2/3) durable; the pageserver ingests asynchronously. Authorization is layered: UC grants + native Postgres roles/RLS, with the Data API using an authenticator pool role that SET ROLEs to the caller per request.

16.1. 🚪 Ingress & routing — how a connection even reaches Postgres (V1 vs V2)

Before any SQL runs, the TCP connection crosses several network layers, and they differ by version (Part 13). This is the piece Part 6 assumed away.

V1 routes globally (GIG) to a fixed pod managed by HCM; V2 stays in-region (RIG) to an autoscaling NeonVM managed by LBM that can wake from zero.

AspectV1 (Provisioned)V2 (Autoscaling)
DNSPer-instance → GIG (global)Regional wildcard → RIG (in-region)
Control planeHCM (Hadron Compute Manager)LBM (Lakebase Manager)
ComputeFixed-size pod (hadron-compute)NeonVM (lakebase-compute), autoscaled
Scale-to-zero / wake❌✅ proxy calls LBM to wake_compute
Connection poolernone server-side (app pools)none in prod (PgBouncer not used) — cf. Part 14 §14.2
🔑 Field-debugging tell: an instance-<uuid>… host with a global hop is V1; an ep-<word>-<word>-<hash>.database.<region>… host that can wake from zero is V2.

16.2. 🧭 Query execution & the read-path cache ladder

Once authenticated, the connection reaches Postgres and runs the normal parse → plan → execute pipeline (Part 2 §2, Part 6). What's Lakebase-specific is where the pages come from — a three-rung ladder, checked top to bottom, each rung slower but bigger:

Each rung is slower but larger. On a full miss the pageserver reconstructs the exact page at the requested LSN and the result backfills both caches.

RungWhereWatch (metric)Meaning
shared_bufferscompute RAM (clock-sweep)Cache hit rate (UI)< ~99 % sustained → working set > buffers, scale up
LFCcompute local NVMe (LRU)Working set size; lfc_hitsworking set > LFC → more pageserver traffic
Pageserverremote storage (S3-backed)getpage_wait_secondshigh → cold cache / undersized compute

On GetPage@LSN the pageserver searches its layer stack top-to-bottom — in-memory → delta → image layers — finds the newest base image and replays deltas up to the requested LSN (expands Part 8 §3.3). Because any page can be rebuilt at any LSN in the retention window, branching, read replicas and PITR all fall out of this one primitive.

🎓 auto_explain is preloaded on every compute (with pg_stat_statements, neon, databricks_auth, passwordcheck, auth_delay) but off by default. Turn it on per-session: SET auto_explain.log_min_duration='100ms'; SET auto_explain.log_analyze=on; SET auto_explain.log_buffers=on; — the logged plan == EXPLAIN (ANALYZE, BUFFERS) (Part 2 §2). "Many buffer reads, few hits" means the working set overflowed the caches.

16.3. ✍️ The write path — WAL, the walproposer, and the safekeeper quorum

Writes never wait for the pageserver. The compute's walproposer (a Neon Postgres extension) streams WAL records to all 3 safekeepers continuously as they are generated (not only at commit), which run a Paxos-derived consensus; the transaction commits when a quorum (≥ 2 of 3) has durably persisted the WAL up to the commit record's LSN — so COMMIT waits for the quorum ack, it does not trigger the send. The pageserver then pulls the WAL: its WAL receiver subscribes to the storage_broker, picks the safekeeper with the highest LSN, and consumes it via physical replication asynchronously (never blocking commit) — the pull mechanism verified in Part 5 §20, not a safekeeper-side push.

Commit returns as soon as a quorum has the WAL — no local fsync, no waiting on the pageserver. Three LSNs (last_record / disk_consistent / remote_consistent) track how far the write has hardened.

Durability tiers: (1) quorum on ≥ 2 safekeepers at commit → survives an immediate compute crash; (2) pageserver local disk; (3) S3. On restart the compute needs no WAL replay — it reconstructs every page from the pageserver on demand. Replica lag shows as replication_delay_bytes / replication_delay_seconds (UI, replica endpoints only).

16.4. 🔑 Authentication — the double check (proxy and compute)

Every connection is authenticated before any query runs — and in V2, twice: once at the lakebase-proxy and again at the compute pod, which re-validates the forwarded credential independently.

MethodHow it's checkedNotes
Databricks OAuth (JWT)RS256 (RSA) signature verified locally against mounted public keys (the databricks_auth verifier only accepts JWT_ALG_RS256); principal-context tokens forwarded by the proxy are ECDSA P-256 (Tink keyset); claims = workspace/tenant/endpoint id; 1-hour expiryproxy (V2) and the databricks_auth extension in the compute
Native PG password (SCRAM)SCRAM-SHA-256 challenge/response; plaintext never leaves the client; hash in pg_authid (and LBM's store for V2)proxy (V2) runs server-side SCRAM using the control-plane hash
mTLS certificatecert method in pg_hba.conf, mapped to databricks_gatewayhow the Data API / HTTP gateway connects

Credential → algorithm → identity matrix (the quick reference — note the three different signature schemes):

CredentialAlgorithmWhere verifiedResulting role
Databricks OAuth JWTRS256 (RSA)proxy (V2) + databricks_auththe caller's user role
Principal-context token (proxy-forwarded)ECDSA P-256 (Tink)databricks_auththe caller's role
Native PG passwordSCRAM-SHA-256proxy (server-side) + pg_authidthat role
Data API / HTTP gatewaymTLS certpg_hba cert mapdatabricks_gateway → SET ROLEs the caller (§16.5)

The production databricks_pg_hba.conf (both versions) requires TLS on every row (hostssl):

# TYPE   DATABASE  USER                       ADDRESS  METHOD
hostssl  all       databricks_control_plane   all      password
hostssl  all       databricks_gateway         all      cert   map=httpgateway
hostssl  all       all                        all      password
🧩 How roles stay in sync (why SQL-created roles still work through the proxy). Create a role two ways: (a) via the Databricks Roles API → the scheduler/LBM writes it to the control plane and the compute; (b) via plain SQL CREATE ROLE … PASSWORD … → the neon extension's DDL forwarding hooks ProcessUtility_hook, and on commit sends a JSON delta (incl. the SCRAM hash from pg_authid) to LBM. Either way LBM ends up with the scram_secret, so the proxy can authenticate the role. (SHOW neon.forward_ddl; — defaults true; false only during control-plane apply_config to avoid loops.)

16.5. 🛡️ Authorization — who you are on each access path

Authentication proves who you are; authorization decides what you can touch. Lakebase layers UC governance (on the Databricks-native surfaces) over native Postgres roles/grants/RLS inside the DB (Part 12). The subtlety: different access paths connect as different identities.

Access pathWho connects to PostgresPG role usedAuthz enforcement
Direct psql / driver (Part 14)the user/SPthat identity's own rolenative PG grants + RLS
Federation (Part 9) via UCserverless SQL warehouse (JDBC)mapped roleUC grants first (USE CATALOG/SELECT), then PG grants
Feature Store — publishend useruser's rolegranted CONNECT,CREATE on the DB
Feature Store / Model Serving — read featuresend user / endpoint SP (OAuth)that identity's roletoken scoped READ_ONLY to specific tables + PG grants
Model Serving — direct Lakebase dep (agent state)endpoint SPSP role with databricks_superuserfull DDL+DML, no per-table scoping
Data API (Part 14 §14.3)authenticator pool → SET ROLE userthe user's own rolePG grants + RLS only (no Databricks-level check past JWT)

The Data API three-role model (the cleanest example of "connect as X, act as Y"):

NOINHERIT is the security keystone: the pool role can switch to a user via explicit SET ROLE but never passively inherits anyone's privileges; NOBYPASSRLS keeps row-level security binding.

System roles present in every instance (know them when you see them in \du):

RolePurpose
databricks_superuseradmin role (Part 15 §15.5); granted to SPs for direct Lakebase deps
databricks_gatewayHTTP Gateway / Data API connection (mTLS)
databricks_control_planecontrol-plane management operations
databricks_monitormonitoring / observability

16.6. 🗺️ The Neon reference map (repos & where things live)

Lakebase's engine is the Neon stack; the code is spread across repos. This orientation map helps when a doc or a stack trace names a component (complements Part 5):

RepoContains
hadronThe Neon storage stack — pageserver, safekeeper, storage_controller, compute_tools (compute_ctl — starts Postgres, fetches basebackup@LSN), pgxn (databricks_auth, neon, wal2delta), proxy (+ proxy/src/serverless = the Data API / PostgREST analog), and a fork of Postgres.
neon-cloudThe compute control planes: LBM (goapp/controlplane), the Neon Console, Neon Auth, telemetry API.
universeThe Databricks monorepo — Brickstore Scheduler (brickstore/scheduler), synced-tables dataplane, lakebase-dataplane-bridge, public docs, UI, Grafana dashboards, deploy configs.
neon-flux-fleetIaC for neon.com (k8s, AWS).
Terminology anchors (glossary): Lakebase = Brickstore (old name); V1/Provisioned/Database Instances → HCM; V2/Autoscaling/Database Projects → LBM; UC = managed_catalog (governs everything except data inside PG, which PG governs itself). "Neon" = the shared PG tech stack on both databricks.com and neon.com.

16.7. 🧒 Recap for a 10-year-old

Your letter (query) goes through a front gate and a door guard (two ID checks) before the librarian reads it. To find a book the librarian looks on the desk (super fast), then a nearby shelf (fast), then sends a runner to the giant warehouse only if needed. To save a change, the librarian tells three note-takers and it's safe the instant two have written it down — even if the librarian trips right after. And who's allowed to touch which books depends on which door you came through: your own key, a robot's key, or a special helper (the Data API) that borrows your key just for your one request and never keeps it.

Sources

  • Internal Lakebase Engineering book (go/lakebase/docs, source databricks-eng/lakebase-docs): Life of a Query in Lakebase; Understanding Lakebase Authentication and Authorization; Neon References / Lakebase Glossary. Internal use only.
  • Life of a query — public Lakebase docs · Create Postgres roles · Data API
  • Cross-references: Part 5, Part 6, Part 8 §3, Part 9, Part 12, Part 13, Part 14, Part 15 §15.5.
PART 17 — Caching in Lakebase: the full memory hierarchy 🗄️

Track D · Vision & deep internals · 🔴 Advanced / reference

Caching is the single most important thing to understand about Lakebase performance, because Lakebase separates compute from storage (Part 4 §0): every durable byte lives in the pageservers/S3, across the network. What makes it feel like a local Postgres is a stack of caches on the compute that keeps the working set close to the CPU. This part pulls together everything the rest of the document says about caching — the buffer manager (Part 1 §10.2), the Local File Cache (Part 5 §2), the plan/catalog caches (Part 3 §3), and the cold-start behavior (Part 4 §7.1) — into one memory hierarchy.

17.0. 🧒 The nested-shelves story, for a 5-year-old

Imagine the librarian (Postgres) fetching books (data pages). There's a tiny desk right in front of them (super fast, tiny), a bookshelf behind the desk (fast, bigger), and a giant warehouse across town (huge, but a runner has to drive there). The librarian always checks the desk first, then the shelf, and only sends the runner to the warehouse if the book is nowhere close. Every time a runner brings a book back, they put a copy on the shelf and on the desk so next time it's instant. When the library closes for the night (scale-to-zero), the desk and shelf are cleared — so the first few requests next morning are slow until the popular books are back within reach.

The one line: reads climb a ladder — RAM (shared_buffers) → local NVMe (LFC) → pageserver over the network — and Lakebase's whole job is to keep your working set on the top two rungs.
👦 Technical explanation. A page read resolves through three tiers: shared_buffers (RAM, 8 KB frames, clock-sweep), the Local File Cache / LFC (compute-local NVMe, chunked, LRU), and the pageserver (GetPage@LSN over the network, page reconstructed from image+delta layers). Hits on the top two tiers cost no network; a miss all the way down costs a round-trip plus layer replay. Two more caches matter but hold metadata/plans, not data pages: per-backend catalog caches (relcache/catcache) and the plan cache (prepared statements). All of these are volatile — after a scale-to-zero suspend or a compute resize the data tiers start cold and warm up as the working set is touched (or is prewarmed, Part 5 §2).

17.1. 🪜 The read-path cache ladder (the big picture)

When the executor needs a page (relation, block) at a visibility point LSN, it checks each tier top to bottom; each rung is slower but bigger than the one above:

Each rung is slower but bigger. The LFC exists so a buffer miss hits local NVMe, not the network floor, on every read.

#TierWhereSizeLatencyEvictionSurvives…
①shared_bufferscompute RAM~25 % of RAM (managed)~nsclock-sweepnothing — cleared on restart/suspend
②LFCcompute local NVMesized with the VM (GBs–TBs)~µsLRU (per partition)nothing — cleared with the VM
③Pageserverremote (RAM+NVMe) → S3effectively unbounded~ms (network)layer compaction / GCit is the durable copy
📗 Why this ladder is the whole ballgame. In a classic Postgres, shared_buffers miss → OS page cache → local SSD, all local. In Lakebase a miss that reaches tier ③ is a network operation, so cache hit rates translate much more directly into latency. Keeping the working set in tiers ①+② is the difference between microsecond and millisecond reads.

17.2. ① shared_buffers — the RAM tier (buffer manager)

This tier is unchanged real Postgres — the buffer manager from Part 1 §10.2 and Part 3 §3. Recapping only what matters for the hierarchy:

  • Structure: an array of 8 KB frames + BufferDesc descriptors + the partitioned SharedBufHash mapping a buffer tag {relfilenode, forknum, blocknum} → frame, under 128 BufMappingLock partitions.
  • Replacement: clock-sweep (StrategyGetBuffer). Ring buffers (BAS_BULKREAD/BAS_VACUUM…) stop a big scan or VACUUM from blowing away everyone's hot pages.
  • Lakebase specifics: shared_buffers is a managed GUC (Part 4 §3) — it tracks the VM size. When a dirty buffer is evicted the engine does not write it to a local file — the neon extension's smgr logs the page's WAL instead (neon_wallog_page); durability comes from the safekeeper quorum (§17.6).
  • Volatility: the whole array lives in the VM's RAM → gone on suspend/restart/resize → cold start (§17.5).

17.3. ② The Local File Cache (LFC) — the NVMe tier

This is the tier that is exclusive to Lakebase (Part 4 §2) and the reason disaggregated storage stays fast. Full code-level detail is in Part 5 §2; here is what it is and why it's central to caching:

The LFC turns "working set > RAM" from a network problem into a local-NVMe one, and doubles as the sensor that sizes tier ①.

  • What it is: a second-level read cache of 8 KB pages held in a file or raw block device on the compute's local NVMe, below shared_buffers and above the pageserver. Chunked (128 KB default, up to 1 MB), hash-partitioned, evicts LRU per partition.
  • Why it matters for caching: a workload whose hot set fits in the LFC runs at local-SSD latency even though the authoritative data is remote.
  • It feeds autoscaling: every access updates a HyperLogLog working-set estimate (lfc_approximate_working_set_size_seconds) that the autoscaler-agent polls to size the VM's RAM (Part 4 §7.1).
  • Resized live & prewarmable: the vm-monitor grows/shrinks it with the VM (neon.file_cache_size_limit, SIGHUP; shrink via fallocate(PUNCH_HOLE)); after a wake it can be prewarmed (lfc_get_state). Databricks also adds a tiered-cache mode (Part 5 §2).
  • Volatility: like RAM, not durable — sized with the VM, discarded on scale-to-zero (Part 4 §7.2). Losing it only costs a cold cache, never data.

17.4. ③ The pageserver — the "cache of last resort" over durable storage

A tier-② miss becomes a GetPage@LSN request to a pageserver (Part 5 §5, Part 8 §3.3). The pageserver is not just storage — it is itself a hot cache tier (RAM + local NVMe) in front of S3, and it reconstructs the exact page you ask for:

The pageserver rebuilds the exact page at the requested LSN — the primitive behind branching, read replicas, and PITR.

  • Reconstruction, not just fetch: Timeline::get finds the newest ImageLayer ≤ LSN and applies the DeltaLayer/InMemoryLayer WAL on top via a wal-redo process. Any page can be rebuilt at any LSN in the retention window (Part 8 §4).
  • Its own caching: layer files live durably in S3 but are cached on the pageserver's local disk and re-fetched on demand; compaction collapses long delta chains into fresh image layers. This is the image-generation pushdown that lets Lakebase disable Full Page Writes (Part 4 §1).
  • Latency: this is the network tier — watch getpage_wait_seconds (§17.8). Sustained high values mean the working set is overflowing tiers ①+②.

17.5. 🧊 Cold start — when the caches are empty

The data tiers are volatile, so after any event that gives you a fresh (or resized) compute — a scale-to-zero wake, a restart, a failover, a new branch/replica — tiers ① and ② start empty and the first queries pay the network price on every miss until the working set is re-cached:

Every fresh compute rebuilds its caches; prewarm and a non-zero min CU soften or avoid the cold start.

LeverEffect on cold start
min CU > 0 (Part 4 §7.1)Keeps a compute floor alive → avoids scale-to-zero for latency-critical apps (no cold start).
Pre-warmed compute pools (compute_pool_reconciler, Part 5 §16)Ready computes shorten the wake itself.
LFC prewarm (lfc_get_state, Part 5 §2)Repopulates the NVMe tier from a snapshot of the hottest chunks after a wake.
Connection pooler + long-lived connectionsAmortize wake/auth cost; keep the working set hot across requests.
auto_explain + BUFFERSConfirms a cold cache: "many reads, few hits" == you're paying the network floor.
⚠️ The cold-start tradeoff is the price of scale-to-zero. For spiky/dev/test workloads it's a great deal; for latency-critical OLTP, set min CU > 0 so the caches never evaporate.

17.6. 📝 Caching and the write path (why writes never wait on the cache)

Caching is a read-path story; writes are decoupled from it (Part 16 §16.3). A COMMIT streams WAL via the walproposer to the safekeepers and is durable on quorum (≥ 2 of 3) — it does not wait for the page to be written anywhere, and not for the pageserver:

Write latency depends on the safekeeper network quorum; read latency depends on the cache hierarchy — two independent axes.

17.7. 🧠 The metadata caches (plans, catalogs) — per backend

Beyond data pages, each backend (connection process) keeps private caches of metadata and plans (Part 3 §3). They don't hold table data, but they matter for latency and for connection-pooling behavior:

CacheWhat it holdsScopeInvalidation
relcachetable/index descriptors (RelationData)per backendSI messages on DDL refresh it
catcache (syscache)rows from the system catalogs (pg_class, pg_attribute…)per backendSI invalidation messages
Plan cacheprepared-statement plans (generic vs custom)per sessionreplan on DDL / stats change; custom→generic after 5 executions
  • Plan cache & prepared statements: after the 6th execution of a prepared statement, Postgres may switch from a per-parameter custom plan to a cached generic plan if it's not estimated to be more expensive. This lives in the session, so it is lost on scale-to-zero and is not shared across pooled connections that reset state.
  • Cache invalidation across backends: a DDL statement queues SI (Shared Invalidation) messages in shared memory (Part 3 §3); other backends refresh their relcache/catcache on their next command.
  • Pooling caveat (Data API / PgBouncer-style): a pooled/stateless path (Part 14) may not preserve prepared-statement or session plan caches between requests, so very hot parameterized queries can re-plan more often than on a long-lived driver connection.

17.8. 🧭 Reading & tuning the hierarchy (practical guide)

Metrics, per tier (UI + Grafana; cf. Part 16 §16.2):

SignalTierWhat it tells youAction
Cache hit rate (UI)① shared_buffers< ~99 % sustained → working set > RAMscale up max CU, or reduce the working set
Working set size (UI)② LFChow much data the workload actively touchesif it exceeds the LFC, expect more pageserver traffic
lfc_hits / lfc_misses (Grafana)② LFCNVMe-tier effectivenesslow hit ratio → cache too small / cold
getpage_wait_seconds (Grafana)③ pageservertime spent waiting on the network tierhigh → cold cache or undersized compute
"reads vs hits" in EXPLAIN (BUFFERS)①/②did this query overflow the caches?tune the query/indexes or the compute size
  • 🧭 Exploit locality. Good access locality (indexes, clustered access, avoiding full scans of cold tables) keeps you in tiers ①+② and off the network (Part 4 §9).
  • 🧭 Right-size min/max CU. min CU > 0 protects latency-critical apps from cold starts; the working-set estimate (LFC) is what the autoscaler uses to grow RAM.
  • 🧭 Prefer long-lived connections + a pooler so both the data tiers and the plan cache stay warm.
  • 🧭 Don't fear scale-to-zero for the right workloads (dev/test, branches, spiky apps) — just know the first post-idle query pays to rewarm the caches.
  • ⚠️ Storage cost is driven by the caches, not object bytes (Part 8 §8): the pageserver hot tiers (RAM + NVMe) dominate Lakebase's storage economics.

17.9. 🧒 Recap for a 10-year-old

To find a page, Postgres looks on the desk (RAM, shared_buffers), then the nearby shelf (local NVMe, the LFC), and only sends a runner to the giant warehouse across town (the pageserver, over the network) if it's nowhere close — and the warehouse rebuilds the exact page from its notes before handing it over. Every fetched page is copied back onto the shelf and the desk so next time it's instant. When the library closes for the night (scale-to-zero) the desk and shelf are wiped, so the first requests next morning are slow — unless you kept a small light on (min CU > 0) or prewarmed the shelf. Saving a change is a different path: it's safe the moment two of three note-takers (safekeepers) have the WAL — it never waits for the shelves at all.

Sources

  • This part consolidates material proven elsewhere: Part 1 §10.2 (buffer manager), Part 3 §3 (catalog caches, SI invalidation), Part 4 §2/§7 (LFC as a Lakebase exclusive; autoscaling & cold start), Part 5 §2/§5 (file_cache.c and the pageserver layer engine), Part 8 §3 (Key × LSN reconstruction), Part 16 §16.2/§16.3 (read-path ladder and write path).
  • Code (verified against databricks-eng/hadron, branch neon-main): pgxn/neon/file_cache.c (LFC), pgxn/neon/pagestore_smgr.c (smgr read path), pageserver/ (layer engine).
  • Public docs: Lakebase / OLTP docs. The exact shared_buffers fraction, LFC sizing, and metric names are managed and evolve.

Quick glossary

TermMeaning
WALA record written before the data to guarantee durability.
LSNByte position in the WAL stream.
MVCCEach change creates a new version of the row.
VACUUMCleans dead versions and prevents XID wraparound.
CheckpointPoint where all dirty pages are flushed to disk.
shared_buffersPage cache in shared memory.
pg_hba.confAuthentication rules by host/user/database.
ACIDAtomicity, Consistency, Isolation, Durability.
BackendServer process dedicated to a client connection.
PostmasterSupervisor process, parent of all; accepts connections and watches over the children.
ProcArrayArray of PGPROC with the live transactions; basis of the MVCC snapshots.
xmin / xmaxXID that created / deleted-updated a row version (MVCC visibility).
LWLockLightweight lock (shared/exclusive) that protects shared-memory structures.
CLOG / pg_xactSLRU with the state (committed/aborted/in-progress) of each XID.
LatchSignaler in shared memory to wake another process without polling.
walsender / walreceiverBackend that sends WAL (primary) / process that receives it (standby).
clock-sweepBuffer replacement algorithm for shared_buffers.
Replication slotMarks the consumer's progress so WAL still needed isn't deleted.
LakebaseDatabricks's managed Postgres (on Neon) with separated compute and storage.
SafekeeperLakebase component that makes the WAL durable with a Paxos quorum.
PageserverLakebase component that reconstructs pages from a base image + WAL deltas.
LFC (Local File Cache)Page cache on the compute's local NVMe, between shared_buffers and the pageserver.
BranchingInstant copy-on-write clone of the database (exclusive to Lakebase/Neon).
Synced tableLakebase table synced with Unity Catalog/Delta in a managed way.
OAuth token (Lakebase)Ephemeral credential (~1h, workspace scope) used as the Postgres password over SSL.
Native Role LoginLakebase option for Postgres roles with a password (without the 1h TTL).
Service principal (M2M)Databricks identity for machine-to-machine access of apps to Lakebase.
smgr (pagestore_smgr.c)Postgres storage manager API that Neon implements against the pageserver (neon_read/neon_write…).
WalProposerC struct of the consensus proposer in the compute (walproposer.c).
SafeKeeper / AcceptorStateAcceptor side of the consensus in Rust; stores the current term and TermHistory.
ImageLayer / DeltaLayerImmutable pageserver layers: full snapshot @LSN / WAL deltas in a key×LSN range.
InMemoryLayerOpen in-memory layer with the recent WAL (Open→Frozen→DeltaLayer).
LayerMap / TimelineRecord of a timeline's layers / line of history (a branch points to its parent timeline).
Key × LSN2D page-addressing space of the pageserver.
hadronDatabricks repo (databricks-eng/hadron, neon-main branch) with the Lakebase engine (Neon fork).
LBM (Lakebase Manager)Lakebase control plane; built in neon-cloud and deployed from universe.
databricks_authPostgres extension (C) that authenticates Databricks/OAuth identities; struct DatabricksIdentity.
LakebaseTokenGeneratorEdDSA JWT generator of compute_manager (lakebase_token.rs).
wal2deltaExtension (Rust) that streams WAL→Delta via DataplaneBridgeClient (forward ETL / Lakebase CDF — the Lakebase→UC direction).
NeonVM / autoscalerThe compute's QEMU micro-VM and the system (neon-autoscaling) that scales it live.
Forward ETL / ZeroETLLakebase→UC direction: publishes Postgres changes to Delta in Unity Catalog (wal2delta).
Lakebase CDFChange Data Feed: materializes the WAL as lb_<table>_history in UC (every ~15 s).
lakebase-dataplane-bridgegRPC service (per DP region) that receives the changes from wal2delta and writes the Delta in UC.
REPLICA IDENTITY FULLCDF requirement: Postgres records the row before and after in the WAL (not just the PK).
Reverse ETL / Synced tableUC→Lakebase direction: a Lakeflow Spark pipeline materializes Delta into a read-only Postgres table.
OltpPgSinkSpark sink that writes to the live Postgres via SQL/COPY (incremental, ≤16 connections).
Direct-to-Storage / reverse_etl_workerBulk path: Spark generates PG pages (heap/index) and uploads them to S3; reverse_etl_worker extension (retl_heapam).
proxy / wake_computeEntry point that identifies the endpoint, authenticates, wakes the compute (scale-to-zero) and routes the bytes.
pg_sni_routerRoutes TLS connections by SNI to the correct compute's internal host:port.
WalReceiver / WalIngestIn the pageserver: receives the WAL from the safekeeper (via storage_broker), decodes and ingests it into the layers.
storage_brokerPub/sub where safekeepers publish their state; the pageserver picks the one with the highest LSN.
Registered database catalogA read-only UC catalog that is a live window onto one Lakebase Postgres database (no data copy); requires a serverless SQL warehouse (Part 7).
CreateDatabaseCatalogThe LBM control-plane operation (behind the UI/CLI/SDK/API) that binds a UC catalog to (instance, postgres_database).
LTAPLake Transactional/Analytical Processing: an architecture (built on Lakebase) that unifies Postgres + open columnar formats (Iceberg/Delta) at the storage layer, giving one logical copy across OLTP + OLAP (Part 8).
TranscodingThe pageserver process that turns row-oriented Postgres pages into compressed columnar Parquet (Delta/Iceberg) on object storage — async and isolated from OLTP (Part 8 §2/§3).
One logical copyThe LTAP principle: users manage/secure/reason about a single dataset, even though it is physically projected as PG pages and Parquet under the hood.
LTAP Direct Writes(Beta) The Lake→Lakebase bulk path: Spark writes directly to storage (pageserver + object store) bypassing OLTP compute (up to 100× faster loads, zero OLTP impact). Powers Synced Tables today.
Real-Time Analytics on Lakebase(Private Preview) Analytical queries against the live operational state; ~seconds freshness via Lakehouse//RT, ~10 min via external engines on the Iceberg projection (roadmap).
Lakehouse//RTThe engine for low-latency, high-concurrency analytics on fresh operational data (sub-second→seconds P95); one of the three LTAP engines alongside Lakebase (OLTP) and DBSQL (batch).
get_page_at_lsn(key, lsn)The pageserver read primitive: reconstruct an 8 KB page as of a point in history by combining a base ImageLayer + DeltaLayer WAL replay (Part 8 §3).
HOT (Heap-Only Tuple)An UPDATE that changes no indexed column and fits in the same page: the new version is chained via t_ctid with no new index entry (Part 1 §10.4).
Hint bitsFlags in a tuple's t_infomask caching the commit fate of xmin/xmax, so most visibility checks skip the CLOG lookup (Part 1 §10.4).
Visibility Map (VM)2 bits per heap page (all-visible / all-frozen) that let VACUUM and index-only scans skip pages (Part 1 §10.5).
Buffer tag / BufferDescA page's identity {relfilenode, forknum, blocknum} and its shared-memory descriptor (state, usage_count, content lock) in the buffer manager (Part 1 §10.2).
Resource manager (rmgr)The WAL subsystem tag (xl_rmid: heap, btree, xact…) that owns a record's redo/desc functions (Part 1 §10.3).
Query / Lakehouse FederationReading an external database (e.g. PostgreSQL) as a read-only UC catalog over JDBC, without ingesting the data (Part 9).
Connection (UC)A Unity Catalog securable storing the host/credentials of an external system, reused across foreign catalogs (Part 9 §9.1).
Foreign catalogA UC catalog that mirrors one external database as read-only tables (Part 9 §9.1).
PushdownFolding filters/projections/aggregates/limit into the SQL sent to the source so the remote engine (PostgreSQL) does the work before rows cross the network (Part 9 §9.4–9.5).
Scheduling policyThe sync mode of a synced table: SNAPSHOT (full atomic replace), TRIGGERED (incremental on demand/schedule, needs CDF), or CONTINUOUS (streaming ~seconds, needs CDF) (Part 10 §10.2).
Timeseries keyAn optional column on a synced table that deduplicates rows sharing a primary key by keeping only the latest value (last-writer-wins); required when the source has duplicate PKs (Part 10 §10.3).
lb_<table>_historyThe append-only, UC-managed Delta history table CDF creates per source table; holds all source columns + CDC metadata, flushed ~every 15 s (Part 11 §11.2).
_pg_change_typeCDF's change-type column: insert, delete, update_preimage (row before), or update_postimage (row after); an UPDATE emits both preimage and postimage rows (Part 11 §11.2).
Schema (Postgres)A named namespace inside one database holding tables, views, functions, etc.; the practical qualified name is schema.object (Part 12 §12.1).
pg_catalogThe system-catalog schema (pg_class, pg_namespace, pg_proc…); always searched first, implicitly, regardless of search_path (Part 12 §12.1).
search_pathThe ordered list of schemas Postgres walks to resolve an unqualified name; first match wins; new objects land in the first non-temp schema (Part 12 §12.3).
Fully-qualified referenceA schema-qualified name (schema.object) that resolves unambiguously without search_path; the UC analogue is the 3-part catalog.schema.object (Part 12 §12.3).
ACL / aclitemPostgres's per-object access-control list stored in catalog arrays (relacl, nspacl…), each entry grantee=privileges/grantor; * = grant option (Part 12 §12.4).
USAGE (schema)The privilege to "look inside" a schema and resolve names in it; required in addition to the object privilege (the "two-lock rule") (Part 12 §12.4).
Role (Postgres)The cluster-global unified concept for users and groups; LOGIN makes it a user; membership + INHERIT gives group privileges; PUBLIC = everyone (Part 12 §12.4).
ALTER DEFAULT PRIVILEGESPre-declares grants for future objects a role creates in a schema; existing objects are unaffected — Postgres has no UC-style downward grant inheritance (Part 12 §12.5).
RLS (Row-Level Security)Per-row visibility/modify filtering via CREATE POLICY on top of table grants; the UC analogue is row filters + column masks (Part 12 §12.4).
Lakebase Provisioned (v1)The legacy, pre-Neon Lakebase: fixed-capacity Postgres (CU_1…CU_8, 16 GB/CU), 24/7 billing, flat Database Instance, no branching or scale-to-zero (Part 13).
Lakebase Autoscaling (v2)The current, Neon-based Lakebase: elastic compute with scale-to-zero, branching/snapshots, nested Project → Branch → Endpoint, usage-based pricing (Part 13).
Database ProjectThe v2 root managed Postgres object; a v1 Database Instance = a Project with a single fixed root branch (Part 13 §13.3).
Database BranchAn isolated environment in a v2 project that shares storage with its parent up to the branch point; the copy-on-write clone unit (Part 13 §13.3).
Database EndpointA compute endpoint on a v2 branch; one read-write endpoint per branch plus any read-only ones; carries the connection host (Part 13 §13.3).
Hybrid instanceThe v1→v2 bridge: an object created/managed via v1 APIs but physically hosted and executed on the v2 platform (Part 13 §13.5).
CU (Capacity Unit)Lakebase compute unit — v1: 2 vCPU/16 GB (CU_1…CU_8); v2: 0.25 vCPU/2 GB (0.5…64 CU, set as a min/max range) (Part 13 §13.1/§13.4).
PostgreSQL driverA client speaking the Postgres wire protocol on :5432 (psql, psycopg, JDBC, pg, pgx…); stateful session with transactions; password = OAuth token (Part 14 §14.2).
Connection poolA set of warm, long-lived Postgres connections shared across requests; in Lakebase must refresh the OAuth token before opening new connections (Part 14 §14.2).
Lakebase Data APIA PostgREST-compatible REST interface — SQL over HTTPS; each table becomes a URL, auth is a Bearer OAuth token, no driver/connection needed (Part 14 §14.3).
PostgRESTThe open REST-over-Postgres spec the Data API is compatible with (auto-generated endpoints, filter/embed syntax, RPC for functions) (Part 14 §14.3).
authenticator roleThe single Postgres role all Data-API HTTP traffic lands on; it assumes the caller's user role so that user's grants + RLS apply (Part 14 §14.3).
databricks_superuserThe bounded high-privilege role a Lakebase instance owner gets instead of real Postgres SUPERUSER (CREATEDB/CREATEROLE/BYPASSRLS + pg_read_all_data/pg_write_all_data + GRANT ALL in public) (Part 15 §15.5).
HA (multi-AZ)High Availability: compute auto-fails-over to a standby in another AZ; carries a 99.99 % monthly uptime SLA vs 99.9 % single-AZ (Part 15 §15.3).
PITRPoint-in-time restore: recover to any LSN within the retention window; mechanically = branching at a past LSN (Part 15 §15.4, Part 8 §4.1).
CMK (Customer-Managed Keys)Autoscaling-only encryption where you control the key wrapping S3/pageserver/safekeeper data; revoking it makes data cryptographically inaccessible to Databricks (Part 15 §15.5).
CU-hour / GB-monthAutoscaling billing meters: compute per CU-hour (scale-to-zero → $0 idle), storage per GB-month (expiring branches bill only deltas) (Part 15 §15.9).
NOPMNew Orders Per Minute — the HammerDB TPROC-C (TPC-C-like) throughput metric used in Lakebase benchmarks (Part 15 §15.8).
GetPage@LSNThe compute→pageserver request for one 8 KB page as of a given LSN; issued only on a shared_buffers + LFC miss (Part 16 §16.2, Part 8 §3).
LFC (Local File Cache)Lakebase's compute-local NVMe cache between shared_buffers and the pageserver; a warm-working-set cache that cuts pageserver round-trips (Part 16 §16.2).
walproposerThe Neon Postgres extension on the compute that streams WAL to the 3 safekeepers and drives the commit quorum (Part 16 §16.3).
GIG / RIGGlobal Ingress Gateway (V1, per-instance DNS, global hop) vs Regional Ingress Gateway / Storage Proxy (V2, regional wildcard DNS, in-region) (Part 16 §16.1).
HCM / LBMHadron Compute Manager (V1 control plane) / Lakebase Manager (V2 control plane) (Part 16 §16.1).
lakebase-proxyThe Neon-based proxy that terminates TLS and authenticates each connection (OAuth JWT or SCRAM) before forwarding to the compute (Part 16 §16.1/§16.4).
DDL forwardingThe neon extension mechanism that syncs SQL-created roles (incl. SCRAM hash) to the control plane at commit, so the proxy can authenticate them (neon.forward_ddl) (Part 16 §16.4).
databricks_gatewaySystem PG role the HTTP Gateway / Data API uses to connect via mTLS; distinct from the authenticator pool role (Part 16 §16.5).
NeonVMThe autoscaling micro-VM that runs Postgres in V2 (lakebase-compute), resized live by the autoscaler-agent (Part 16 §16.1, Part 13).
Logical sizeThe Postgres-visible size of one branch at one LSN — all tables/indexes across all its databases (+ SLRUs/metadata); the metric used for quotas (Part 8 §3.5).
Synthetic sizeNeon's project-wide billing model of storage = base snapshot at the PITR-window start + WAL history, with shared branch data counted once (copy-on-write aware) (Part 8 §3.5).
autoscaler-agentThe host-side DaemonSet (one per node) that reads a VM's CPU/memory/LFC signals, computes the desired CU, and drives resizing — up with scheduler approval, down with vm-monitor approval (Part 4 §7.1).
vm-monitorThe guest-side component (inside compute_ctl) that guards downscale, resizes the LFC on change, and requests immediate upscale on a memory spike over a websocket to the agent (Part 4 §7.1).
Study material generated as a learning aid.
